Stocks Resume Rally After a 1-Day Break; Earnings Data Loom
Monday's Markets
Stocks Resume Rally After a 1-Day Break; Earnings Data Loom
By E.S. Browning
380 words
13 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AFTER SEVEN WEEKS of market gains, stocks began the new week with yet another advance amid
optimism about coming fourth-quarter earnings reports.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, whose many technology stocks slumped in mid-December, surged to another
30-month high, rising 1.19%, or 24.86 points, to 2111.78. The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 26.29
points, or 0.25%, to 10485.18, short of the 21-month high of 10592.44 hit Thursday.
Optimism about earnings overshadowed last week's worries about the weak December employment report,
which knocked stocks down on Friday. Bellwether Intel will release quarterly earnings tomorrow and General
Electric will on Friday.
Stocks began yesterday with a slump, as several analysts warned that the market is overdue for a pullback.
But by day's end, stocks were headed up again.
"It appears that the flow of dollars into stock mutual funds continues to be strong, and investors are
encouraged about the rebound in the economy" and in earnings, said Tim Heekin, director of trading at San
Francisco brokerage firm Thomas Weisel Partners.
The dollar rebounded as comments from European Central Bank President Jean-Claude Trichet were taken
as a hint that the ECB might intervene to keep the euro from rising too high against the dollar. Gold fell
slightly, as did Treasury bonds.One cloud was the continued rise in the price of oil, to $34.72, the highest
finish since March of last year.
The broad S&P 500 index rose 0.48%, or 5.37 points, to 1127.23, just short of the 21-month high it hit last
week.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.46 billion shares traded, 2,063 stocks rose and 1,220 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 2/32, or 62.5 cents for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, rose to 4.087%. The 30-year bond was down 9/32 to yield 4.979%.
The dollar strengthened. It traded at 106.69 yen, up from 106.37 yen, while the euro fell against the dollar to
$1.2747 from $1.2843.

Quarterly Mutual Funds Review --- Funds Shine Despite Legal Woes --- Strength in Economy, Corporate Profits Help Industry Post Double-Digit...
Quarterly Mutual Funds Review --- Funds Shine Despite Legal Woes --- Strength in Economy,
Corporate Profits Help Industry Post Double-Digit Gains; Some Fear a Repeat of the 1990s Bubble
By Ian McDonald The Wall Street Journal Online
1,733 words
8 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IT IS SAFE for mutual-fund investors to open their account statements again.
Amid cascading allegations that many fund firms allowed select investors to profit at other investors' expense
through improper trading, stock funds quietly rode rising markets to stunning gains last year. On average,
diversified U.S. stock funds rocketed up more than 32% in 2003, their best showing since 1991 and their
third-best return since 1970, according to fund tracker Lipper Inc. By comparison, the Standard &Poor's
500-stock index rose more than 28%, including dividends, in 2003.
Foreign stock funds did even better, with the average diversified international portfolio gaining nearly 35%.
And despite a rocky summer, bond funds finished in the black with the average taxable U.S.-bond fund and
average municipal-bond fund up 7.7% and 4.8%, respectively.
The heady gains, spurred by a burst of economic and corporate profit growth, were good news for fund
investors and scandal-plagued fund companies alike after a three-year bear market.
"The economic environment feels and looks entirely different from just six months ago," says Peter L.
Bernstein, a widely followed New York investment researcher and economic consultant. "That explains all
these gains."
But many on Wall Street worry that the 2003 rally, led by the volatile shares of small companies and tech
outfits, was an echo from the mania of the late-1990s bubble. The fear is that the combination of steep
valuations and raised expectations could be poisonous for fund investors, kicking off several years of
below-average returns.
"This feels like a return to froth," says Clifford Asness, an investment researcher and managing principal at
AQR Capital Management LLC, a New York hedge fund. "We have an investing public and an investing
profession that haven't learned and are dying for the fun to begin again. But this is not where bull markets
start -- it's where they go to die."
Some Main Street investors aren't ready to put on their party hats just yet. Tom Farrelly, a 70-year-old retired
International Business Machines Corp. employee in Seattle, for one, has about half his assets in cash after
selling a home.
"I'm mostly in cash now because I'll be darned if I know what to do with it," he says. "I think the stock market
is iffy, and all I can look forward to is rising interest rates, which would hurt bonds."
What fueled 2003's outburst, the first positive performance for the stock market and stock funds since 1999?
A steady stream of rate and tax cuts finally jolted the U.S. economy back to life last year. The third quarter's
8.2% annualized jump in gross-domestic-product growth was its strongest rise in 20 years. The 2003
earnings of companies in the S&P 500, using generally accepted accounting principles, jumped more than
60% from 2002, according to S&P estimates.
While the gains were broad -- every U.S. stock-fund category, except for bear-market funds, rang up
double-digit gains -- the riskiest categories earned the highest returns. Among U.S. offerings, technology
funds led the way, gaining more than 56% on average.
The smaller the companies a fund invested in, the better its returns. All types of small-cap funds gained an
average of more than 42%, while midcap funds climbed more than 35% on average and large-cap funds rose
more than 25%. High-octane growth stocks on the whole narrowly edged their more price-conscious value
peers. Multicap growth funds, for example, rose more than 35% compared with 31% for the average multicap
value fund. Aside from bear funds, the nation's worst-performing U.S. stock-fund category was balanced
funds with a 19% gain.

--- Page 7 ---
Page 7 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Overseas stock funds were hardly wallflowers, either. Latin American funds, emerging-markets funds and
gold funds -- classified as overseas funds because many gold companies are based outside the U.S. -- all
rose more than 55%. The average diversified foreign stock fund gained nearly 35%, compared with 35.3% for
the Morgan Stanley Capital International Europe, Australasia and Far East Index.
And despite a painful selloff over the summer, every bond-fund category managed to finish the year in the
black. Again, riskier fare soared highest, with the average high-yield and world income funds gaining 24%
and 18%, respectively.
Intermediate-term bond funds, the largest bond-fund category measured by assets, rose 4.6% on average.
These sunny returns couldn't have come at a better time for bear-market battered fund investors, and the
same goes for fund companies. If today's scandal had surfaced with stock-fund investors suffering a fourth
consecutive down year, many investors might have seen little reason to hang on to their fund shares.
At least partly due to rising account balances, emboldened investors kept investing in mutual funds. Stock
and bond funds saw investors add a net $202.4 billion to their accounts during the first 11 months of 2003,
according to the Investment Company Institute, the fund industry's largest trade group. That is up 66% from
the same period a year earlier.
Of course, searing returns are hardly a guarantee of continued success. Consider that the nation's average
technology fund, which gained 129% in 1999 and more than 50% in 2003, is still underwater over the past
five years with an average annual loss of 3.6%. It's also worth remembering that even with 2003's strong
gains, the average diversified U.S. stock fund still averages a 2.8% annual loss over the past three years and
just a 2.6%annual gain over the past five years.
Many on Wall Street see middling investment returns to come, mostly thanks to rich stock valuations. At the
end of 2003, the stocks in the S&P 500 traded at 24 times their expected earnings for 2004, following GAAP.
That is well above their historical average price-to-earnings multiple of 15.
High valuations leave little room for more price appreciation, so they typically presage meager gains.
Between 1927 and 2001, for example, when the stocks in the S&P 500 have traded at more than 19 times
their average inflation-adjusted earnings during the previous decade, the index has tended to sputter, trailing
inflation over the next decade, according to data from Mr. Asness, the New York hedge-fund manager and
researcher. Today that figure is about 27, he says.
"I'm pretty good at being wrong about the short term," says Mr. Asness, "but 27 is too high."
There are other signs the valuations have gotten stretched. Researcher Morningstar Inc. uses quantitative
criteria to set fair values for about 500 stocks, giving each a ranking of between one and five stars based on
how undervalued the shares are. Only 36 stocks, or 7% of those covered by Morningstar analysts, were given
the highest undervalued marks of four or five stars at the end of December.
"I certainly think 2003 has to be viewed as an outlier year," says Bill Nygren, manager of the $4.8 billion
Oakmark Fund. "The market is not priced at a level where it could continue to deliver 20% returns for long
before it got to a crazy valuation level."
Given stocks' high price tags, much of an investor's total returns from equity investments these days may
come from dividends rather than price appreciation. But investors have shown little interest. Dividend-paying
stocks in the S&P 500 gained 34% in 2003, compared with 62%for nonpayers.
And despite a tax break on corporate dividends approved in 2003, stock yields remain paltry. At the end of
2003 the S&P 500's yield was just 1.6%, compared with an average of 2.7% over the past 20 years,
according to S&P data.
Mr. Bernstein, Mr. Asness and others believe investors should expect equity returns of between 6% and 8%
on average over the next decade -- a far cry from 2003's advance or the 18% annualized gains the S&P 500
rang up in the 1990s.
To be sure, the short term is impossible to predict, and a case can be made for the strong gains to continue in
2004. Buzzing about the jump-started economy, low interest rates and stabilizing unemployment, Bill Miller,
manager of the $9.3 billion Legg Mason Value Trust, called for strong stock gains in 2004 during a year-end
media briefing, for example.
Mr. Miller is the only mutual-fund manager to post gains topping the S&P 500 in each of the past 13 years.
Even the bearish Mr. Bernstein admits that a healthy economy could make for "a terrific year" in 2004, adding
that the Bush administration and Federal Reserve will be prone to support the economy in an election year.

--- Page 8 ---
Page 8 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In the end, though there is no shortage of Wall Street numerologists and market swamis with guesses on
where stocks and bonds are headed, these predictions are of limited value to the vast majority of fund
investors.
Experts say the most sensible advice for fund investors facing an expensive market, along with the specter of
muted future returns, is the least exciting: Diversify your portfolio, and do all you can to keep your investing
costs low. In addition, it makes sense to trim spending and increase your monthly savings. Boosting your
investments makes it easier to reach your investment goals without having to rely on home-run market
returns.
"Realistically, you're not going to get double-digit returns from anything," says Russ Kinnel, director of fund
research at Morningstar. "So, boost your savings. That will make you feel more secure."
This approach allows investors to appreciate an occasional year of outsize gains, like 2003, without coming to
rely on repeat performances from an already searing stock market. "Could it do it one more year?" asks Mr.
Nygren, regarding the market's performance. "Sure. But there's no reason to expect or to require that."

Dollar Touches Round of Fresh Lows --- Currency Feels Pressures Of Apparent Indifference By U.S. To Its Weakness
Currency Trading
Dollar Touches Round of Fresh Lows --- Currency Feels Pressures Of Apparent Indifference By U.S.
To Its Weakness
By Jamie McGeever Dow Jones Newswires
626 words
7 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Unable to shake off the heavy downward pressure from low U.S. interest rates and the
apparent indifference of U.S. monetary authorities toward its weakness, the dollar sank to new lows.
With investors coming back to the market following winter holidays, the dollar's decline actually accelerated
as liquidity returned to normal levels.
"New money is going to work and the trend is continuing," said a senior currency dealer at a U.S. bank in
New York.
In heavy options-related selling as investors sought to protect themselves against further dollar weakness,
the dollar hit a record low against the euro and multiyear depths against most other major and second-tier
currencies.
Once again, the dollar's biggest pillar of support came from Japanese monetary authorities, which reportedly
intervened aggressively in the market to prevent the dollar from falling below 106 yen for the first time since
September 2000.
Their yen-selling intervention exceeded $5 billion yesterday, dealers said. This comes on top of a similarly
aggressive round of intervention Monday. Some dealers estimate the Ministry of Finance has bought up to
$20 billion in the past week.
In late trading in New York Tuesday, the euro was at $1.2739, up from $1.2657 in New York late Monday but
off the new $1.2810 high hit earlier yesterday. The euro was also up at 135.30 yen from 134.45 yen.
The dollar ended New York trading yesterday at 106.20 yen, down from 106.27 yen. It had slipped as low as
106.01 yen earlier yesterday. The dollar was also at 1.2314 Swiss francs, down from 1.2349 francs late
Monday but again up from the session and seven-year low of 1.2245 francs, while sterling came off its
11-year high of $1.8280 to close at $1.8219. That was still well up from $1.8059 late Monday in New York.
The dollar also forged multiyear bottoms against the Australian, New Zealand and Canadian dollars but
managed to end up off its new lows as some profit-taking kicked in.
Tim Stewart, chief global currency strategist at Morgan Stanley in New York, said there may be an element of
unease creeping into the market about the pace of the dollar's fall, an issue that will likely be addressed at the
Group of Seven meeting of finance ministers and central bankers in Florida next month.
"A bit of profit-taking may be starting to show its head in the euro, which maybe shows some nervousness
ahead of these meetings and sensitivity to the [euro's] high price," Mr. Stewart said. "But I still think you
should hold core short dollar positions. The story's not over yet."
The U.S. economic data calendar was light yesterday, with only the services sector Institute for Supply
Management index for December on tap.
The headline print came in at 58.6, below expectations for 60.5 and November's 60.1. This prompted a surge
in U.S. Treasury prices and fall in bond yields with the two-year yield falling to 1.829% from 1.929% and the
10-year yield to 4.279% from 4.384%.
"Interest rates remain the catalyst," for dollar weakness, said Tim Mazenac, currency strategist at Investors
Bank &Trust in Boston.

--- Page 10 ---
Page 10 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
If low interest rates are the catalyst for the dollar, the low dollar is the catalyst for surging gold prices.
Yesterday, spot gold rose over 2% intraday to hit a 14-year high of $430.50 a troy ounce before easing back
at the New York close.

Telecommunications Brief -- Banque Nationale de Belgique: Private Shareholders Sue State Over Part of Gold-Sale Profit
Telecommunications Brief -- Banque Nationale de Belgique: Private Shareholders Sue State Over Part
of Gold-Sale Profit
173 words
6 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Private shareholders of the Belgian central bank, Banque Nationale de Belgique, stepped up their fight over
billions of euros of disputed bank assets by filing a lawsuit against the state. The Belgian law firm of Mischael
Modrikamen confirmed that it lodged the complaint with the Brussels commercial court Dec. 29 on behalf of
30 shareholders. The shareholders claim that from 1990 to 2002 the bank transferred a sum of Belgian francs
equivalent to 6.45 billion euros ($8.12 billion) in profit from the sale of gold reserves to the state. Because
private investors own 50% of the national bank, they demand their share from these proceeds. The latest
move is part of a larger battle the private shareholders have been waging against the bank and the state for
more than a year. Unlike other central banks, Belgium's is a listed company, traded on Euronext. Half of its
shares are in free float, while the state owns the other half.

Kmart, Endo Pharmaceuticals Push Nasdaq Near 2-Year High
Abreast of the Market
Kmart, Endo Pharmaceuticals Push Nasdaq Near 2-Year High
By Karen Talley Dow Jones Newswires
1,124 words
6 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- The Nasdaq Composite Index hit a nearly two-year high, aided by large-cap Nasdaq stocks
Kmart and Endo Pharmaceuticals, while the Dow Jones Industrial Average topped 10500.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 134.22 points, or 1.29%, to 10544.07 and the Nasdaq Composite
Index gained 40.68, or 2.03%, to 2047.36. The Standard &Poor's 500 Index advanced 13.74, or 1.24%, to
1122.22, taking out its 20-month high of 1111.92.
There was positive news on the economic front, with U.S. construction spending climbing past expectations in
November, elevated by higher outlays on homes, roads and office buildings. Plus, over the weekend, Federal
Reserve Governor Ben Beranke hinted that interest rates will stay low for much of 2004. And a number of
companies came out with positive outlooks.
Investors took to the positive developments. "There is a sense that the corporate economy is picking up
steam," said Henry Dickson, chief investment strategist at Lehman Brothers.
Kmart Holding jumped $6.12, or 27%, to $29.12 on Nasdaq after the discount retailer said the first two
months of its fiscal fourth quarter were profitable.
Endo Pharmaceuticals leapt 4.50, or 24%, to 23.63 on Nasdaq after a federal judge ruled that closely held
Purdue Pharma LP's patents protecting its painkiller Oxycontin are invalid, clearing the way for Endo to sell a
generic version of the drug.
Siebel Systems (Nasdaq) gained 1.40, or 10%, to 15.39 after the e-business-software provider raised its

Greenspan Sees Recovery as Vindication for Fed --- Policy of Managing Risks Gets Credit for Success; Fresh Inflation Fears Lurk
The Economy
Greenspan Sees Recovery as Vindication for Fed --- Policy of Managing Risks Gets Credit for
Success; Fresh Inflation Fears Lurk
By Greg Ip
883 words
5 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SAN DIEGO -- Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan took a victory lap this weekend for steering the
U.S. economy through its recent troubles.
But even as his Fed colleagues signaled continued willingness to keep rates low for a long time, they faced
the question of how they will avoid the mistake of unleashing inflation again.
Mr. Greenspan said Saturday that during his 16 years running the U.S. central bank, he and his colleagues
have had no road map for dealing with their biggest challenges, such as the 1987 stock-market crash, the
early 1990s credit crunch and the 1998 debt-markets crisis. Their greatest challenge was dealing with the
recent stock-market bubble. As the aftershocks threatened to tip the U.S. into a deflationary downward spiral
last year, Mr. Greenspan was criticized for failing to prick the bubble in the late 1990s.
The economy is rebounding, and Mr. Greenspan is increasingly confident the Fed's course has been
vindicated. "There appears to be enough evidence, at least tentatively, to conclude that our strategy of
addressing the bubble's consequences rather than the bubble itself has been successful," he said during a
panel discussion here organized by the American Economic Association. Fed governor Ben Bernanke added
yesterday that it looks like "2003 will be remembered as the year when this recovery turned the corner."
Critics argue that imbalances from the 1990s such as high consumer-debt levels and rich stock prices persist
and could threaten the economy, but the Fed chairman dismissed such concerns. Historically, such
imbalances aren't fully fixed until years after a recession ends, he said, and "it will be a stretch" to blame the
next recession on the imbalances of "many years earlier."
He added that another asset bubble seems unlikely for some time because it usually takes so long for "the
trauma of the collapse to wear off."
Mr. Greenspan attributes the Fed's recent success to setting monetary policy according to "risk
management" rather than rules. That means the Fed, instead of fixing interest rates according to a single
forecast of the economy and inflation, also considers remote possibilities, such as a deflationary bust, if they
have especially dire consequences.
Inherent in this approach is the risk of letting the inflation genie out of the bottle. The Fed cut its short-term
interest-rate target to 1% last year, and Mr. Bernanke said "this policy remains appropriate." The Fed has
said it will keep rates low for a considerable period. That policy is explicitly accepting some risk of letting
inflation rise again, to vanquish the more serious, though smaller, risk of deflation.
Mr. Greenspan acknowledged that if the Fed was concerned only with the likeliest scenario, then its policy
has indeed been "too loose."
But Bank of England governor Mervyn King warned Mr. Greenspan's approach risks perils of its own. Citing
academic research, he said the Fed used a risk-management approach when it kept rates too low in the early
1970s, ultimately fueling skyrocketing inflation and interest rates later that decade. The most popular
economic model at the time suggested the Fed should have tightened policy, but it was worried about the
serious consequences if alternative scenarios proved correct. The dilemma it faced then, Mr. King warned, "is
not dissimilar to the current situation."
A small minority of Fed officials is fretting over scattered inflation signals -- such as the behavior of
inflation-protected Treasury bonds -- though not enough to press for higher interest rates. The statement by
the Fed's 19-member policy committee last month that inflation risks are "almost" balanced rather than tilted
toward deflation, was in part an acknowledgment of those concerns.

--- Page 15 ---
Page 15 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Bernanke, who doesn't think inflation is coming back, nonetheless said Sunday that the views of those
who think it is must be "taken seriously." At another panel discussion, he looked at rising raw-material prices,
commodities, gold and the weakened dollar, and he concluded they "almost certainly" don't imply consumer
prices are about to surge. Raw-material prices usually rise early in an expansion and account for a small
portion of total costs, he said. Gold's rise represents in part "safe haven" fears related to geopolitical
tensions, and foreign producers will accept lower profit margins rather than pass the full impact of the dollar's
drop to U.S. customers. For now, he said, the Fed "has the luxury of being patient."
The message is that for now, the Fed will continue to consider the risk of deflation in its decisions. This was
reinforced Saturday by Vincent Reinhart, the head of monetary affairs at the Fed, in reviewing the alternatives
Mr. Bernanke developed last year for stimulating the economy even when the federal-funds rate is zero, such
as holding the rate there for a long time or buying bonds to push down long-term interest rates.
"There's much less talk of deflation in recent months," Mr. Reinhart said. Yet the Fed must still think about it
because with low inflation now prevailing, it is easier to "conceive of events" that push the U.S. back into the
danger zone.

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2003 --- Americas: Global Stock Markets Regain Their Luster --- Latin American Stocks Get Boost From...
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2003 --- Americas: Global Stock Markets Regain Their Luster ---
Latin American Stocks Get Boost From World-Wide Recovery
By David Luhnow
1,401 words
2 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
R6
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Mexico City -- IT WAS THE BEST YEAR for Latin American stocks since 1991. And it was about time. During
the previous three years, the region's markets had suffered a bloodletting that made the decline on the
Nasdaq Stock Market seem mild. In 2002 alone, Brazil's stock market sank 46% in dollar terms.
The region, as well as Canada, got a boost from conditions that helped other stocks around the globe,
namely low interest rates and the beginnings of a global economic recovery.
Shares in South America were poised to do well after 2002, a year in which Argentina's economy sank due to
the biggest debt default in history, Brazil's currency wobbled on concerns it would follow that of its neighbor,
and Venezuela endured a crippling oil strike and abortive coups.
"It was both the cyclical conditions that favored emerging markets, as well as the fact Latin America had such
an awful 2002 that it was ready to bounce back," said Geoffrey Dennis, Latin America equities strategist at
Citigroup Inc.'s Salomon Smith Barney in New York.
In 2003, Argentina's economy rebounded, while Brazil's new leftist President Luiz Inacio Lula da Silva proved
he could ably manage the region's biggest economy. Higher commodity prices and China's growing demand
for resources helped Chile and Brazil, as well as the northern U.S. neighbor, Canada.
If South America was on the right side of China, Mexico was on the wrong side. China replaced Mexico as
the No. 2 exporter to the U.S. market, after Canada, and weak U.S. industrial output for most of last year hurt
Mexico's export-fueled economy. Even though Mexican stocks went up nearly 34% in dollar terms last year, it
was among the worst-performing major emerging markets.
For the year ahead, analysts say, investors who believe the global recovery will gather steam should bet
again on cyclical economies such as Brazil and Chile. Another good sign: Brazil's market is still well below its
1997 peak in dollar terms. Morgan Stanley, for one, expects Latin American stocks to rise about 20% in dollar
terms on average this year. The danger would be if U.S. interest rates begin to rise fast, sucking away
liquidity.
Here's a look at some individual markets:
Brazil
Brazil enjoyed what could be a "perfect storm" in reverse -- a confluence of positive factors that helped share
prices post some of the biggest gains in any country. Its leading stock index, the Bovespa, rose 142% last
year in dollar terms.
A strengthening currency helped add to gains in dollar terms, which along with lower inflation and interest
rates helped many companies skirt bankruptcy. Another plus was the performance of President da Silva, a
former labor leader who took office early last year and within months made a convert of Wall Street, keeping
a tight lid on public finances and pushing through market-friendly economic programs such as pension
overhaul that even his conservative predecessors had been unable to pass.
China's demand for commodities played to the strength of Brazil's resource-rich economy. Steel giants,
including Cia. Siderurgica Nacional, Usiminas and Cia. Siderurgica de Tubarao, all rose more than 200% for
the year. The American depositary shares of Siderurgica Nacional, for instance, rose 274% to close at $53.60
on the New York Stock Exchange.
Still, Brazil's overall market isn't expensive, compared with its past and with its peers' markets, said Mario
Epelbaum, chief of Latin America equity strategy at Morgan Stanley. That could fuel gains of 20% to 30% this
year, he said.

--- Page 17 ---
Page 17 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Canada
A rebound in technology stocks and surging commodity prices helped Canadian stocks produce healthy gains
in 2003, snapping a two-year losing streak. The S&P/TSX Composite Index rallied 24%.
Shares of telecommunications-equipment giant Nortel Networks Corp. more than doubled, to close at C$5.49
(US$4.23) as the company's heavy losses turned to modest profit. Bank stocks rallied sharply amid strong
earnings, tame loan losses and an upswing in investment-banking activity. Shares of Canadian Imperial Bank
of Commerce jumped 47% to C$64 in 2003, while Bank of Montreal shares climbed 28% to C$53.50.
Shares of companies producing everything from gold to natural gas also posted solid gains. The weak U.S.
dollar helped propel gold more than 19% to US$416.10 an ounce, sending the TSX gold-stock index up
nearly 14% over the year. With prices for base metals such as copper and nickel surging, the TSX metals and
mining index jumped 76% on the year. Shares of nickel miner Inco Ltd. rose 55% to C$51.65. Strong energy
prices led to a nearly 24% rise in the TSX energy index.
Many expect more modest market gains this year, since valuations have climbed. "A year ago, I'd look at
three stocks and buy one. Now I look at 10 and buy one," says Irwin Michael, who runs ABC Funds in
Toronto. He sees the Canadian market tacking on another 10% to 15% in 2004, and he has stuffed the fund
with forest-products stocks. Still, "the wild card is the Canadian dollar," since it could hurt exporters' bottom
lines if it continues to surge against the U.S. dollar, he says.
Mexico
The bellwether market indicator, the Indice de Precios &Cotizaciones, climbed nearly 34% in dollar terms.
It was a big year for mobile-phone giant America Movil SA, which has turned itself into the crown jewel of
billionaire Carlos Slim's vast business empire. America Movil's ADSs, trading on the New York Stock
Exchange, climbed 90% after beginning the year at $14.36. While most global telecom companies were
retrenching, the concern, the largest mobile-phone company in the region, went on an expansion drive,
snapping up low-price operations everywhere from Brazil to Nicaragua.
Meanwhile, many investors fear the country is drifting in the face of intense competition from China. For the
third year since President Vicente Fox took power, politicians were unable to reach agreement on any
meaningful economic changes to boost competitiveness. "Another year, another lost opportunity," concluded
Alonso Cervera, chief economist for Mexico at Credit Suisse First Boston.
The short-term outlook for Mexico may not be as gloomy as it seems, however. While China has stolen some
share of the U.S. import market from Mexico, the country still should get a good bounce from a U.S. industrial
recovery. Traditionally, economic growth in Mexico has been tightly linked to U.S. industrial output, and as a
recent Morgan Stanley report concluded, "the link is alive," despite China.
"Mexico's low interest rates and stability will lead down the road to significant credit growth and will have a
major effect on the consumer economy," said Fernando Donayre, portfolio manager at Zephyr Funds.
Argentina
Argentine stocks got a lift from an economic recovery following the country's disastrous sovereign-debt
default in 2002. The leading Merval Index soared 134% in dollar terms -- among the world's top performers.
Much of the turnaround, however, was simply a case of radically different expectations for companies
investors had believed to be on the verge of bankruptcy a year earlier. Telecom Argentina, for instance, saw
its ADS price rise to $8.75 at the end of the year from $2.25 at the start.
Chile
Latin America's third-biggest market did better than expected last year. Chilean stocks never went down as
much as its neighbors', so their recovery wasn't as spectacular. But Chile's IGPA index nonetheless posted a
78% gain in dollar terms, burning players who shunned the country as too boring.
Airline LanChile's ADS took flight and climbed 12% on the Big Board during the year. Meanwhile, the ADS of
retailer Distribucion &Servicio climbed to $20.66 from $10 at the start of the year.
---
Mark Heinzl in Toronto contributed to this article.

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2003 --- Commodities Markets Surge Again --- As World-Wide Demand Grows, Gold Crosses $400, While...
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2003 --- Commodities Markets Surge Again --- As World-Wide
Demand Grows, Gold Crosses $400, While Natural Gas Soars
By Peter A. McKay
2,456 words
2 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
R3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
MAJOR COMMODITY markets rose for a second straight year in 2003, as recovering economies world-wide
required ever greater amounts of raw materials to churn out finished goods for consumers. The U.S. dollar's
plunge against major foreign currencies made raw materials priced in dollars inexpensive in overseas
markets, encouraging U.S. exports. China was a voracious buyer of several U.S. agricultural products.
Among the winners: Soybean prices soared more than 40%, to near multiyear highs. Gold crossed the
threshold of $400 (317.92 euros) per troy ounce for the first time in more than seven years. Natural gas
surged ahead in the fourth quarter.
As 2004 begins, prices for most commodities seem poised to continue higher, albeit at a slower pace. The
Dow Jones-AIG Index of 20 commodities finished last year at 134.672, up 22%, compared with a 24% gain in
2002.
Most foreign-exchange traders expect the dollar to stay weak prolonging its bullish effect on commodities.
The continuing growth of the American economy also should boost commodity prices for the foreseeable
future, said James Glassman, senior U.S. economist for J.P. Morgan Chase &Co. "Industrial activity all
around the world is taking off," Mr. Glassman said. "Personally, I'm cheered by it. I don't really see inflation
as a worry -- yet."
The possibility that the U.S. Federal Reserve may raise interest rates later this year isn't a major concern
right now, he said. A more immediate risk is that some European central bank will jump the gun, tightening a
major commodity-consuming country's money supply and cutting off a major source of cash to buy raw
materials.
Another threat to commodity prices is the relative strength of the U.S. stock market. Investment demand
helped drive some resources' prices higher in 2003 -- especially gold -- but investors this year might instead
decide to chase the gains in the newly robust stock market.
The new year also is likely to see continued change in the structure and governance of commodities markets.
The German-Swiss online bourse Eurex plans to launch a U.S. financial-futures market in February, for
example, although it still needs regulatory approval and has run into fierce opposition from traditional Chicago
markets.
The Commodity Futures Trading Commission is reviewing U.S. futures exchanges' self-regulatory activities
and governance, a probe that has attracted more interest among industry insiders since executive
compensation and governance became an issue at the New York Stock Exchange. The commission's final
report, expected sometime early in 2004, may order significant changes in exchanges' business practices.
The New York Board of Trade relocated into the headquarters of the New York Mercantile Exchange in 2003,
fueling rumors of a merger between the two former rivals. The former headquarters of NYBOT were
destroyed in the Sept. 11, 2001, terrorist attacks and traders worked for more than a year out of a cramped
backup facility in Queens.
Here's a rundown of the major markets in 2003:
Energy
The diplomatic standoff with Iraq drove energy prices higher in the first few months of the year, but markets
moved little once all-out war erupted in mid-March. That relatively subdued reaction was in sharp contrast to
the first Gulf War in 1991, when crude prices soared over $40 a barrel on the first day of fighting.

--- Page 20 ---
Page 20 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Crude oil, which began the year around $31 a barrel, fell to lows around $25 by late April, after U.S. forces
succeeded in deposing Saddam Hussein's regime.
Prices gyrated through the rest of the year but generally trended upward, amid rising U.S. demand, falling
stockpiles and tight supply control by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries. Crude-oil futures
ended the year at $32.52 a barrel, up $1.32 or 4.2% at the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Along with the rise in crude, gasoline futures gained 6.32 cents, or 7.3%, to 93.29 cents a gallon. Heating-oil
futures rose 4.6 cents, or 5.3%, to 91.46 cents a gallon at Nymex.
OPEC's statements lately seem to point toward a continuation of high prices. The cartel's incoming president,
Indonesian Oil Minister Purnomo Yusgiantoro, has said he is concerned about high oil prices, but most
officials within the organization have said the problem is driven by excess demand and is unlikely to reach the
point where it will spark release of additional barrels.
Meanwhile, U.S. inventories are running low, according to the Energy Information Administration, the
Department of Energy's statistics arm. Through mid-December, EIA reported stockpiles of crude were at
272.8 million barrels, more than 9% below the five-year average and close to the 270 million-barrel level that
is often considered a sign of unusually low inventories. "I don't want to make it sound like it's a cliff you fall off,
but it certainly is an indicator of tightness," said EIA Administrator Guy Caruso.
John Conolly, head of the futures division of Terra Nova Trading, a Chicago brokerage, said high demand is
likely to continue at least through early 2004, due to increasing U.S. economic strength. "In the near time, I
don't see the crude market going crazy, but there will be pressure to the upside," Mr. Conolly said.
Nymex natural-gas futures were up sharply in 2003, closing at $6.189 per million British thermal units, up
29%, or $1.40. Much of the gain, however, came between Thanksgiving and early December, when there
was an early cold snap and snowstorms in the Northeast. Prices weakened in the last two weeks of trading --
a trend analysts expect to continue into the new year, since gas inventories are about 8.1% above year-ago
levels, according to the American Gas Association.
Metals
Gold, which has soared ever since the September 2001 terrorist attacks, continued to rise in 2003,
confirming its traditional role as an investor safety net during times of uncertainty. Other precious metals
followed, while copper led industrial metals higher, strengthening along with the U.S. economy.
Gold futures jumped $67.90, or more than 19%, to $416.10 an ounce on the Comex division of the New
York Mercantile Exchange. But opinion is split on how much longer average investors' interest can last. For
more than 20 years, gold was generally out of favor on Wall Street as the stock market rose. During the
dot-com boom of the late 1990s, it hit lows around $250.
Jeffrey Christian, managing director of the New York-based research firm CPM Group, doesn't expect a dip
back to those levels, but he notes that spikes in gold investment demand usually last only a year or two. "I
won't necessarily say it's going to end tomorrow, but there's no guarantee it's going to run too much further,
either," Mr. Christian said.
Gold's gains already appear to be slowing somewhat, in percentage terms. Last year's more than 19% gain
compared with a 25% gain in 2002, although the absolute dollar value of the move was similar.
But there are still true believers in the investment community. Frank Holmes, chairman and chief executive
officer of U.S. Global Investors, which manages several gold-oriented mutual funds, said Wall Street analysts
are out of touch with average investors' lingering discomfort with "paper assets," spurred by the
Internet-company crash, terrorism, war and the weak currency. He believes investors will continue to flock to
gold as a tangible store of value -- a common argument put forward by "gold bugs" -- and gold could gain
another 17% or so in 2004, to around $500.
"Wall Street has been calling for the gold rally to end for the last two years now -- ever since it began, really,"
said Mr. Holmes. "But it just keeps going."
Some of the market's big players have quietly jumped on the gold bandwagon along with everyday investors.
One indicator of that, said veteran gold trader George Gero, is that the number of gold futures and options
outstanding recently topped one million contracts for the first time at Comex.
"That means the hedge funds [lightly regulated investment pools designed for wealthy individuals and
institutions] are getting involved in a big way in this market," said Mr. Gero, senior vice president at Legg
Mason Wood Walker Inc. "That's . . . a really bullish sign for the metal."

--- Page 21 ---
Page 21 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Other metals also did well in 2003, and most have a bright outlook for the new year, especially if the U.S.
economy remains robust. Silver futures rose $1.153, or 24%, to $5.965 an ounce at Comex. Platinum, which
continues to be in short supply, jumped $210.90, or more than 35%, to $805.30 an ounce on Nymex.
But palladium, a key component in automobiles' catalytic converters, dropped 17%, or $40.50, to $197.50 an
ounce, as platinum became a substitute in the emissions-reducing car parts.
Copper leapt 34.3 cents, or 49%, to $1.0455 a pound at Comex, close to a six-year high, with the most
dramatic gains coming after the U.S. government reported a jump in economic growth in the third quarter.
Other base metals surged in trading at the London Metal Exchange. Aluminum rose $244.75, or 18%, to
$1,588.75 a metric ton. Zinc jumped $254.75, or 34%, to $1,003.75 a metric ton.
Agriculture
Grains and other crops were helped by strong demand for U.S. exports, while the discovery of mad-cow
disease in the U.S. weighed on beef prices.
Soybean futures soared $2.29, or more than 40%, to $7.94 a bushel at the Chicago Board of Trade as China
made several large purchases throughout the year. "Right now, China is the 8,000-pound gorilla in the
soybean market," said analyst Victor D. Lespinasse of A.G. Edwards &Sons.
The U.S. Department of Agriculture recently said it expects China to import a record 808 million bushels of
soybeans over the next year, up 55 million bushels from its October estimate and more than double the
amount China imported from all sources during the past year. If the forecast is accurate, China will import
more than half of the soybeans it uses.
(MORE)
But China is a net exporter of corn and doesn't consume much wheat. Hence, those items have not rallied as
strongly as soybeans. At CBOT, wheat rose 52 cents, or 16%, to $3.77 a bushel. Corn was up 10.25 cents, or
4%, at $2.46 a bushel.
China also was a major player in the cotton market, which saw futures rise 47%, or 23.91 cents, to 75.07
cents a pound last year at the New York Cotton Exchange division of the New York Board of Trade. And, in a
sign that the rally is likely to continue, the USDA forecasts that China's domestic cotton crop will fall by about
14% this year, raising demand for U.S. imports.
Livestock markets face a murkier outlook this year. Pork prices fell in 2003, amid a glut of animals being
delivered to slaughterhouses late in the year. At the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, hog futures rose 1.825
cents, or 3.5%, to 53.425 cents a pound. Frozen pork bellies, the parts of hogs from which bacon and other
products are made, gained 0.275 cent, or 0.3%, to 86.475 cents a pound.
Beef prices were much stronger throughout the year, in part due to the popularity in the U.S. of the Atkins
diet, which advocates heavy protein consumption. But the discovery last month of a cow in Washington state
with mad-cow disease hurt prices in the last week of trading. Taiwan, Japan, Mexico, Chile and other
countries immediately slapped bans on the import of U.S. beef after the discovery. The U.S. exports about
10% of its annual production.
Live-cattle futures were down 8%, or 6.1 cents, to 73.525 cents a pound at CME.
"Everything in the meat sector will change in some way from this point forward," said Timothy Ramey of D.A.
Davidson &Co.
Shares of food companies and hamburger chains also have come under pressure lately, with some analysts
worried that a mad-cow scare might take several years to subside in the U.S., as it did in Europe when the
brain-wasting disease was discovered a few years ago.
Tropical Commodities
Coffee was the only one of the major tropical "soft" commodities to post gains in 2003, a situation analysts
expect to continue in the new year.
Coffee futures rose 4.75 cents, or 7.9%, to 64.95 cents a pound on the Coffee, Sugar, and Cocoa Exchange
division of NYBOT. Stockpiles dipped, and are likely to remain tight this year because Brazil's coffee crop is
unlikely to increase as much as previously expected due to poor growing conditions, said Judith
Ganes-Chase, an independent commodity consultant.

--- Page 22 ---
Page 22 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
She said she sees little hope for frozen orange-juice prices, which have been hurt by the emphasis on low
carbohydrates in the Atkins diet. Also, Florida's orange crop is likely to grow about 25% this year, which could
mean a glut of juice. Futures fell 29.15 cents, or 31%, to 64.1 cents a pound at NYBOT.
Sugar dropped 1.94 cents, or more than 25%, to 5.67 cents a pound, depressed by an 11th consecutive year
of world-wide production surpluses. "There's just a mountain of sugar out there that's not going to disappear
anytime soon," Ms. Chase said.
In the cocoa market, prices were strong early in 2003, rising near to 17-year highs because of political unrest
in the Ivory Coast, the world's leading cocoa producer. But the West African nation's politics have since
stabilized, and by year's end, cocoa futures plummeted 25%, or $506, to $1,515 a metric ton.
This year, analysts say the Ivory Coast seems headed toward a cocoa harvest of at least one million metric
tons, about 25% higher than originally expected and a sign that prices are likely to remain low. "This has been
a crazy time in this market," analyst Boyd Cruel, of Alaron Trading, said. "We've gotten a little more clarity as
the year has progressed, but it's not necessarily the news that producers would have wanted."

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2003 --- Another Saga of the Running of the Bulls --- Fabulously Low Interest Rates Help to End Markets...
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2003 --- Another Saga of the Running of the Bulls ---
Fabulously Low Interest Rates Help to End Markets' Decline; Investors Wonder About a Peak
By E.S. Browning
2,073 words
2 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. STOCKS HAVE JUST completed their first up-year since 1999, and already, investors are hoping for
more.
What a change that is from the mood 15 months ago, when the fear was that stocks were headed down for
the foreseeable future.
What ended the bleeding in the stock market was a U.S. economic recovery touched off by some of the
lowest interest rates in 45 years. At first, it was just a small group of daring investors who foresaw the
rebound and bet on it. As evidence slowly mounted this year that the economy had hit bottom and turned up,
more investors began to pile in.
Despite a slump early in the year, touched off by worries over the war in Iraq, the Dow Jones Industrial
Average finished 2003 with a gain of 2,112.29 points, or 25.3%, at 10453.92. That compares with a gain of
25.2% in 1999, the last winning year for any major index.
From one perspective, the return to the 10000 level was small consolation. The Dow, after all, first crossed
10000 early in 1999, meaning that even after its huge rebound of the past 15 months, there has been little
progress in well over four years. For many investors, however, the recovery from the five-year low hit in
October 2002 was such a relief that the lack of progress over the longer term didn't matter.
The Dow now is up 43.5% since its low of 7286.27, on Oct. 9, 2002, and the more volatile Nasdaq Composite
Index has risen 80% since its low of 1114.11 that same day. To some, the near despair of the bear market
has begun to seem almost like a bad dream.
The recovery of the past 15 months brought the Dow industrials more than half the way back to the record
high of 11722.98 hit four years ago, in January 2000. (The Nasdaq composite, however, remains far from its
record of 5048.62, hit in March 2000. It finished 2003 at 2003.37, up 50% on the year.)
Rightly or wrongly, the main question on most people's minds today is no longer how far stocks will fall, but
how high they can rise. They are asking: Is this bull market a cyclical thing that will run out of steam some
time this year, sending the Dow back below 10000? Or is it a longer-lasting, "secular" affair that will push the
blue chips up to a new record in the next year or two?
The prevailing view is rosy.
"The combination of low interest rates, tax cuts, a weaker dollar and lean inventories has restarted the U.S.
economic engine. Momentum is now building, which should carry into 2004. While the outlook might appear
too good to be true, expect solid economic growth to be accompanied by gradually rising employment, low
inflation and higher corporate profits," Lynn Reaser, chief economist for Banc of America Capital
Management, wrote in a preview of the new year.
Many money managers have shifted funds toward stocks, in hopes that, even if the gains are slower than in
the old year, they will be robust.
"All this stimulus will generate stronger economic growth next year. That means higher stock prices," said
David Kotok, president of money-management firm Cumberland Advisors in Vineland, New Jersey, in a report
to clients. He compared the outlook for stocks during the current war on terrorism to the market's
performance during World War II, a time when stocks rose.
"Stocks are headed higher," Mr. Kotok advised his clients. "We believe that stock portfolios should remain
fully invested in a broadly diversified way."

--- Page 24 ---
Page 24 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Less than a year ago, with the U.S. fighting in Iraq, investors wrung their hands over stocks' ability to post
lasting gains in this unsettled world. In March stocks hit their lows for the year. Since then, if people haven't
shrugged off those worries entirely, they have at least suppressed them.
Few analysts think stocks can rise 20% again this year, but a large number of them are forecasting some kind
of gain. That marks a sharp contrast to the feeling one year ago, when a fourth consecutive year of stock
declines seemed a real threat.
To some forecasters, this building optimism is a negative sign. The concern is that stocks tend to rise most
strongly at the start of an economic recovery, not when the recovery is already well under way.
The worry-word in the front of many people's minds today is "sustainability." The question is whether the
robust recovery, which saw the U.S. economy rebound 8.2% at an annual rate in the third quarter, can be
sustained. The fear is that, as economic growth and profit gains inevitably slow, investors will be disappointed
and begin pulling back from stocks, capping the rally.
Adding to these worries, the broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock Index is trading at 27 times its companies'
earnings for the past 12 months, well above the historic average of 16. Analysts like to say that stocks are
"priced for perfection," meaning that such high price-earnings ratios can be justified only if the economy,
corporate performance and world stability all remain strong. If chinks start to appear, stocks could suffer.
Some analysts, including investment strategist Tobias Levkovich at Citigroup's Smith Barney brokerage arm
in New York, worry that people have become so optimistic that problems are almost inevitable.
If the U.S. economy continues to be robust, he noted in a report last month, then interest rates should move
higher, diluting the stock market's main fuel. On the other hand, if the economy falters, earnings could suffer,
also hurting stocks. And if the economy pursues a middle course, growing modestly, then economists could
start worrying again about deflation, or falling prices.
"Overall, a frustrating year for investors is quite plausible, despite perhaps an initially good beginning," Mr.
Levkovich warned.
At the same time, signs of difficulty were cropping up in other financial markets.
Reflecting worries about possible future inflation and a weaker dollar, gold prices soared past $400 (318
euros) in 2003, from less than $300 at the start of 2002 and less than $350 at the start of last year.
Oil prices, which rose above $30 a barrel last year as the Iraq war loomed, finished the year above $30
despite the war's quick completion, held up by a recovering economy and by continued Middle East tensions.
Commodity prices surged.
But overall, consumer prices remained remarkably stable. And that was great news for interest rates.
Because of low inflation and a desire to ensure a strong economic recovery, the U.S. Federal Reserve kept
its target short-term rate at just 1%, a 45-year low.
That low benchmark rate, together with residual worries about the U.S. economy and world tensions, kept the
10-year Treasury note remarkably little changed despite the economic recovery. Although its yield fluctuated
sharply during and after the Iraq fighting, the 10-year yield finished at only 4.25%, not that far from the 3.8%
at which it started the year.
The year's real success story, however was the stock market. Its gains were the sort of thing some investors
had begun to despair of ever seeing again.
The broad S&P 500 rose 26.4% on the year. The Wilshire 5000, an index of about 5,200 stocks including
almost every publicly traded company based in the U.S., gained 29%. The Wilshire's market value, a
measure of the total value of U.S. stocks, rose $4.2 trillion, to $13 trillion, from the low it hit on Oct. 9, 2002, to
the end of 2003. That compares with $17 trillion at the record high on March 24, 2000.
One of the oddest things about the recent rally has been the feeling of deja vu. By far the biggest gainers
have been the stocks that were the most discredited during the bear market -- technology stocks, notably
those related to the Internet.
Amazon.com, which lost more than 90% of its value during the bear market, more than doubled in value last
year. Rambus, which makes technology for computer chips, nearly quadrupled in 2003. China.com, a
Chinese Internet company whose stock trades in the U.S., was up more than 180%, despite a sharp pullback
in the latter part of the year.

--- Page 25 ---
Page 25 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
One of the biggest questions facing professional investors, aside from the market's overall direction, is
whether these volatile companies can continue to lead the gains. It is an issue the pros call "rotation," and the
question is whether market leadership is in the process of "rotating" to a new group.
Among the weaker stocks for most of the past year were those that are the most stable and benefit the least
from an economic upturn. Those include makers of drugs and consumer staples like food and diapers, whose
sales remain stable and predictable regardless of the economy's swings. At year's end, the volatile tech
stocks hit headwinds, while the laggard "quality" stocks -- so-called because their earnings are so predictable
-- began to catch up.
Some analysts said this was nothing but year-end profit-taking in the stocks that had risen the most. They
forecast that tech stocks would take off again in the new year. But others pointed out that, when a lasting new
bull market begins, the leaders rarely are the same as those that led the previous bull market. And with baby
boomers facing more health needs, many stock-pickers have been saying for years that health stocks are
overdue to show some lasting strength.
"We continue to like technology, media and selected industrial companies," said Mark Jordahl, chief
investment officer at U.S. Bancorp Asset Management in Minneapolis. "Having said that, we believe a key
strategy to get right in 2004 is a rotation out of these more volatile sectors into higher quality, larger
companies with the capacity to grow earnings. These may also be higher-dividend-paying stocks."
He is preparing to shift to the stable group from the volatile one once he sees the pace of earnings growth
begin to slow.
That kind of shift to "defensive," stable stocks can be a sign that the uptrend is getting long in the tooth, says
Phil Roth, chief technical market analyst at New York brokerage firm Miller Tabak. Some market strategists,
in fact, are warning clients that the bull market could end in the coming months.
(MORE)
"We are not forecasting a long-term bull market," John Caldwell, chief investment strategist at McDonald
Financial Group, a brokerage and money-management arm of Cleveland's KeyCorp, wrote in a report to
clients.
"Investors did not learn a lesson over 2000-2002. We continue to see an unhealthy addiction to technology
stocks, as well as a revival of the margin debt problem." Stock gains could be lower than earnings gains this
year, he said.
But the great majority of forecasters are reassuring clients that stocks should continue to rise now.
"Our models and analysis suggest that it will be very difficult for the stock market in 2004 to `score' the same
type of returns that it did in 2003," wrote Brian Belski, fundamental market strategist at brokerage firm U.S.
Bancorp Piper Jaffray in Minneapolis, in a recent report to clients. Still, he forecasts that the S&P 500 will be
up as much as 14% over the next year to 18 months, rising to the 1200-1250 range from the 1111.92 at
which it finished 2003.
The Conference Board, a business-supported research group, last month revised its economic forecast
sharply upward, projecting that the U.S. economy will grow 5.7% this year, after controlling for inflation, the
strongest year in two decades.
And some investors are reminding themselves that, as was the case last year, stocks have a way of
surprising people -- both on the way up and on the way down.

2204" -- WSJE Jan. 2, 2004)

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2003 --- War &Recovery
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2003 --- War &Recovery
By Brian Hershberg, Phil Izzo, John C. Long and Kathleen H. Sison
13,113 words
2 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
R8
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE YEAR THAT WAS began and ended enveloped by Iraq. In between, as a world-wide economic recovery
gained steam, 2003 saw another space-shuttle explosion, the global scourge of SARS and a massive
blackout.
The stock market rose after three consecutive years of decline, with the industrials surpassing the 10000
mark again. The Big Board was shaken by a pay scandal and corporate boardrooms saw a shakeout, as a
wave of executives faced justice for past excesses. And mutual funds, once seemingly immune from
impropriety, became the latest Wall Street institution to feel scandal's taint. The U.S. dollar was pummeled in
currency markets.
Iraq was liberated from Hussein, and the deposed leader was pulled from a hole. But the terror war remained
far from resolved, as attacks and chatter continue. And peace eluded the Israelis and the Palestinians.
Here is a summary of the news events as chronicled on the front page of The Wall Street Journal. In most
cases, the dates reflect when the items were published.
JANUARY
3: J.P. Morgan says it will take a $1.3 billion charge for the fourth quarter, largely to settle litigation over its
involvement with Enron.
Blue chips surge 265.89 points, or 3.19%, the third-biggest opening-day percentage gain ever. Two trading
days later, after a small pullback, the industrials surge 2% to 8773.57, capping a record three-day start to a
new year.
6: International Steel offers to buy most of Bethlehem Steel's assets for about $1 billion, creating the largest
U.S. steel company.
7: Republicans pick New York City as the site for their national convention in August 2004.
8: Bush proposes a $670 billion package of tax cuts and some additional domestic spending in an effort to
stimulate the economy. The centerpiece of the plan is a sweeping tax break on stock dividends.
10: North Korea pulls out of the nuclear-nonproliferation treaty, magnifying a crisis over its
nuclear-development program and heightening a standoff with Washington.
Sony Music's Thomas Mottola is stepping down as chairman and CEO to start his own label, amid an
industrywide sales drop.
13: Steve Case resigns as chairman of AOL Time Warner following months of pressure from major
shareholders and directors. Days later, AOL's board names CEO Richard Parsons as chairman, solidifying
his power over the company.
14: CNN's Walter Isaacson quits as chairman and CEO of the cable news network to head the Aspen
Institute, a think tank.
16: The Supreme Court upholds Congress's 20-year extension of copyright protection. Disney and other
entertainment companies applaud the decision, as they feared that their creations could have lapsed into the
public domain. The law protects works during an author's lifetime, plus 70 years.
Bush ventures onto the political tightrope of affirmative action, announcing his administration is asking the
Supreme Court to overturn University of Michigan admissions policies. The president says that while he
supports diversity, he believes racial preferences just create "another wrong."

--- Page 27 ---
Page 27 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
NASA clears space shuttle Columbia for liftoff this morning. It is carrying Israel's first astronaut into space,
and the security is even tighter than usual at Cape Canaveral.
17: Microsoft says it will begin paying a regular annual dividend to shareholders, a first for the software
maker.
21: Energizer agrees to buy the Schick-Wilkinson Sword razor business from Pfizer for $930 million,
challenging Gillette as it aims to expand beyond batteries.
23: Southwest posts fourth-quarter net of $42.4 million, making it the only profitable major U.S. airline.
Freddie Mac will restate its earnings higher for at least the past two years after its auditor recommended
accounting changes.
24: Amazon reports a quarterly profit for only the second time on a 28% sales jump and says it will continue a
free-shipping offer.
McDonald's reports a $343.8 million loss, its first ever as a publicly traded company, due to a charge for
closing restaurants. Earlier, a judge dismissed a suit seeking to hold the fast-food chain responsible for
obesity-related problems.
27: Tom Ridge was sworn in as the Homeland Security Department came into being Friday.
The Tampa Bay Buccaneers won football's Super Bowl on Sunday in a 48-21 victory over the Oakland
Raiders.
28: NextWave wins a big victory as the Supreme Court rules that the FCC had erred in reclaiming dozens of
wireless licenses. The decision lets NextWave, which has been operating under Chapter 11, regain full
control of spectrum licenses valued at around $6.5 billion to use or sell to other wireless carriers.
29: Bush, in a State of the Union speech at a critical juncture in his presidency, declares he is ready to take
on Iraq as well as the economy. He outlines an aggressive domestic agenda of tax cuts, a Medicare drug
benefit for recipients who join private HMOs, medical-malpractice caps and a ban on certain late abortions.
30: AOL Time Warner posts a 2002 loss of $98.7 billion -- the biggest in corporate history -- after a $45.5
billion charge, mostly to write down the value of its America Online unit. AOL also announces the resignation
of Ted Turner as vice chairman.
Sprint CEO William Esrey says he will step down and his presumed successor, President Ronald LeMay,
says he will resign. BellSouth's Gary Forsee is tapped for the job, sparking an executive spat.
31: Bush -- closing a month of Persian Gulf troop buildup, U.N. arms inspections and stern warnings -- gives
diplomacy "weeks, not months" to avert a war in Iraq. Earlier, in a report more critical than many expected,
U.N. arms inspector Hans Blix said Hussein hasn't displayed "genuine acceptance" of international demands
to give up banned weapons. Tens of thousands world-wide have joined in protest of possible war and U.S.
policy.
John Snow wins confirmation as Treasury secretary, filling a void in Bush's economic team and giving the
White House a spokesman to help sell Congress and the public on the president's economic package.
FEBRUARY
3: "The Columbia is lost," President Bush told the nation Saturday, after the space shuttle disintegrated upon
re-entry, killing the seven astronauts aboard.
4: Bush proposes a $2.23 trillion budget with some Reagan-era echoes. The plan, which has $1.3 trillion in
new or accelerated tax cuts over 10 years and proposals to overhaul or squeeze social programs, projects
deficits at least until fiscal 2007.
5: Sprint forced out its two top executives last month as part of a dispute over their use of a questionable tax
shelter. There isn't any indication that Esrey and LeMay did anything illegal. Days earlier, BellSouth won a
court order blocking Forsee from accepting an offer to be Sprint's CEO, touching off back-and-forth legal
wrangling.
GE Capital agrees to buy the consumer-finance business of Abbey National for $1.39 billion, continuing on
with its aggressive global expansion.

Page 28 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
6: Powell tells the U.N. that the Iraqi game is hide-and-seek in a presentation that includes riveting intercepts
of phone conversations, apparently on concealment tactics, and satellite photos the secretary of state says
showed pre-inspection cleanups. Earlier, Blix put the war clock at "five minutes to midnight."
7: The SEC will require Wall Street analysts to certify that they believe what they put in research reports and
public statements.
11: Johnson &Johnson agrees to pay $2.4 billion for California biotech Scios, in a deal that will bolster its
drug pipeline.
12: Alarm greets a purported bin Laden tape vowing new attacks. The audio recording broadcast by
al-Jazeera, which U.S. officials appeared to accept as genuine, urges Muslims to fight Americans and defend
Iraq.
BP will invest $6.75 billion in a Russian joint venture as part of its focus on six new areas.
El Paso says CEO William Wise will resign. The pipeline company has been under fire for its energy-trading
practices.
13: Sprint received big tax benefits in 1999 and 2000 from the exercise of stock options by its executives. The
exercises also made the telecom concern's performance look better by boosting its net asset value.
14: The Senate confirms Big Board ex-chairman William Donaldson as SEC chief, succeeding Harvey Pitt,
who quit election night over unhappiness at accounting regulatory efforts.
18: Major airlines push for worker concessions amid the industry's worst-ever recession and growing concern
about the economic fallout of an Iraq war. Losses for the nation's nine-biggest airlines totaled $11.2 billion in
2002.
A nightclub stampede, prompted by the release of pepper spray during an argument between patrons, kills 21
in Chicago.
A massive snowstorm smothers Northeast and mid-Atlantic states, snarling most travel and leaving more
than 20 people dead. Economists later predict the Presidents Day blizzard will leave little lasting economic
damage.
A suspected arson attack kills more than 120 and injures 145 on a subway in Taegu, South Korea. The
suspect, who has a history of mental illness, was trying to kill himself, police later say.
19: Jamie Kellner leaves his post as Turner Broadcasting's chairman and CEO. He will be succeeded by
Philip Kent, a former top executive at Turner. The move means that most of the top AOL executives
associated with the Time Warner merger now have left the company.
"Joe Millionaire" attracts up to 42.6 million viewers for its final episode, the biggest entertainment audience in
Fox's history.
21: The FCC derails Chairman Michael Powell's effort to deregulate local telephone companies, in a victory
for AT&T and WorldCom. By a 3-2 vote, the FCC frees the regional Bells of having to lease their new
high-speed data lines to competitors at discounted rates. But the agency defeats a proposal to also lift the
rules for the companies' phone lines.
(MORE)
24: Grieving relatives visit the site of the Rhode Island fire that killed 98 late last week. A dispute rages about
whether the band Great White had permission to use pyrotechnics in the packed bar.
Devon is buying Ocean for $3.5 billion in stock plus debt assumption, to form the top U.S. independent oil and
natural-gas producer.
25: Ahold ousts its CEO and chief financial officers and says it will have to lower its earnings for the past two
years by at least $500 million. The U.S. later opens inquiries into accounting at the Dutch supermarket
owner's U.S. Foodservice unit.
The SEC recognizes a fourth credit-rating agency, Dominion Bond, amid criticism that the existing firms have
an oligopoly.
26: Consumer confidence falls to its lowest level since 1993, hurt by energy costs, the terrorism threat and a
stagnant job market.

27: Bush lays out a vision of a Mideast transformed by an Iraq war. The president says toppling Hussein will
boost Arab democracy and help midwife an Israeli-Palestinian peace. However, after a month of diplomatic
wrangling, Germany, Russia and France remain resolute in their opposition to U.S. and British war plans.
Britain's Blair won parliamentary support for his Iraq policy despite a revolt by many in his Labour Party.
28: Died: Fred Rogers, 74, whose TV neighborhood welcomed children for decades, in Pittsburgh, from

MARCH
3: War planners scramble as Turkey votes down a northern front. Iraq begins demolishing its al Samoud 2
missiles under U.N. order, but Washington dismisses that as a "game of deception."
Al Qaeda takes a solid blow in the capture of a top Sept. 11 figure. Run to ground near Islamabad by U.S.

acts of terrorism ever on U.S. soil. He also is believed to have had a role in the Daniel Pearl slaying.

An Israeli raid on Gaza following Wednesday's bus bombing leaves 11 Palestinians dead, including eight who
witnesses said were killed by a tank shell as they worked to extinguish a fire.
11: Stocks slide to a five-month low and Treasury bonds rise, sending yields to their lowest level since 1958,
amid investor uncertainty about war with Iraq. The Dow Jones industrials drop 2.22% to 7568.18. The yield
on the benchmark 10-year Treasury note falls to 3.568%.
Palestinian lawmakers approve appointment of a prime minister, but won't give Mahmoud Abbas control of
security forces or peace talks as the U.S. wants. Arafat keeps that.
Bristol-Myers concedes its accounting has been "inappropriate" and that sales from 1999 to 2002 were

The Dow Jones industrials fall 44.12 to 7524.06, their lowest level of the year, on war concerns and some

years.
13: Utah police find Elizabeth Smart alive and in the company of a drifter called Emmanuel. The teen's June

14: Stocks soar, posting their biggest gains in five months. The Dow Jones industrials jump 269.68 points, or

biggest one-day drop in 16 months.
17: Bush set a "moment of truth" at the U.N. The president, at a snap Azores summit with Britain, Spain and
Portugal, said diplomatic efforts to disarm Saddam Hussein will end today. Big weekend protests
accompanied the meeting. The Iraqi dictator vowed to fight the U.S. "anywhere in the world."
The World Health Organization declares a global threat from a severe, hard-to-treat pneumonia that has killed
nine and infected about 150 in 10 nations or territories.
China completes a change in leadership as the National People's Congress confirms Hu Jintao president and
Wen Jiabao premier. Jiang Zemin keeps a key military post.
18: Stocks soar in both the U.S. and abroad amid expectations that a conflict in Iraq would end quickly. The
Dow Jones industrials climb 3.6% to 8141.92; the Nasdaq rises 3.9% to 1392.27. Markets in Germany,
France and Britain also surge.
Procter &Gamble announces a deal to acquire Wella, a German hair-care company, for roughly $5.9 billion.

Bush declares Saddam Hussein's options are now fight or flight. The president goes on TV to issue a 48-hour
ultimatum, after which war is set for "a time of our choosing." He urges Iraq's military not to fight for a "dying

U.S. forces open fire in a war Bush said may task the nation. Shortly after air-raid sirens begin wailing in

and liberation ride with the troops, but the fighting may be "longer and more difficult than some predict." The

U.S. troops in Afghanistan open a big offensive against al Qaeda fugitives outside Kandahar. Raids by more
than 1,000 helicopter-borne soldiers are the largest since Operation Anaconda a year ago.
Mahmoud Abbas accepts the job of Palestinian premier, a step in reviving Mideast peace efforts.
21: EDS ousts its chairman and CEO, Richard Brown, and names former CBS chief Michael Jordan to the top
spot. The world's No. 2 computer-services company has seen its shares plunge 75% in the past year amid
weak results and an SEC probe of its accounting.
24: Citigroup CEO Sanford Weill withdraws his nomination as a director to represent public investors on the
board of the NYSE.
25: Stocks plunge on fears that the war in Iraq could be more difficult than investors had anticipated. The
Dow Jones industrials post their worst percentage drop in nearly six months, tumbling 307.29, or 3.61%, to
8214.68. The Nasdaq falls 3.66% and the S&P 500 drops 3.52%.
Goldman's John Thornton, the firm's president and a top contender for the CEO job, quits, paving the way for
a power struggle.
Kmart reports a $3.22 billion loss for its latest fiscal year as sales continued to slide and it failed to win back
customers.
The NYSE is under fire for its corporate governance in the wake of its nomination of Citigroup's Sanford Weill
to be a director. A few days earlier, Weill withdrew his nomination.
26: The Senate votes to cut Bush's $725 billion tax-cut plan by more than half, a move that could threaten or
kill the president's proposal to repeal taxes on stock dividends.
27: Goldman ousts Todd Christie, CEO of its Spear Leeds unit and an NYSE board nominee. The SEC
chairman asks exchanges to review their corporate governance.
HealthSouth's finance chief pleads guilty to falsely certifying financial statements.
28: Lucent agrees to a $568 million settlement to resolve shareholder suits that alleged the telecom firm
engaged in financial fraud and aggressive sales practices.
Hong Kong orders school closings and quarantines to fight a new pneumonia, and Ontario, Canada, is taking
emergency steps.
31: US Airways emerges from bankruptcy-court protection, getting a green light to do so after less than eight
months.

airline unit's unions.
HealthSouth dismisses CEO Richard Scrushy and moves to replace its outside auditor, Ernst &Young.

"willing" doctor agreeing to follow their terms.
4: U.S. forces capture Baghdad's Saddam International Airport and special-forces troops raid one of
Hussein's palaces near Tikrit, north of the capital.
7: Israel balks at a U.S. peace "road map" unless the Palestinian premier cracks down on militants.
(MORE)

Resistance folds in Baghdad as crowds turn on the regime. Jubilant Iraqis kiss Americans in the streets and
beat on fallen statues and pictures of Saddam Hussein with shoes.
11: Air France and British Air confirm they will cease all Concorde flights this year, effectively retiring the
supersonic jet.
14: WorldCom wins backing from 90% of its creditors for a bankruptcy-reorganization plan.
United Airlines' pilots ratify a labor contract, and the carrier's mechanics union reaches a tentative pact on
concessions.
15: An Illinois judge reverses his earlier decision, saying Philip Morris won't have to post a $12 billion bond.
The ruling could have forced the cigarette maker into Chapter 11.
Pfizer and Pharmacia win final approval for their merger. Pharmacia's CEO plans to take the top post at
Schering-Plough.
Tikrit falls, and the Pentagon declares major combat over in Iraq.
16: The WHO definitively confirms the cause of SARS as a new member of a family of germs known as
coronaviruses.
The SEC names William McDonough, current president of the New York Fed, to head the new accounting
oversight board.
Nasdaq names Robert Greifeld, a financial-industry software executive, as its next CEO.
17: The Big Board opens investigation into whether at least two of its specialist firms may have engaged in
"front running," or trading ahead of clients.
President Bush signs an $80 billion spending bill to cover war costs, including funds for homeland security
and airline relief.
18: Bechtel wins a U.S. contract to rebuild Iraq's infrastructure. The contract, a starting point in reconstruction,

AMR workers react angrily to disclosure of special payments for American executives, setting off a firestorm.
The program is soon canceled.
21: Marines hand over control of the Iraqi capital to the Army as electricity is restored in some areas east of
the Tigris, U.N. aid starts to arrive and rudimentary commerce re-emerges.

Congress launches its own HealthSouth investigation, following on the heels of the Justice Department and

Arafat relents and allows Mahmoud Abbas to form the government he wants, a deal that opens the way for a

Iraq's former deputy premier, Tariq Aziz, surrenders to U.S. forces.
