28: A federal court rules that Grokster and StreamCast could continue to distribute Internet file-sharing
software. The judge rules that Grokster and StreamCast aren't breaking copyright law, even though their
software lets users illicitly copy and trade music and movies.
Former Merrill analyst Henry Blodget pays $4 million and is barred from the securities industry for life as part
of a settlement over his research calls.
29: Ten securities firms agree to pay a record $1.4 billion to settle government charges that they issued
overly optimistic stock research to win investment-banking business. The pact also settles charges that at
least two firms improperly doled out IPOs to executives.
Apple Computer rolls out its online music service, which lets users download songs for a fee.
30: Tyco finds roughly $1.2 billion in fresh accounting problems. The new problems come on top of $265
million to $325 million in charges announced in March.
ImClone's CEO and chairman both step down in connection with the firm's failure to pay taxes on options and
warrants.
MAY
1: Visa agrees to pay retailers $2 billion to settle a suit over debit-card fees. The move follows a $1 billion
settlement by MasterCard. Both agree to lower charges for most debit-card use. The settlements will end the
card associations' power to force merchants to accept their debit cards, and will let retailers use less-costly
alternative networks.
Bush says major combat is over in Iraq, but refrains from declaring victory amid continued fighting. Britain's
Blair says those who doubt banned weapons will be found will be "eating some of their words." Bush also
kicks off a Mideast peace push. The "road map" calls for an end to violence and for Palestinian statehood by
2005.

Kmart emerges from Chapter 11 after 15 months.

9: The SEC is in talks with Qwest to settle potential fraud charges stemming from the phone company's

the SEC contests.
13: The dollar falls to a new four-year low against the euro. The decline follows comments by Treasury
Secretary John Snow that convinces markets the White House secretly welcomes the economic benefits of a
weaker dollar.
Quattrone is indicted on criminal obstruction-of-justice and witness-tampering charges. Later this month, he

14: Ahold ousts the chief executive of U.S. Foodservice, the unit whose accounting problems have forced the

15: Treasury-bond yields plunge to their lowest levels in nearly 45 years.
Russia's lower house ratifies a landmark nuclear-disarmament deal with the U.S. that cuts arsenals by

19: The Labor Department says the April inflation rate hit a 37-year low, less than two weeks after the Fed
warns of deflation.
Warner Bros.' "Matrix" sequel sells about $135.8 million in tickets. The film ranks as the second-biggest-ever

20: The dollar slides to near a record low against the euro in the wake of Treasury Secretary Snow's remarks
indicating a retreat from a strong-dollar policy. The White House moves to limit the impact of Snow's

MCI agrees to pay investors $500 million to settle fraud charges the former WorldCom misled investors in the
biggest accounting scandal in U.S. history.
22: The tobacco industry wins a big victory as a Florida appeals court throws out a $145 billion judgment
against five companies. Later, the industry loses a gambit to have ad-fraud claims dismissed.
23: House and Senate tax writers approve a $350 billion economic package, clearing the way for final
passage. The bill's centerpiece temporarily cuts to 15% the top rate on dividends and capital gains.
Bond-fund dividends and some stock-fund dividends still are taxed as ordinary income. The compromise
package is less than half what Bush wanted, but it still gives him the third tax cut of his term. Bush signs the
bill days later.
27: The euro surges to a record against the yen and falls just short of a record against the dollar, after hitting
a high of $1.1876.
28: Tenet CEO Jeffrey Barbakow stepped down after the hospital chain's board met Friday and decided on a

JUNE
2: Bush indicates that the dollar's recent slide against the euro and yen has gone too far, contradicting recent
comments by Treasury Secretary John Snow.
The U.S. threat level fell to yellow Friday. Later this week, the Homeland Security chief concedes the
color-coded threat-level system is flawed and may put too much of a burden on local law enforcement.
Pixar's "Finding Nemo" sold an estimated $70.6 million of tickets in its opening weekend, a record for an
animated movie.
3: North Korea says it has nuclear arms and is nearly done reprocessing fuel rods to make more.
The FCC clears the way for broadcasters to buy more local affiliates and makes it easier for companies to
own both newspapers and TV stations in the same market. Later in the month, a Senate panel votes to
reverse the decision.
5: Liberian leader Charles Taylor is indicted by a U.N.-sponsored war-crimes tribunal for atrocities in
neighboring Sierra Leone.
Martha Stewart is indicted on criminal charges of securities fraud, conspiracy and making false statements to
federal agents. In a separate civil case, the SEC accuses her of insider trading related to her ImClone share
sale. Also charged is Peter Bacanovic, Stewart's ex-stockbroker at Merrill. Stewart relinquishes her chairman
and CEO posts at Martha Stewart Living. He and Stewart have denied any wrongdoing.
The House votes 282-139 to finish passage of a bill banning certain late-term abortions. Abortion-rights
groups vow an immediate court fight, one of the most significant since Roe v. Wade.
The Pentagon denies intelligence on Iraq arms was manipulated to sell the war, but a classified CIA report
displays greater uncertainty about Hussein's atomic program than Bush did.
6: The SEC forces six former and current Xerox executives, including two ex-CEOs and a former finance
chief, to pay $22 million in fines and other penalties to settle civil-fraud charges related to overstated
revenues and profits. The agency says the executives profited by bonuses and stock sales based on false
financial results.
A report on accounting fraud at WorldCom concludes for the first time that former CEO Bernard Ebbers
played a role in efforts to improperly boost revenue. A week later, two external probes find that Ebbers
conspired with top officials and employees beginning in the late 1990s to carry out a massive fraud. Ebbers's
lawyer says the investigation doesn't "point to a single piece of paper or any witness demonstrating that
Bernie Ebbers participated in or knew" of the fraud.
Howell Raines resigns as executive editor of the New York Times in the wake of a scandal over plagiarism by
reporter Jayson Blair.
The NYSE unveils rules that will lead to the disclosure of its chairman's pay and force officials to quit the
boards of listed firms.
9: Palestinian militants try anew to halt the latest peace effort. Hamas, Islamic Jihad and Al Aqsa Martyrs'
Brigades claim joint responsibility for a fatal attack on a Gaza checkpoint. On Friday, Hamas called off
cease-fire talks with the new Palestinian premier.
Oracle launches a $5.1 billion hostile takeover offer for rival PeopleSoft. If completed, the deal would make
Oracle the No. 2 independent software company after Microsoft. Later this month, Oracle sweetens its bid by
nearly 22%, but PeopleSoft rejects the bid and turns to takeover defenses and legal maneuvers to thwart the
rival.

Cellphone users can keep their telephone numbers when they change their phone companies, a federal
appeals court rules. The "number portability" rule will likely trigger an escalation in the already intense
industry pricing war.
10: Freddie Mac fires its president and asks its chairman and CEO and its finance chief to resign. The firm
reiterates plans to restate three years of earnings. The moves send Freddie's stock down 16%.
Bush insists Iraq had a program to make weapons of mass destruction, but stops short of promising hard
evidence will be found. Troops assigned to the hunt report running out of sites to search.
NASA launches the Mars Exploration Rover, the first of two mobile robots that are to explore for signs that
conditions for life ever existed.
11: Samuel Waksal is sentenced to seven years and three months in prison for insider trading, obstructing
justice and tax evasion.

officer, will be able to exercise stock options valued at $14.2 million for Brendsel and $5.3 million for Glenn.
The U.S. attorney and SEC have opened probes of the firm.
Mideast violence spirals upward, tearing at U.S. hopes for peace. The death toll since summits a week ago
nears 50.
13: Guidant pleads guilty to 10 felony counts involving its medical device to treat aneurysms. Among the
charges is that Guidant failed to disclose that its stent graft may have contributed to 12 deaths and dozens of
injuries. The firm agrees to pay the government $92.4 million. Guidant shares slide 6.2%.
Died: Gregory Peck, 87, film star who conveyed an image of graceful decency and courageous rectitude in
roles from "To Kill a Mockingbird" to "Moby Dick," in Los Angeles.
16: The U.S. farm economy is showing signs of a rebound after a five-year recession. Commodity prices and
exports are climbing and drought has eased in the Midwest.
17: Stocks surge to their highest level in nearly a year, boosted by some upbeat economic news. The Dow
Jones Industrial Average jumps 2.21% to 9318.96. The S&P 500 climbs 2.24% to 1010.74, its first close
above 1000 since last June. The Nasdaq soars 2.46% to 1666.58.
18: Rite Aid's ex-CEO pleads guilty to two conspiracy counts in a massive accounting fraud. Under the plea
deal, Martin Grass agrees to pay $3.5 million in fines and forfeiture.
The plaintiff in Roe v. Wade, who now regrets her decision, files a motion to overturn the landmark abortion
ruling of 1973. A week later, the motion is dismissed.
19: The Census Bureau says the Hispanic population grew at four times the overall U.S. rate, cementing the
group's place as the largest minority.
23: GM launches a $13 billion debt offering this week to help shore up its pension funds. The debt package is
the largest ever by a U.S. company. Strong demand for the deal pushes its overall size to more than $17
billion.
The fifth Harry Potter book by J.K. Rowling breaks book-publishing records, with Scholastic estimating
first-day sales of five million copies.
24: Biogen and Idec agree to merge in a $6.4 billion stock swap that would create the world's third-largest
biotech company in terms of revenue.
The Supreme Court keeps affirmative action at universities alive. In cases from the University of Michigan,
justices rule that a point system used to favor minorities in undergraduate admissions is unconstitutional, but
that the law school's weighing of race among other criteria passes muster because it isn't the "decisive"
factor.
26: The Fed cuts the federal-funds rate by a quarter point to 1%, its lowest level since 1958, and indicates
that the interest-rate target would remain there for a long time. The central bank says the economy has yet
to show "sustainable growth."
Freddie Mac says it understated past earnings by as much as $4.5 billion after taxes, blaming lax accounting
controls.

JULY
1: The Dow industrials ended the quarter up 12% at 8985.44, their strongest quarterly percentage gain since

3: The Nasdaq jumps 2.35% to 1678.73, its highest close in over a year. The industrials rise 1.13% to

Ernst &Young agrees to pay the government $15 million to settle an examination into the firm's handling of
tax shelters. The settlement relates to tax rules that require tax-shelter promoters to register certain tax
strategies with the government and to keep lists of investors.
Wal-Mart says it decided to include sexual orientation in its antidiscrimination policy after getting letters from
gay employees.
7: U.N. health authorities declared over the weekend that SARS is contained for now and removed the last
hot spot, Taiwan, from a list of infected areas.
Liberty Media's decision to buy Comcast's 57% stake in QVC for $7.9 billion on July 3 adds a new twist to
Vivendi's asset auction.
9: Microsoft says it will stop issuing stock options and instead begin giving its 50,000 employees restricted
stock. The decision comes amid mounting criticism of options and after three years of stock-market declines
that made them nearly worthless.
PG&E files for Chapter 11 for its wholesale energy unit and other subsidiaries, essentially scrapping a $3
billion investment.
A Sudan Air 737 crashes while attempting an emergency landing just after takeoff from Port Sudan, killing
116.
10: Nike will pay about $305 million to acquire Converse, a 95-year-old company known for its inexpensive
canvas sneakers.
(MORE)
11: Southern California Edison will cut utility rates by an average of 13%, its first reduction since the energy
crisis ended in 2001.
15: Citigroup declares a 75% increase in its annual dividend, to $1.40 a share. More companies are expected
to follow suit, driven by improving business and the impact of the recent tax cut.
Yahoo agrees to buy online-advertising company Overture for $1.63 billion, aiming to better compete with
Google.
Mirant files for Chapter 11, the latest merchant energy firm to seek bankruptcy protection.
16: Greenspan pledges to keep interest rates low for a long time, even if the economy rebounds. Despite his
vow, bond prices plunge, and the 10-year Treasury yield surges to 3.93%, as investors conclude that the Fed
won't cut rates again.
Citigroup says it plans to buy Sears Roebuck's massive credit-card and financial-products business for about
$3 billion.
Loral files for Chapter 11, and Boeing says it would take a $1.1 billion charge, amid a slump in the satellite
and rocket business.

18: The U.S. recession ended in late 2001 despite continuing contraction in the job market, the National

Government aid to airlines helps Delta, Northwest and Continental swing to profits despite a slump in spring

25: Boeing will lose $1 billion in future rocket business, as the Air Force determines the company illegally

DaimlerChrysler, hurt by its embattled U.S. arm, reports that net income fell 90% in the second quarter, which

California's rating on general-obligation debt is lowered by Standard &Poor's on political turmoil and a recall

Sony's net plunged 98% for its first quarter, but the company maintains its 2003 profit forecast.
28: MCI rivals have brought new fraud allegations to the attention of federal prosecutors, who have opened
an investigation. Verizon, AT&T and SBC claim MCI has been defrauding them and other phone companies
since 1994 through "call laundering." The move comes just weeks before a hearing to determine whether MCI
can emerge from Chapter 11. A week later, MCI says an internal review hasn't found evidence it improperly
routed phone calls.
29: Citigroup and J.P. Morgan Chase agree to pay a total of $305 million to settle actions related to loans and
trades made with Enron and Dynegy, ending 19 months of intense regulatory scrutiny. They also agree to
overhaul the way they vet their most complex financial deals. Regulators say the settlement involves $8.3
billion in loans improperly accounted for by Enron.
30: MGM pulls out of the auction for Vivendi Universal's entertainment assets. Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer says
Vivendi's $14 billion asking price was too high.
Thomas Patrick is departing as Merrill Lynch's No. 2 executive. He was an architect of the firm's drastic
cost-cutting efforts.
31 Pillowtex files for bankruptcy-court protection, saying it will close its 16 plants and sell off its assets.
Rudolph Giuliani is joining with Bear Stearns to launch a fund backing firms with antiterrorism products and
technologies.
A last Volkswagen Beetle rolls off the last line making Hitler's original "people's car." The run ends in Mexico
68 years and 21,529,464 Bugs later.
AUGUST
1: The Dow industrials advance 33.75 to 9233.80, ending July with a fifth straight monthly gain.
Halliburton swung to a profit in the second quarter, as contracts to rebuild Iraq's oil industry helped lift
revenue 11%.
4: Landmark rulings last week against IBM and Xerox, two companies that sought to profit by revising
employees' pension plans, have unsettled hundreds of firms that have adopted similar programs. The
separate rulings sliced through the arcana of pension rules to conclude that cash-balance plans discriminate
against older workers, cut older workers' benefits and serve to lower the costs and bolster the profits of
employers that use them.

Cisco posts a 27% rise in fiscal fourth-quarter profit and gives its most upbeat outlook in years. But it meets

Merck will spin off pharmacy-benefit manager Medco on Aug. 19, ending an experiment dogged by

Jakarta, Indonesia, is rocked by a hotel bombing that kills at least 14. The suspected suicide attack at the

8: Bombing of the Jordanian Embassy in Baghdad kills 11 and injures 50.
Hijackers crashed Flight 93 into a Pennsylvania field deliberately on Sept. 11 rather than face a passenger
uprising, investigators now think.
12: Liberia's torment may ease, as leader Charles Taylor decamps.
Delta says it will stop funding a special bankruptcy-proof pension program for executives and will cut some
management bonuses, seeking to end a "divisive debate" over executive compensation at the airline.
13: The Fed keeps its interest-rate target at a 45-year low of 1% and predicts that rates would stay near that
level for some time to come.
14: The U.S. and EU agree to reduce farm trade subsidies and tariffs.
15: The largest electric-power blackout in history hits eight U.S. states and Canada, including the cities of
New York, Toronto, Cleveland and Detroit, affecting 50 million people. Terrorism is quickly discounted. The
number affected far surpasses the 1965 outage in the same region.
Lehman and SG Cowen are fined a total of $7.5 million for failing to prevent fraud by former stockbroker
Frank Gruttadauria.
General Dynamics and Northrop win an $8.7 billion Navy contract to build six submarines.
18: Libya accepted responsibility for the 1988 Lockerbie bombing in a letter to the U.N. Friday.
Died: Idi Amin, 80, notably cruel Uganda dictator from 1971 to his 1979 ouster, Saturday, in Saudi Arabia.
20: An Iraq truck bomb kills the U.N. envoy in Baghdad and about 19 others. The latest in an increasingly
bold campaign by insurgents to destabilize the U.S.-led occupation is presenting Bush with unpleasant
options. The president vows the U.S. will "persevere through every hardship." The suicide attack hit a
Baghdad hotel the U.N. used since 1991.
A Jerusalem bomb blasts peace hopes, leaving at least 18 dead.
22: The Defense Department awards contracts valued at up to $28 billion to Health Net, Humana and TriWest
to manage its Tricare health system for five years.
The NYSE fines Salomon Smith Barney $1 million for failing to supervise brokers dealing with WorldCom
employees.
France's Chirac vows health-service action but also faults the public for not aiding the elderly in a heat wave
he said may have killed 10,000.
Kodak says it is reorganizing its business units to put executives with digital-imaging experience at the helm
of every division.
Coke signs 18-year-old basketball prodigy LeBron James to a six-year deal to pitch Sprite.
25: Freddie Mac bowed to pressure from its federal regulator Friday and agreed to remove Chief Executive
Greg Parseghian, a former head of Freddie Mac's $600 billion loan portfolio who was tapped to be CEO just
two months ago in a management shake-up.

Page 39 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The FBI and computer antivirus experts over the weekend averted a fresh attack of the disruptive SoBig
virus, which had clogged computer networks after spreading by e-mail last week. Earlier this month, a
so-called computer worm dubbed "Blaster" forced businesses to temporarily halt operations.
26: U.S. deaths in Iraq reach a grim milestone as the number of Americans killed in action or by accident
since Bush declared major combat ended May 1 rises to 138, the number who died in the war, according to
an Associated Press tally.
27: NASA is dressed down for safety lapses in the Columbia report. The investigative board's findings on the
Feb. 1 space-shuttle disaster that killed seven astronauts, as expected, pegs the cause as a wing hole,
punched at liftoff by a piece of foam-insulation debris, which let in superhot gases during re-entry. But it finds
space-agency problems run deeper and include a failure to learn from its errors and a mistaken belief that its
safety regimen is of top quality. At the same time, the report found nothing "inherently unsafe" about the
shuttles, leaving the door open for a resumption of flights.
The NASD orders brokers to pay refunds that could total in the tens of millions of dollars to mutual-fund
investors who didn't receive discounts on commissions.
(MORE)
28: Bush is scrambling to turn around a deteriorating Iraq situation. U.S. officials say they would consider
letting a U.N.-sponsored force help with security as long as it operates under U.S. command.
Bechtel's contract for postwar reconstruction work in Iraq will be increased by $350 million, or more than 50%.
The decision by U.S. officials to steer additional funds to Bechtel is the latest sign the Bush administration
has seriously underestimated the cost and complexity of rebuilding Iraq.
Oklahoma files criminal charges against MCI and six ex-executives, including its ex-CEO, for violating

Dollar Finishes Dreadful Year; Clouds Remain --- U.S. Current-Account Deficit And Low Interest Rates Present Continuing Threats to Currency
Currency Trading
Dollar Finishes Dreadful Year; Clouds Remain --- U.S. Current-Account Deficit And Low Interest Rates
Present Continuing Threats to Currency
By Craig Karmin, Aaron Lucchetti and Peter A. McKay
1,399 words
2 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOLLAR LIMPED to the finish line Wednesday, ending a brutal year in which it weakened to a record
low against the euro.
In thin trading, the dollar opened poorly on New Year's Eve in New York but recovered from its worst levels.
Late in the day, the euro had strengthened to $1.2579 from $1.2555 late Tuesday in New York. The dollar
rose to 107.37 yen from 107.07 yen Tuesday.
Despite clear signs of a rebounding U.S. economy, many observers expect 2004 to bring more challenges for
the U.S. currency. Rather than focusing on the economy, currency traders fret about the deficit in the U.S.
current account, a broad measure of trade, and short-term U.S. interest rates that are well below similar
rates in Europe and many other parts of the world. Some traders also mention terror threats as weighing on
the dollar.
If U.S. interest rates rise and the current-account deficit narrows this year -- as many analysts expect --
traders say there is likely to be a lag of several months to more than a year before the dollar starts to rally.
"We're going to see a continuation of the weak-dollar trend in 2004," says John Taylor, chief investment
officer for FX Concepts, a New York hedge fund.
The dollar's weakness is contributing to rising oil and gold prices and has started to raise concerns for
nascent growth in countries that use the euro. A weaker dollar helps U.S. companies by making exports
cheaper and repatriated profits richer. The reverse effect penalizes euro-zone companies, curtailing growth
and profit prospects.
In the worst year for the dollar since 1987, the euro gained 20% on the U.S. currency in 2003. The yen ended
the year nearly 10% higher against the dollar despite repeated efforts by the Japanese central bank to
weaken its currency. The Australian dollar, meanwhile, jumped 34% against its U.S. counterpart, on rising
global demand for gold, iron ore and other commodities that are plentiful in Australia.
The dollar's decline, while relatively orderly, has persisted for more than two years. "It doesn't shock me,"
says David Wyss, chief economist at Standard &Poor's. "The dollar has been overvalued for the last five
years, and according to Newton's laws of currencies, we have to have an equal and opposite undervaluation."
According to Mr. Taylor of FX Concepts, the dollar's future is simple to forecast: It is likely to keep falling until
U.S. interest rates become higher than those in Europe. Until then, holders of euros will reap a greater
return than holders of dollars. "And there's no sign of that happening," he says. Indeed, the European Central
Bank has indicated it is willing to tolerate a strong currency, while the U.S. Federal Reserve maintains it will
keep short-term rates low for a "considerable period."
Marcel Kasumovich, a currency analyst at Merrill Lynch, expects the U.S. current-account deficit to begin to
narrow this year, another important condition for the dollar to rebound. But because of the lag effect in
foreign-exchange markets, he thinks the dollar will continue to fall until about the middle of 2005. By then, he
expects the euro to have strengthened to about $1.40 and the dollar to have weakened to about 85 yen.
Other currencies to chalk up big gains against the dollar last year included the Canadian dollar, which rose
18% against the U.S. currency, and the British pound and the Swiss franc, which both rose 11%.
So far, bond yields in the U.S. haven't reflected the dollar's troubles, in part because American inflation
remains low and the labor market still has enough slack. Both factors convince many bond investors that the
Fed isn't likely to raise its short-term interest rate -- currently at a 45-year-low of 1% -- until mid-to-late 2004
and that any rate increases won't be huge.

--- Page 48 ---
Page 48 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
While investors in the U.S. and abroad have shed dollars, foreign central banks and official institutions
continue to buy and hold large amounts of dollar-denominated assets, primarily U.S. Treasury securities,
helping to keep Treasury yields low. According to Fed data, foreign central banks and institutions held about
$848 billion of Treasury securities as of Dec. 24, up more than 20% since the end of 2002.
"Are they going to take their money home and push bond yields up?" asks Mr. Wyss. "Some of that will
happen, but I don't think it's going to be severe" because the U.S. is still a good place to invest compared with
the alternatives.
Kenneth Hackel, chief U.S. fixed-income strategist at Merrill Lynch, says the relationship between the U.S.
and Asian countries such as China reminds him of vendor-financing arrangements that many U.S. companies
set up during the technology boom in the 1990s. In other words, Asian countries ultimately lend American
consumers the money to buy their exports, helping their own economies even though their investments in
U.S. bonds are hurt by the dollar's fall.
"Despite the decline in the dollar, they want to have the U.S. continue to be a major buyer of their goods," Mr.
Hackel says. "They don't seem to be moving" away from that. Foreign ownership of U.S. bonds fell a bit in
September but recovered in October.
Some of the selling pressure on the dollar is coming from Americans. Billionaire investor Warren Buffett, for
example, recently announced that he had made an investment in foreign currencies.
The weakening dollar, while not affecting U.S. Treasurys greatly, is starting to have a broader impact on other
markets, chiefly commodities. Gold and oil, for instance, are both priced in dollars, and the declining currency
has helped keep their prices high.
That dynamic was on display Wednesday, as the dollar recovered from early lows. Gold futures for February
delivery peaked at $418.40 a troy ounce, almost setting a 13-year high-water mark, but settled at $416.10,
down $1.10 for the day on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange. Crude-oil futures
peaked at $33.20 a barrel, but settled at $32.52, down 27 cents, on the Nymex.
Gold especially illustrates the dollar's impact. While the dollar price of gold rose nearly 20% in the past year,
the metal's price in euros has barely moved.
The dollar's role in the energy market, according to analyst Phil Flynn of Alaron Trading Corp., has been to
bolster demand and discourage the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries from releasing more oil. In
particular, he said the cartel's recent decision not to act, even if prices rise above a target range of $28-$30 a
barrel it set a few years ago, was driven by the dollar's weakness.
"They're essentially saying that the band is underpriced now, because it was set in dollars from two or three
years ago," Mr. Flynn said. "They're saying, in effect, that if the dollar has gone down 20% or 30% since then,
then they want 20% or 30% more revenue before they'll act."
But in the meantime, U.S. crude-oil inventories are becoming depleted, approaching what analysts and the
government say is a critically low level below 270 million barrels. On Wednesday, the Energy Information
Administration, which is the U.S. Department of Energy's statistical arm, said crude inventories fell for the
fourth time in five weeks, with stockpiles for the period ended Dec. 26 down 3.8 million barrels to 270.7 million
barrels. That means crude inventories are 3% below the year-ago level and 9% below the five-year average.
That could become a problem if there's a major cold snap sometime this winter, driving up demand for
heating oil and other refined oil products, Mr. Flynn said. "We're susceptible to a huge price spike," he said,
estimating that crude could hit $40 a barrel easily in such a scenario. "It's like we're writing the script for it."

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2003 --- Dollar Faces Further Pressure Under U.S. Deficits, Protectionism --- Drubbing Will Continue As...
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2003 --- Dollar Faces Further Pressure Under U.S. Deficits,
Protectionism --- Drubbing Will Continue As Confidence Diminishes; Terrorism Worries Still Nag
By Michael R. Sesit
1,159 words
2 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
R13
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
DON'T EXPECT the dollar to experience a major reversal of fortune after the beating it took in 2003. Most
currency strategists predict the currency will keep sliding.
Even so, making money in the foreign-exchange market this year won't be the cakewalk it was during the
past 12 months, as what many were expecting finally came to pass. Last year "was overdue and somewhat
predictable, as a range of currencies returned to more normal equilibrium levels," says Dirk Morris, head of
currency at Putnam Investments in Boston. "Most currency participants had a very good and profitable year."
But 2004 "will be a much tougher year to predict individual currency directions and make money," Mr. Morris
says. That's because -- except for possibly China and several other Asian countries -- exchange rates are
widely considered to be less out of kilter than they were a year ago.
On Dec. 31, 2003, Europe's common currency was trading at $1.2579, up from $1.05 at year end 2002 and
$1.1719 at its launch on Jan. 1, 1999. The dollar stood at 107.3653 yen on Dec. 31, compared with 118.76
yen at the start of 2003. Over the same period, the British pound rose to $1.7859 from $1.6097.
As it reached these year-end values, the dollar tumbled to its lowest level since late 1996 against the
currencies of the 12 countries that use the euro. It also fell to 11-year lows against the British pound, its
lowest point in a decade against the Canadian dollar, a seven-year low against the Swiss franc, six-year lows
against the Australian dollar and a three-year nadir against the yen.
Moreover, worried about the dollar's eroding purchasing power, investors and speculators drove the price of
gold futures to $416.10 an ounce, near an eight-year high.
"The dollar is facing a long-term, slow-motion crisis of confidence," says Anne Parker Mills, a
foreign-exchange economist at Brown Brothers Harriman &Co. in New York. "Or to put it less dramatically, a
shift in investor preferences away from dollar-denominated assets."
Six months from now, she sees the U.S. currency trading at $1.30 to the euro and 90 yen; and by year end
2004, Ms. Mills says, the euro could be 15% higher than now, at $1.45, and the dollar still at 90 yen.
Weighing on the dollar will be many of the same factors that have plagued it for much of the past year and a
half. "The two key economic negatives are the twin deficits" in the U.S. federal budget and the U.S. current
account, says Stephen Jen, chief currency economist at Morgan Stanley in London. A measure of trade in
goods and services plus certain financial transfers, the current account is projected to be more than $500
billion in deficit in 2003, or more than 5% of U.S. gross domestic product. And because Americans don't save
enough, the deficit requires foreign financing.
But with portfolios already chock-full of U.S. stocks and bonds, U.S. interest rates at four-decade lows and
concerns that the U.S. economic recovery and current Wall Street rally might not be sustainable, foreign
private investors are hesitant to keep purchasing American assets. Investors also are uneasy, because they
suspect the Bush administration welcomes a weaker dollar to boost exports, increase jobs and thus enhance
its re-election chances.
Another "important political negative [for the dollar] is the resurgence of protectionism," says Mr. Jen. "It
would diminish what has historically been a key explanation of America's economic dynamism: the ability of
companies to outsource and fully capitalize on their comparative advantages."
Additional dollar worries center on continued risks of a terrorist attack against the U.S. and the possibility that
Asian central banks could choose to reduce their huge dollar holdings and diversify their reserves more into
other currencies.

--- Page 50 ---
Page 50 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
While most analysts expect the dollar to remain weak throughout 2004, many believe its biggest declines will
come against Asian currencies. Because Asian countries have consistently intervened -- buying dollars -- to
keep their exports competitive, their currencies haven't risen as much against the dollar as the euro.
By year end, Mr. Jen sees the euro at $1.23, the dollar at 102 yen and the pound at $1.81.
Last year, the low level of interest rates in most of the world on cash deposits "pushed investors into
anything with yield -- whether in the form of corporate bonds, emerging-market debt, high-dividend stocks or
high-interest rate currencies," notes John Normand, a strategist at J.P. Morgan in London. He adds that it is
no coincidence that the highest-yielding currencies -- such as the Australian dollar, New Zealand dollar and
South African rand -- performed best in a year when the gap in yields between government and corporate
bonds contracted to near historic lows.
"Yield will still be a primary driver of currencies in 2004, but valuation will play a much bigger role in
determining relative performance," says Mr. Normand. "The cheapest currencies, like the yen, will play
catch-up, while the relatively more expensive currencies -- like the euro and the commodity currencies [those
of countries whose economies are commodities-driven] -- will lag." By year end, J.P. Morgan forecasts the
euro will be at $1.30 and the yen at 95 to the dollar.
Looking back, 2003 will be remembered as "the year the dollar decline took hold," says Brown Brothers' Ms.
Mills. It was also a year in which Sweden said "no" to adopting the euro, protectionism raised its ugly head
and Japan spent roughly $186 billion to keep the yen from rising too far, too fast. What's more, it was a period
when the U.S. and much of Europe called on China to stop pegging its yuan to the dollar and let it drift higher.
That, many analysts contend, would make Chinese products more costly and, therefore, less competitive.
Both Ms. Mills and Putnam's Mr. Morris put the odds of a yuan revaluation this year at 50-50. But "if China
doesn't revalue, it could trigger a big political backlash in the U.S.," Mr. Morris warns.
He envisions two scenarios: "One, on the back of a Chinese revaluation, the dollar could fall further to about
100 yen and $1.35 against the euro," Mr. Morris says. "In contrast, if the Chinese don't move and a trade war
breaks out between Asia and the U.S., Japan is the most exposed, given the frailty of its economy, and the
dollar could go to 115-to-120 yen," while the euro stays firm in the $1.20-to-$1.25 range.

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2003 --- Another Run for the Bulls --- Fabulously Low Interest Rates Help End Markets' Decline, but...
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2003 --- Another Run for the Bulls --- Fabulously Low Interest
Rates Help End Markets' Decline, but Investors Wonder How High Stocks Can Rise
By E.S. Browning
2,032 words
2 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS HAVE JUST completed their first up-year since 1999, and already, investors are hoping for more.
What a change that is from the mood 15 months ago, when the fear was that stocks were headed down for
the foreseeable future.
What ended the bleeding in the stock market was an economic recovery touched off by some of the lowest
interest rates in 45 years. At first, it was just a small group of daring investors who foresaw the rebound and
bet on it. As evidence slowly mounted this year that the economy had hit bottom and turned up, more
investors began to pile in.
Despite a slump early in the year, touched off by worries over the war in Iraq, the Dow Jones Industrial
Average finished 2003 with a gain of 2,112.29 points, or 25.3%, at 10453.92. That compares with a gain of
25.2% in 1999, the last winning year for any major index.
From one perspective, the return to the 10000 level was small consolation. The Dow, after all, first crossed
10000 early in 1999, meaning that even after its huge rebound of the past 15 months, there has been little
progress in well over four years. For many investors, however, the recovery from the five-year low hit in
October 2002 was such a relief that the lack of progress over the longer term didn't matter.
The Dow now is up 43.5% since its low of 7286.27, on Oct. 9, 2002, and the more volatile Nasdaq Composite
Index has risen 80% since its low of 1114.11 that same day. To some, the near despair of the bear market
has begun to seem almost like a bad dream.
The recovery of the past 15 months brought the Dow industrials more than half the way back to the record
high of 11722.98 hit four years ago, in January 2000. (The Nasdaq composite, however, remains far from its
record of 5048.62, hit in March 2000. It finished 2003 at 2003.37, up 50% on the year.)
Rightly or wrongly, the main question on most people's minds today is no longer how far stocks will fall, but
how high they can rise. They are asking: Is this bull market a cyclical thing that will run out of steam some
time this year, sending the Dow back below 10000? Or is it a longer-lasting, "secular" affair that will push the
blue chips up to a new record in the next year or two?
The prevailing view is rosy.
"The combination of low interest rates, tax cuts, a weaker dollar and lean inventories has restarted the U.S.
economic engine. Momentum is now building, which should carry into 2004. While the outlook might appear
too good to be true, expect solid economic growth to be accompanied by gradually rising employment, low
inflation and higher corporate profits," Lynn Reaser, chief economist for Banc of America Capital
Management, wrote in a preview of the new year.
Many money managers have shifted funds toward stocks, in hopes that, even if the gains are slower than in
the old year, they will be robust.
"All this stimulus will generate stronger economic growth next year. That means higher stock prices," said
David Kotok, president of money-management firm Cumberland Advisors in Vineland, N.J., in a report to
clients. He compared the outlook for stocks during the current war on terrorism to the market's performance
during World War II, a time when stocks rose.
"Stocks are headed higher," Mr. Kotok advised his clients. "We believe that stock portfolios should remain
fully invested in a broadly diversified way."

--- Page 52 ---
Page 52 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Less than a year ago, with the U.S. fighting in Iraq, investors wrung their hands over stocks' ability to post
lasting gains in this unsettled world. In March stocks hit their lows for the year. Since then, if people haven't
shrugged off those worries entirely, they have at least suppressed them.
Few analysts think stocks can rise 20% again this year, but a large number of them are forecasting some kind
of gain. That marks a sharp contrast to the feeling one year ago, when a fourth consecutive year of stock
declines seemed a real threat.
To some forecasters, this building optimism is a negative sign. The concern is that stocks tend to rise most
strongly at the start of an economic recovery, not when the recovery is already well under way.
The worry-word in the front of many people's minds today is "sustainability." The question is whether the
robust recovery, which saw the economy rebound 8.2% at an annual rate in the third quarter, can be
sustained. The fear is that, as economic growth and profit gains inevitably slow, investors will be disappointed
and begin pulling back from stocks, capping the rally.
Adding to these worries, the broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock Index is trading at 27 times its companies'
earnings for the past 12 months, well above the historic average of 16. Analysts like to say that stocks are
"priced for perfection," meaning that such high price-earnings ratios can be justified only if the economy,
corporate performance and world stability all remain strong. If chinks start to appear, stocks could suffer.
Some analysts, including investment strategist Tobias Levkovich at Citigroup's Smith Barney brokerage arm
in New York, worry that people have become so optimistic that problems are almost inevitable.
If the economy continues to be robust, he noted in a report last month, then interest rates should move
higher, diluting the stock market's main fuel. On the other hand, if the economy falters, earnings could suffer,
also hurting stocks. And if the economy pursues a middle course, growing modestly, then economists could
start worrying again about deflation, or falling prices.
"Overall, a frustrating year for investors is quite plausible, despite perhaps an initially good beginning," Mr.
Levkovich warned.
At the same time, signs of difficulty were cropping up in other financial markets.
Reflecting worries about possible future inflation and a weaker dollar, gold prices soared past $400 in 2003,
from less than $300 at the start of 2002 and less than $350 at the start of last year.
Oil prices, which rose above $30 a barrel last year as the Iraq war loomed, finished the year above $30
despite the war's quick completion, held up by a recovering economy and by continued Middle East tensions.
Commodity prices surged.
But overall, consumer prices remained remarkably stable. And that was great news for interest rates.
Because of low inflation and a desire to ensure a strong economic recovery, the Federal Reserve kept its
target short-term rate at just 1%, a 45-year low.
That low benchmark rate, together with residual worries about the economy and world tensions, kept the
10-year Treasury note remarkably little changed despite the economic recovery. Although its yield fluctuated
sharply during and after the Iraq fighting, the 10-year yield finished at only 4.25%, not that far from the 3.8%
at which it started the year.
The year's real success story, however was the stock market. Its gains were the sort of thing some investors
had begun to despair of ever seeing again.
The broad S&P 500 rose 26.4% on the year. The Wilshire 5000, an index of about 5,200 stocks including
almost every publicly traded company based in the U.S., gained 29%. The Wilshire's market value, a
measure of the total value of U.S. stocks, rose $4.2 trillion, to $13 trillion, from the low it hit on Oct. 9, 2002, to
the end of 2003. That compares with $17 trillion at the record high on March 24, 2000.
One of the oddest things about the recent rally has been the feeling of deja vu. By far the biggest gainers
have been the stocks that were the most discredited during the bear market -- technology stocks, notably
those related to the Internet.
Amazon.com, which lost more than 90% of its value during the bear market, more than doubled in value last
year. Rambus, which makes technology for computer chips, nearly quadrupled in 2003. China.com, a
Chinese Internet company whose stock trades in the U.S., was up more than 180%, despite a sharp pullback
in the latter part of the year.

--- Page 53 ---
Page 53 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
One of the biggest questions facing professional investors, aside from the market's overall direction, is
whether these volatile companies can continue to lead the gains. It is an issue the pros call "rotation," and the
question is whether market leadership is in the process of "rotating" to a new group.
Among the weaker stocks for most of the past year were those that are the most stable and benefit the least
from an economic upturn. Those include makers of drugs and consumer staples like food and diapers, whose
sales remain stable and predictable regardless of the economy's swings. At year's end, the volatile tech
stocks hit headwinds, while the laggard "quality" stocks -- so-called because their earnings are so predictable
-- began to catch up.
Some analysts said this was nothing but year-end profit-taking in the stocks that had risen the most. They
forecast that tech stocks would take off again in the new year. But others pointed out that, when a lasting new
bull market begins, the leaders rarely are the same as those that led the previous bull market. And with baby
boomers facing more health needs, many stock-pickers have been saying for years that health stocks are
overdue to show some lasting strength.
"We continue to like technology, media and selected industrial companies," said Mark Jordahl, chief
investment officer at U.S. Bancorp Asset Management in Minneapolis. "Having said that, we believe a key
strategy to get right in 2004 is a rotation out of these more volatile sectors into higher quality, larger
companies with the capacity to grow earnings. These may also be higher-dividend-paying stocks."
He is preparing to shift to the stable group from the volatile one once he sees the pace of earnings growth
begin to slow.
That kind of shift to "defensive," stable stocks can be a sign that the uptrend is getting long in the tooth, says
Phil Roth, chief technical market analyst at New York brokerage firm Miller Tabak. Some market strategists,
in fact, are warning clients that the bull market could end in the coming months.
(MORE)
"We are not forecasting a long-term bull market," John Caldwell, chief investment strategist at McDonald
Financial Group, a brokerage and money-management arm of Cleveland's KeyCorp, wrote in a report to
clients.
"Investors did not learn a lesson over 2000-2002. We continue to see an unhealthy addiction to technology
stocks, as well as a revival of the margin debt problem." Stock gains could be lower than earnings gains this
year, he said.
But the great majority of forecasters are reassuring clients that stocks should continue to rise now.
"Our models and analysis suggest that it will be very difficult for the stock market in 2004 to `score' the same
type of returns that it did in 2003," wrote Brian Belski, fundamental market strategist at brokerage firm U.S.
Bancorp Piper Jaffray in Minneapolis, in a recent report to clients. Still, he forecasts that the S&P 500 will be
up as much as 14% over the next year to 18 months, rising to the 1200-1250 range from the 1111.92 at
which it finished 2003.
The Conference Board, a business-supported research group, last month revised its economic forecast
sharply upward, projecting that the economy will grow 5.7% this year, after controlling for inflation, the
strongest year in two decades.
And some investors are reminding themselves that, as was the case last year, stocks have a way of
surprising people -- both on the way up and on the way down.

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2003 --- Commodities Markets Surge Again as World-Wide Demand Grows --- Soybeans Jump to Highs, Gold...
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2003 --- Commodities Markets Surge Again as World-Wide
Demand Grows --- Soybeans Jump to Highs, Gold Crosses Over $400 And Natural Gas Rises
By Peter A. McKay
2,472 words
2 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
R14
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
MAJOR COMMODITY markets rose for a second straight year in 2003, as recovering economies world-wide
required ever greater amounts of raw materials to churn out finished goods for consumers. The U.S. dollar's
plunge against major foreign currencies made raw materials priced in dollars inexpensive in overseas
markets, encouraging U.S. exports. China was a voracious buyer of several U.S. agricultural products.
Among the winners: Soybean prices soared more than 40%, to near multiyear highs. Gold crossed the
threshold of $400 a troy ounce for the first time in more than seven years. Natural gas surged ahead in the
fourth quarter.
As 2004 begins, prices for most commodities seem poised to continue higher, albeit at a slower pace. The
Dow Jones-AIG Index of 20 commodities finished last year at 134.672, up 22%, compared with a 24% gain in
2002.
Most foreign-exchange traders expect the dollar to stay weak, prolonging its bullish effect on commodities.
The continuing growth of the American economy also should boost commodity prices for the foreseeable
future, said James Glassman, senior U.S. economist for J.P. Morgan Chase &Co. "Industrial activity all
around the world is taking off," Mr. Glassman said. "Personally, I'm cheered by it. I don't really see inflation
as a worry -- yet."
The possibility that the Federal Reserve may raise interest rates later this year isn't a major concern right
now, he said. A more immediate risk is that some European central bank will jump the gun, tightening a
major commodity-consuming country's money supply and cutting off a major source of cash to buy raw
materials.
Another threat to commodity prices is the relative strength of the U.S. stock market. Investment demand
helped drive some resources' prices higher in 2003 -- especially gold -- but investors this year might instead
decide to chase the gains in the newly robust stock market.
The new year also is likely to see continued change in the structure and governance of commodities markets.
The German-Swiss online bourse Eurex plans to launch a U.S. financial-futures market in February, for
example, although it still needs regulatory approval and has run into fierce opposition from traditional Chicago
markets.
The Commodity Futures Trading Commission is reviewing U.S. futures exchanges' self-regulatory activities
and governance, a probe that has attracted more interest among industry insiders since executive
compensation and governance became an issue at the New York Stock Exchange. The commission's final
report, expected sometime early in 2004, may order significant changes in exchanges' business practices.
The New York Board of Trade relocated into the headquarters of the New York Mercantile Exchange in 2003,
fueling rumors of a merger between the two former rivals. The former headquarters of Nybot were destroyed
in the Sept. 11, 2001, terrorist attacks, and traders worked for more than a year out of a cramped backup
facility in Queens.
Here is a rundown of the major markets in 2003:
Energy
The diplomatic standoff with Iraq drove energy prices higher in the first few months of the year, but markets
moved little once all-out war erupted in mid-March. That relatively subdued reaction was in sharp contrast to
the first Gulf War in 1991, when crude prices soared to more than $40 a barrel on the first day of fighting.

--- Page 55 ---
Page 55 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Crude oil, which began the year around $31 a barrel, fell to lows around $25 by late April, after U.S. forces
succeeded in deposing Saddam Hussein's regime.
Prices gyrated through the rest of the year but generally trended upward, amid rising U.S. demand, falling
stockpiles and tight supply control by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries. Crude-oil futures
ended the year at $32.52 a barrel, up $1.32, or 4.2%, at the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Along with the rise in crude, gasoline futures gained 6.32 cents, or 7.3%, to 93.29 cents a gallon. Heating-oil
futures rose 4.6 cents, or 5.3%, to 91.46 cents a gallon at Nymex.
OPEC's statements lately seem to point toward a continuation of high prices. The cartel's incoming president,
Indonesian Oil Minister Purnomo Yusgiantoro, has said he is concerned about high oil prices, but most
officials within the organization have said the problem is driven by excess demand and is unlikely to reach the
point where it will spark release of additional barrels.
Meanwhile, U.S. inventories are running low, according to the Energy Information Administration, the
Department of Energy's statistics arm. Through mid-December, EIA reported stockpiles of crude were at
272.8 million barrels, more than 9% below the five-year average and close to the 270 million-barrel level that
is often considered a sign of unusually low inventories. "I don't want to make it sound like it's a cliff you fall off,
but it certainly is an indicator of tightness," EIA Administrator Guy Caruso said.
John Conolly, head of the futures division of Terra Nova Trading, a Chicago brokerage, said high demand is
likely to continue at least through early 2004, because of increasing U.S. economic strength. "In the near
time, I don't see the crude market going crazy, but there will be pressure to the upside," he said.
Nymex natural-gas futures were up sharply in 2003, closing at $6.189 per million British thermal units, up
29%, or $1.40. Much of the gain, however, came between Thanksgiving and early December, when there
was an early cold snap and snowstorms in the Northeast. Prices weakened in the last two weeks of trading --
a trend analysts expect to continue into the new year, since gas inventories are about 8.1% above year-ago
levels, according to the American Gas Association.
Metals
Gold, which has soared ever since the September 2001 terrorist attacks, continued to rise in 2003,
confirming its traditional role as an investor safety net during times of uncertainty. Other precious metals
followed, while copper led industrial metals higher, strengthening along with the U.S. economy.
Gold futures jumped $67.90, or more than 19%, to $416.10 an ounce in the most-active February contract
on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange. But opinion is split on how much longer
average investors' interest can last. For more than 20 years, gold was generally out of favor on Wall Street
as the stock market rose. During the dot-com boom of the late 1990s, it hit lows around $250.
Jeffrey Christian, managing director of the New York-based research firm CPM Group, doesn't expect a
decline back to those levels, but he notes that increases in gold-investment demand usually last only a year
or two. "I won't necessarily say it's going to end tomorrow, but there's no guarantee it's going to run too much
further, either," Mr. Christian said.
Gold's gains already appear to be slowing somewhat, in percentage terms. Last year's more than 19% gain
compared with a 25% gain in 2002, although the absolute dollar value of the move was similar.
But there are still true believers in the investment community. Frank Holmes, chairman and chief executive
officer of U.S. Global Investors, which manages several gold-oriented mutual funds, said Wall Street analysts
are out of touch with average investors' lingering discomfort with "paper assets," spurred by the
Internet-company crash, terrorism, war and the weak currency. He believes investors will continue to flock to
gold as a tangible store of value -- a common argument put forward by "gold bugs" -- and gold could gain
17% or so more in 2004, to about $500.
"Wall Street has been calling for the gold rally to end for the last two years now -- ever since it began, really,"
said Mr. Holmes. "But it just keeps going."
Some of the market's big players have quietly jumped on the gold bandwagon along with everyday investors.
One indicator of that, said veteran gold trader George Gero, is that the number of gold futures and options
outstanding recently topped one million contracts for the first time at Comex.
"That means the hedge funds [lightly regulated investment pools designed for wealthy individuals and
institutions] are getting involved in a big way in this market," said Mr. Gero, senior vice president at Legg
Mason Wood Walker Inc. "That's . . . a really bullish sign for the metal."

--- Page 56 ---
Page 56 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Other metals also did well in 2003, and most have a bright outlook for the new year, especially if the U.S.
economy remains robust. Silver futures rose $1.153, or 24%, to $5.965 an ounce at Comex. Platinum, which
continues to be in short supply, jumped $210.90, or more than 35%, to $805.30 an ounce on Nymex.
But palladium, a component in automobiles' catalytic converters, dropped 17%, or $40.50, to $197.50 an
ounce, as platinum became a substitute in the emissions-reducing car parts.
Copper leapt 34.3 cents, or 49%, to $1.0455 a pound in the March contract at Comex, close to a six-year
high, with the most dramatic gains coming after the U.S. government reported a jump in economic growth in
the third quarter.
Other base metals surged in trading at the London Metal Exchange. Aluminum rose $244.75, or 18%, to
$1,588.75 a metric ton. Zinc jumped $254.75, or 34%, to $1,003.75 a metric ton.
Agriculture
Grains and other crops were helped by strong demand for U.S. exports, while the discovery of mad-cow
disease in the U.S. weighed on beef prices.
Soybean futures soared $2.29, or more than 40%, to $7.94 a bushel in the March contract at the Chicago
Board of Trade, as China made several large purchases throughout the year. "Right now, China is the
8,000-pound gorilla in the soybean market," said analyst Victor D. Lespinasse of A.G. Edwards &Sons.
The U.S. Department of Agriculture recently said it expects China to import a record 808 million bushels of
soybeans over the next year, up 55 million bushels from its October estimate and more than double the
amount China imported from all sources during the past year. If the forecast is accurate, China will import
more than half of the soybeans it uses.
(MORE)
But China is a net exporter of corn and doesn't consume much wheat. Hence, those items haven't rallied as
strongly as soybeans. At CBOT, wheat rose 52 cents, or 16%, to $3.77 a bushel. Corn was up 10.25 cents, or
4%, at $2.46 a bushel.
China also was a major player in the cotton market, which saw futures rise 47%, or 23.91 cents, to 75.07
cents a pound last year at the New York Cotton Exchange division of the New York Board of Trade. And, in a
sign that the rally is likely to continue, the USDA forecasts that China's domestic cotton crop will fall by about
14% this year, raising demand for U.S. imports.
Livestock markets face a murkier outlook this year. Pork prices fell in 2003, amid a glut of animals being
delivered to slaughterhouses late in the year. At the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, hog futures rose 1.825
cents, or 3.5%, to 53.425 cents a pound. Frozen pork bellies, the parts of hogs from which bacon and other
products are made, gained 0.275 cent, or 0.3%, to 86.475 cents a pound.
Beef prices were much stronger throughout the year, partly because of the popularity in the U.S. of the Atkins
diet, which advocates heavy protein consumption. But the discovery last month of a cow in Washington state
with mad-cow disease hurt prices in the last week of trading. Taiwan, Japan, Mexico, Chile and other
countries slapped bans on the import of U.S. beef after the discovery. The U.S. exports about 10% of its
annual production.
Live-cattle futures were down 8%, or 6.1 cents, to 73.525 cents a pound at CME.
"Everything in the meat sector will change in some way from this point forward," said Timothy Ramey of D.A.
Davidson &Co.
Shares of food companies and hamburger chains also have come under pressure lately, with some analysts
worried that a mad-cow scare might take several years to subside in the U.S., as it did in Europe when the
brain-wasting disease was discovered a few years ago.
Tropical Commodities
Coffee was the only one of the major tropical "soft" commodities to post gains in 2003, a situation analysts
expect to continue in the new year.
Coffee futures rose 4.75 cents, or 7.9%, to 64.95 cents a pound on the Coffee, Sugar, and Cocoa Exchange
division of Nybot. Stockpiles fell, and are likely to remain tight this year because Brazil's coffee crop is unlikely
to increase as much as previously expected, because of poor growing conditions, said Judith Ganes-Chase,
an independent commodity consultant.

--- Page 57 ---
Page 57 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
She said she sees little hope for frozen orange-juice prices, which have been hurt by the emphasis on low
carbohydrates in the Atkins diet. Also, Florida's orange crop is likely to grow about 25% this year, which could
mean a glut of juice. Futures fell 29.15 cents, or 31%, to 64.1 cents a pound at Nybot.
Sugar dropped 1.94 cents, or more than 25%, to 5.67 cents a pound, depressed by an 11th consecutive year
of world-wide production surpluses. "There's just a mountain of sugar out there that's not going to disappear
anytime soon," Ms. Chase said.
In the cocoa market, prices were strong early in 2003, rising near to 17-year highs because of political unrest
in the Ivory Coast, the world's leading cocoa producer. But the West African nation's politics have since
stabilized, and by year's end, cocoa futures plummeted 25%, or $506, to $1,515 a metric ton (approximately
2,200 pounds).
This year, analysts say the Ivory Coast seems headed toward a cocoa harvest of at least one million metric
tons, about 25% higher than originally expected and a sign that prices are likely to remain low. "This has been
a crazy time in this market," analyst Boyd Cruel, of Alaron Trading, said. "We've gotten a little more clarity as
the year has progressed, but it's not necessarily the news that producers would have wanted."

Sally Jones, Richard Gibson and Terin Miller contributed to this article.

Nasdaq's Surge for the Year Hits 50% --- Tech Barometer Crosses Over 2000 And Dow Industrials Rise 125.33; Traders Say It Isn't Mania Again
Monday's Markets
Nasdaq's Surge for the Year Hits 50% --- Tech Barometer Crosses Over 2000 And Dow Industrials Rise
125.33; Traders Say It Isn't Mania Again
By E.S. Browning
1,521 words
30 December 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE STOCK MARKET has begun hitting milestones again -- but without some of the giddiness that investors
felt in the late 1990s, when stocks did this for the first time.
Yesterday, the Nasdaq Composite Index closed above 2000 for the first time since January 2002, almost two
years ago, when it was still sinking after the bursting of the technology-stock bubble. The index first crossed
the 2000 milestone in 1998, on the way to a record high of 5048.62 in March 2000, at the pinnacle of the
technology craze. Earlier this month, the Dow Jones Industrial Average pushed back through 10000, a mark
first crossed in 1999.
The Nasdaq's surge -- yesterday it jumped 1.69%, or 33.34 points, to 2006.48 -- leaves the tech-heavy index
up 50% since this year began, and up 80% since it bottomed out and headed up on Oct. 9, 2002.
But investors aren't looking quite as wide-eyed today as they did before.
The recent gains are more a tribute to low interest rates and a recovering economy than to the kind of
tech-stock euphoria that took over investors' psyches in the 1990s. It is a quieter, more determined
confidence in the American economy that is driving stocks, and while some stocks seem to have hit
unsustainable heights, broad market expectations seem less inflated today than they did back then.
"There is a general view that we've got improving fundamentals, an improving economy and an indication
from the Federal Reserve that we are going to have interest rates that won't move up quickly in 2004. Those
things have led the path of least resistance to be up," said Matthew Johnson, head of U.S. stock trading at
New York brokerage firm Lehman Brothers. "A terror-free holiday has helped" as well, he added.
Stock indexes are poised to turn in their first year of gains since 1999, after a painful bear market brought
three consecutive years of declining stocks.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, whose stocks have been more staid than those of the Nasdaq composite
(though the two indexes do share Intel and Microsoft as members), nevertheless is up 25% so far this year.
Yesterday, the Dow rose 125.33 points, or 1.21%, to an even 10450.00, its first finish above 10400 in 21
months.
In a sign of how broad the gains have been this year, all 30 of the stocks in the Dow industrials rose on the
day, a relative rarity that now has happened five times this year.
Stocks typically rise in December, and especially at the end of December, and traders attributed the day's
gains more to that late-year momentum than to any specific news.
But there were at least two positive developments that contributed to the market advance. There were
indications that retailers' holiday sales were stronger than skeptics had feared.
And with the fourth quarter -- a period that is crucially important to many companies' full-year sales and
earnings -- drawing to a close, few companies have been issuing warnings of disappointing results to come.
That lack of announcements of bad news to come has fueled a quiet hope that fourth-quarter results, due out
in the middle of next month, could surpass expectations, as has been the case with earnings in 2003, and
could fuel further stock gains in the new year.
"We have two trading days left in the year, and there isn't a lot of negative news coming out," said John
Meara, president of money-management firm Argent Capital Management in St. Louis. "People are

--- Page 59 ---
Page 59 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
positioning themselves for a pretty good earnings season coming up, and people are positioning for a pretty
good first half of the new year.
"Two years ago," when the Nasdaq was last at these levels, "there was an absence of buyers," Mr. Meara
continued. "Today, there is an absence of sellers."
Traders said the day's stock moves were exaggerated by the fact that trading volume was light -- well below
average on both the Nasdaq Stock Market and the New York Stock Exchange, with many investors away for
the holidays.
The indexes' big gains overshadowed some less-positive market indicators. Gold hit $414.80 an ounce in late
New York trading, its highest level in 13 years.
Soaring gold prices typically reflect worries about the risk of higher inflation, a lower dollar or an uncertain
world, but lately, gold and technology stocks have managed to rise in tandem.
At the same time, the dollar hit another record low against the young euro, which has existed only since 1999.
The dollar also drew near the three-year low against the yen that it hit earlier this month.
Treasury bonds fell sharply as money shifted toward stocks and a belief spread that the economic recovery is
on track, which sooner or later will force interest rates higher, hurting the value of existing bonds.
Some analysts already are warning that stocks have risen so much this year that they have "stolen" some of
the gains that normally would come next year, following the widely anticipated good earnings news due out in
January. In that view, investors could find the coming year a disappointment.
But the doubters have been issuing such warnings almost since the day that stocks first turned up in October
of last year, and the stock market's resilience has built a level of confidence that has drawn more and more
investors back to stocks.
Since the first quarter of this year, when stocks slumped amid worries about the Iraq war, the market has
rarely pulled back more than a few percentage points at a time before resuming its rise, and that has
reinforced investor optimism.
Because the Nasdaq Composite Index is calculated based on the total market value of all the companies on
the Nasdaq Stock Market, the stocks that move the index tend to be the biggest stocks and not necessarily
those with the biggest percentage gains.
The largest contributor to the Nasdaq's 80% rise since October 2002 has been chip-making giant Intel, which
accounted for more than 9% of the Nasdaq gain, according to research and money-management firm Birinyi
Associates in Westport, Conn.
After Intel came such big Nasdaq stocks as Cisco Systems, Microsoft, Oracle and eBay.
As is often the case, however, smaller, more volatile stocks put in bigger percentage gains -- they just had
less impact on the index because they are smaller in size.
Rambus, a company that makes technology for computer chips, has nearly quadrupled in value this year
alone. China.com, a Chinese Internet company listed on the Nasdaq, has nearly tripled.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index, a broad index of large stocks, is at a 20-month high, and yesterday it
was up 1.24%, or 13.59 points, at 1109.48 -- its first close above 1100 since May 2002. It is up 26% so far
this year.
The Russell 2000 small-stock index rose 1.62%, or 8.98 points, to 563.88, its highest level since March 2000,
when stocks were peaking.
The Russell 2000 is up 47% this year and is only 7% below the record high of 606.12 hit on March 9, 2000.
The Dow industrials, by contrast, remain more than 10% off their record close of 11722.98, and the Nasdaq
composite still isn't halfway back to its record.
Some money managers caution that stocks seem very likely to pull back at some point in the new year and
catch their breath.
"We are going to be bounded by the laws of gravity at some point here," said Robert Morris, chief investment
officer at Jersey City, N.J., fund-management group Lord Abbett. "But I don't want to be prematurely
pessimistic here. The economy will be there to support the earnings, which is the prime assumption behind
these gains."

--- Page 60 ---
Page 60 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Morris's expectation, he said, is that stocks will continue to advance, but that some of the more
established stocks such as International Business Machines, Microsoft and General Electric could start to
take over the lead from the smaller, more volatile stocks.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.87%, or 1.23 points, to 143.01.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.01 billion shares traded, 2,507 stocks rose and just 768 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 24/32, or $7.50 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, rose to 4.248%. The 30-year bond was down 1 4/32 to yield 5.047%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 107.04 yen, down from 107.52, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.2486 from $1.2429.

Iraq: Open for Business!
Iraq: Open for Business!
By Kamel Al-Gailani
771 words
29 December 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BAGHDAD -- While the capture of Saddam Hussein has been greeted with overwhelming joy and relief by
Iraq's people, living conditions for average Iraqis are still difficult. They want to see urgent improvements in
their lives. Iraq is a potentially rich country which has been driven into extreme poverty by Saddam and his
regime. The Baathists destroyed the economy through an appalling combination of centralized control,
mismanagement, corruption, cronyism, war, and a massive debt burden. Since 1989 our GDP has declined
by almost two-thirds and the corresponding decline in living standards has brought suffering to almost every
family. The best way to improve the lives of the Iraqi people is to unleash the potential of the Iraqis.
Together with colleagues in the Governing Council and ministries, we have developed a program of
far-reaching economic and financial reforms that will provide the foundations for a revitalized economy. The
new Iraq will have an open and free market economy characterized by good governance, equality of
opportunity, transparency, accountability and the rule of law. We want to accelerate Iraq's re-entry into the
global economy while building domestic institutions that are based on international standards and best
practices.
The first phase of our new economic program includes several key planks: establishing a friendly climate for
foreign investment, strengthening the financial sector, and setting low taxes and tariffs. Iraq will now allow
foreign investors ownership in any sector. The only two exceptions are the extraction of natural resources and
real estate, which international investors can lease for up to 40 years. Foreign companies are permitted to
have direct ownership, joint ventures, or branches as they see fit. We will guarantee equality of legal standing
for all foreign firms as well as full and immediate remittance of profits, dividends, interest, and royalties. The
investment process will be quick and clear with no bureaucratic hurdles to get in the way.
We are working to strengthen and deregulate Iraq's financial sector. The two main Iraqi financial institutions,
the Rafidain Bank and Rasheed Bank, will be modernized and made internationally competitive. Smaller
private banks will be required to strengthen balance sheets or find partners. Regulatory criteria for the
financial sector are being brought up to international standards. The Central Bank of Iraq has for the first
time become a truly independent institution and a new Trade Bank of Iraq, which will be managed by an
international consortium of banks led by JP Morgan Chase, will finance imports and exports. Foreign banks
are now welcome to enter Iraq and the first two licences will be issued early in 2004.
Corporate and personal taxes will start at 3% and rise in a progressive scale to a maximum of 15% and tariffs
will be capped at 5% across the board except for food, medicine, clothing and books, which will be exempt.
Some Iraqis have expressed fears that they will find themselves at a competitive disadvantage in a newly
opened marketplace. It will not be easy to escape the ill effects of four decades of socialist misrule and
Baathist corruption and 13 years of sanctions; however, we intend to help Iraqis compete on an equal basis.
The biggest impediments that Iraqi businesses currently face are lack of capital, international contacts, and
expertise. We are putting in place remedies in all these areas. We also plan to help the Iraqi people weather
the adjustment through programs of employment generation, retraining, and a social safety net for the most
vulnerable. We will not let Iraqis suffer at the expense of an idealized free market. The very purpose of
opening our economy is to let Iraqis prosper by their own hard work and talents.
Iraq is opening to the world for business but at the same time we caution foreign investors and exporters not
see Iraq merely as a new bonanza. The firms that will prosper are not those with a gold-rush mentality but
rather those who come to build long-term relationships. We do not welcome those who plan to put 30 Iraqi
firms out of business by setting up here, as one potential investor recently said. We welcome partners who
can show that they will contribute to our national priorities of productive investment, job creation, technology
and knowledge transfer, and strengthening the non-oil sectors.

--- Page 62 ---
Page 62 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
There are doubtless still problems for international investors in areas like security, services and
communications, but the situation is improving weekly. Iraq is emerging from its long nightmare and the Iraqi
people are ready to do business.
---
Mr. Al-Gailani is Iraq's minister of finance.
