Many Traders See Rise in Volatility In the New Year
Options Report
Many Traders See Rise in Volatility In the New Year
By Kopin Tan Dow Jones Newswires
497 words
29 December 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C11
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- As 2003 draws to a close, investors have much to be thankful for, with stocks at 19-month
highs and the markets calm. But option traders looking ahead say this sleepy lull might not extend too far into
the new year.
With the Chicago Board Options Exchange volatility index, or VIX, near seven-year lows, many traders have
made instinctive predictions in recent weeks for volatility -- which is a big driver of option prices -- to stir. That
hasn't happened yet as the option market's forecast of short-term risk has stayed muted. "We've had a good
run-up and people think everything is great, and that's one reason why volatility is so low," said Scott
Fullman, chief strategist at Investec US Inc.
But while some traders say volatility and option premiums can still decline further if stocks continue their rally,
most expect volatility to eventually pick up sometime next year.
Among the macro factors that can drive up volatility and traders' willingness to pay for option protection: the
uncertainty of an election year, less forgiving expectations as stock prices grow richer, and the anticipated
tightening of monetary policy. Other things -- like the potential impact of a weakening dollar and the threat of
terrorism -- also weigh on investors' minds and will keep option premiums from subsiding too drastically.
The answers differ widely when traders are asked when volatility might increase substantially: In the first
quarter. In the second half. When interest rates rise. Not before the elections.
"I see a 15% run-up in the Standard &Poor's 500 in the first half of the year, and then would start to look for a
correction," said Vincent McCrudden, a managing director of equity derivatives at the institutional
broker-dealer Pali Capital. That ought to coincide with option volatility "being at multiyear lows, and which
should start to pick up around mid-year."
Peter Dunay, Wall Street Access' chief market strategist, said stocks that had seen much price movement are
likely to remain volatile. For instance, chip companies and gold miners whose prices have fluctuated recently
or pushed to 52-week highs aren't likely to stop moving abruptly; they either will continue to push higher or
retrace their rise, possibly benefiting directional investors who hold the appropriate puts and calls. Mr.
McCrudden said he believes energy, and metals and mining, are some sectors in which option volatility could
increase.
Meanwhile, trading volume was light in Friday's holiday-shortened session. Investors bidding up index puts
for portfolio insurance sent the VIX up 0.79 to 17.45 even as stocks closed higher. Among investors' possible
concerns were economic data showing declining durable-goods orders and the discovery of mad-cow
disease in the U.S. The VIX, also called the fear gauge, increased 6.3% last week but remains near its lowest
level since late 1996.

Rapid Reversal: Once a Breadbasket, Zimbabwe Today Can't Feed Itself --- Politics, Drought, AIDS Bring A Severe Food Shortage; Aid Is Coming...
Rapid Reversal: Once a Breadbasket, Zimbabwe Today Can't Feed Itself --- Politics, Drought, AIDS
Bring A Severe Food Shortage; Aid Is Coming Up Short --- Caterpillars Become a Staple
By Roger Thurow
2,129 words
24 December 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
PUPU, Zimbabwe -- There will be no traditional Christmas goat roasting on a spit here this year, and no
Christmas chickens, either. The prospect of Christmas beer dried up long ago, along with the supplies of
sorghum used for brewing. The big holiday helpings of corn meal will be smaller than usual, for corn, the
nation's staple food, is the scarcest commodity of all.
"We don't have enough food to really celebrate this year," says Luka Philip Ngwenya. "Christmas will just
come and go like any other day."
For most people, that will mean a small ration of corn meal, supplemented with the roasted Mopani
caterpillars and dried wild fruit that have helped keep villagers alive for the past couple of months.
Mr. Ngwenya, a 63-year-old peasant farmer, stretched out under a dying Msasa tree in one of the hungriest
places in one of the hungriest countries in the world. He waited patiently, along with hundreds of his
neighbors, for the monthly distribution of food from the U.N.'s World Food Program and the aid organization,
World Vision. On that day, 4,008 people were fed -- 70% of the local population. Throughout Zimbabwe,
international humanitarian agencies are gearing up to feed more than six million people, which is more than
half of the entire nation. That makes Zimbabwe, proportionally at least, the neediest recipient of food aid in
the world.
The feeding of such multitudes is a surprising sight in a country that several years ago was selling up to
500,000 metric tons of surplus food to the WFP for distribution to starving people elsewhere. Now, it receives
500,000 tons of food aid. Zimbabwe today is home to many of the same factors, natural and man-made, that
are propelling an increase in the number of hungry people world-wide -- even though more food is being
produced than ever before.
A scorching drought has taken a toll here. But the situation has been exacerbated by politics. President
Robert Mugabe, in the face of rising opposition, pushed a fast-track land reform that confiscated white-owned
commercial farms and redistributed the property to loyal supporters of his Zanu-PF party. Many of the new
owners were inexperienced in running large agribusinesses and food production has fallen dramatically.
In Christmases past, as recently as four or five years ago, when Zimbabwe was a breadbasket of Africa, this
was a time of communal feasting and Christian celebration. But now, most families don't have enough for
themselves, let alone for sharing. Rampant food shortages and an inflation rate soaring toward 700% have
made goats and chickens too expensive to eat.
"Even if we have a chicken for a special meal, it is better to sell it for money to buy other food," says Lahlekile
Mpofu, a skinny, elderly woman who opens up a ragged plastic bag to receive her ration of split peas.
She and others came to this distribution last week hoping there might be a Christmas present of a bit more
food than usual. Instead, organizers announced they would actually be getting less, as the standard ration of
corn meal was cut in half to 11 pounds. The pipeline of international food aid into the country is growing thin,
the hungry were told, so corn-meal rations were reduced this month to make supplies stretch until the next
harvest in April or May.
"What can we do? We have no choice," Mrs. Mpofu says with a shrug. "Half a loaf is better than none."
The drought is into its third year in some rural places such as Pupu. HIV/AIDS has stricken one-third of
Zimbabwe's adults and is devastating the ranks of productive farm workers. So many people are dying that
one of the few thriving industries is funerals. The county's largest funeral company announced last week that
it would be opening five new parlors around the country, prompting speculation that it might even seek a
listing on the Zimbabwe Stock Exchange.

--- Page 82 ---
Page 82 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Today only a couple of hundred of the 4,500 confiscated farms are still fully functioning. Harvests of food
staples plummeted by as much as 90%, livestock herds dwindled and production of the main cash crop,
tobacco, slumped badly.
The resulting dearth of foreign currency has caused shortages of seed, fertilizer and fuel, which in turn have
led to a drop in production on the peasant farms. Unemployment, which has soared to 70%, combines with
the inflation rate to make whatever food is available too expensive for most of the population. A goat now
costs as much as the equivalent of $200, which would nearly consume a teacher's monthly salary.
Human-rights groups have charged that the ruling party has doled out food that is being produced locally in
exchange for electoral support.
In October, the U.S.-based Human Rights Watch released a report documenting examples of residents being
forced to display a Zanu-PF party membership card before being given some government grain. Those that
didn't went hungry, the group says.
"Zimbabwe stands alone as how one person can ruin a country," says Tony Hall, the U.S. ambassador to the
U.N.'s Food and Agriculture Organization and the WFP. Mr. Mugabe, he says, "has committed crimes against
humanity."
Mr. Mugabe has dismissed such criticism, as well as the financial and travel sanctions imposed by the U.S.
and Europe against him, as the actions of rich, white nations that don't want to see a black African country
succeed. His government rarely issues visas to foreign correspondents, branding them agents of Western
critics. Reporters who do slip into the country must report clandestinely and are unable to question
government officials. In the past, Mr. Mugabe, who led Zimbabwe to independence in 1980, has blamed his
troubles on former colonial master Britain for not supporting land reform earlier. Britain has said that Mr.
Mugabe corrupted the process by rewarding the party faithful.
Addressing a party conference earlier this month, Mr. Mugabe defended his government's seizure of
commercial farms for redistribution to black Zimbabweans. "Our people are overjoyed, the land is ours," he
told his followers, according to local press reports of the conference. "We are now the rulers and owners of
Zimbabwe."
January to April are the toughest months in Zimbabwe as food stocks from the previous spring's harvest are
exhausted. During that period earlier this year, the international community fed seven million of Zimbabwe's
12 million people. Now, the numbers are inching up to that mark again, as hunger also takes hold among
unemployed urban residents and the resettled farmers.
At the same time, the pipeline of food aid coming into the country is getting thinner. After two years of sending
food to Iraq and Afghanistan, as well as Ethiopia and other African nations, donor fatigue seems to be setting
in. Humanitarian-aid agencies are finding donations aren't keeping pace with all the competing demands.
In Zimbabwe, the crisis has deteriorated rapidly. While neighboring countries such as Zambia and Malawi,
which were also stricken by drought, have recovered as the weather has improved, Zimbabwe, with its
political and economic turmoil, hasn't.
In December, the WFP had enough cereals, mainly corn meal, to meet only 49% of estimated requirements,
which is why it went to half-rations. By March, given current donor pledges and scheduled shipments, it
estimates it will be able to meet only 25% of cereal requirements and 19% of staples such as beans and
peas. It takes about two months for food donations to be shipped to Zimbabwe, and at the moment, there is
little scheduled to arrive in April. The WFP is trying to generate more donations of food or money for
Zimbabwe. If it doesn't, rations will continue to get smaller to stretch out the food that is in the pipeline.
This puts the U.S. in the quandary of responding to a humanitarian crisis in a country led by a government it
has stridently criticized, somewhat like the situation in North Korea. Even though the U.S. has pushed to
isolate the Mugabe government, it has sent aid to the country. Since 2002, the U.S. has donated 437,000
metric tons of food to Zimbabwe.
One group that hasn't received much international aid so far are farmers on the resettled lands in Zimbabwe.
International aid agencies say some large donors have been reluctant to have their money and food go to
these farmers because the donors don't want to support Mr. Mugabe's land program. The government, in
turn, hasn't cooperated with aid agencies seeking to do a needs survey on those lands.
The U.S. and the European Union, the two largest donors of food to Zimbabwe, say they are monitoring the
situation there and will continue to respond to humanitarian need.
Next year's harvest may not offer much relief. Even if the weather cooperates, the corn production in
Zimbabwe isn't expected to exceed this year's harvest of about 800,000 tons. Already, the international aid
community in the U.S. and Europe is penciling in a 1 million-ton deficit for 2004.

--- Page 83 ---
Page 83 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In the western area of the country around Pupu, the outlook is bleak. The rains are late, seeds are scarce and
many animals once used for plowing have died from a lack of food and water. The yield from last April's
meager harvest began running out in August, forcing many people to survive on tea, wild fruit and the Mopani
caterpillars, which in good times are eaten as a snack, not as sustenance. The WFP and World Vision, which
fed this area during the lean months last year, returned again in November.
"If they didn't come back, people would be dying," says Siphatisiwe Ncube while waiting in line for food.
Field monitors of international aid agencies report scattered cases of desperate farmers eating their seeds
rather than waiting for optimal planting conditions. Under the Msasa tree in Pupu, Mr. Ngwenya and a dozen
other peasant farmers say they haven't resorted to that.
But are they tempted?
"Oh, yes," they shout in unison.
(MORE)
While the people waited patiently under a scorching noonday sun for their rations of corn, split peas and
cooking oil, the village dogs bided their time too. When the last bag of food aid had been carried away, the
dogs moved in to lick up any of the corn meal or peas that fell to the ground and escaped the brooms of the
sweepers.
"This is really a sign of hunger," says Robinah Mulenga, a WFP official at the distribution as she watched two
brown dogs lap up stray corn meal. "African dogs usually eat what is left over from the family meals. But now
nothing is left over, so even the dogs are hungry."
Other signs of growing desperation noted by aid workers in the country: men leaving their drought-choked
farms to pan for gold, women heading to the cities to work as prostitutes, young people sneaking across the
border to find work in the neighboring countries, particularly South Africa.
On food distribution day, the parched soccer field of the Pupu primary school was covered with hundreds of
bags filled with food from all over the world. From one of the goalposts hung a sign explaining the rations: 11
pounds of corn per person, 4 pounds of peas, and 3 pounds of the corn-soya blend.
Another banner posted at a corner of the field said: "Food provided to the people of Zimbabwe by WFP in
collaboration with World Vision to restore hope, alleviate suffering and save lives."
"This is just about food. People must come in normal clothes, no political T-shirts," said Zvidzai Maburutse,
World Vision's deputy director of relief in Zimbabwe. Last year, aid agencies suspended distributions in other
parts of the country when local politicians tried to turn the handouts to their own benefit.
At this distribution, 35 tons of food was handed out without incident. Mrs. Ncube, barefoot and in a blue dress,
walked two hours to collect her share, but she worries it won't be enough to feed a family of 11 for a month.
As she waited her turn, she chewed on a small piece of dried wild fruit. "It gives you energy for several
hours," she said.
What is her Christmas wish? "More corn meal, please," she says.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Brutality in Zimbabwe" -- WSJ Jan. 2, 2004)

Hedge Funds' Latest Risky Play: Commodities
Hedge Funds' Latest Risky Play: Commodities
By Henny Sender and Peter A. McKay
1,156 words
23 December 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
HARD ASSETS ARE hot, and investors are betting on everything from gas to gold.
The reason: Commodities do well when economies world-wide are recovering -- or booming, as is the case in
China. Demand rises. The falling dollar has helped increase prices.
But hedge funds -- investment vehicles for the wealthy -- have also been pouring money into commodities
this year, adding to demand, helping to drive prices higher and increasing volatility. This speculative buying,
aided by low interest rates that make borrowing cheap, is adding another variable to the supply/demand
equation.
"The hedge funds recognize that this is the best reflation play. They got on the bandwagon early," says Neal
Shear, a managing director at Morgan Stanley. "They are having a big impact."
The Dow Jones-AIG Index of 20 commodities is up 21.4% for the year, despite yesterday's 2.1% dip to
133.889. The Reuters-CRB Futures Index, which comprises 17 commodities, is up 9.5% for the year, despite
a 1.6% fall yesterday to 256.79. The Dow index's sharper rise is largely because it gives more weight to more
actively traded commodities.
"You have to have revolutionary price movements to make money" in commodities, says Neal Berger,
founder of Aquila Capital Partners LP, a $100 million fund set up last year that devotes 15% of its assets to
commodities. With so much turbulence in the world, he believes that is now the case. "The climate is now
very fertile for trading commodities," he says.
Yesterday's natural-gas trading was a prime example of a volatile market in which investors could quickly win
or lose big money on a single commodity. Natural-gas futures plunged 9.4%, or 65.8 cents, to $6.324 per
million British thermal units, due to mild weather and large stockpiles.
While many investors believe the major run-ups in commodity prices are over, they say money can still be
made because the markets are likely to stay volatile. By betting that prices will go up or down in the short run,
commodities traders can still rack up big gains.
In the 1990s, hedge funds focused first on hot new stock offerings and then distressed debt. But in the past
few years, new funds such as Ospraie Management and Catequil Asset Management have raised over $1
billion each to focus on commodity trading and are already closed to new investors. Other established funds,
including those led by Paul Tudor Jones and George Soros, are allocating sizable portions of their assets to
commodities after having ignored them for years. Some of the money is even coming from usually
conservative pension funds and college endowments, managers of the funds say.
Willem Kooyker, chairman of Blenheim Capital Management LLC of Somerset, N.J, whose fund invests
largely in commodities and is up 26% this year through October, is so convinced that this is the time for
investors to buy that he hired two marketing staffers to get the message out.
Some investors are discovering how treacherous the commodities markets can be. Much of the betting is
done with borrowed money, meaning the potential for losses -- as well as gains -- is large.
One recent casualty is the DKR Commodity Arbitrage Fund, based in Stamford, Conn. DKR closed its U.S.
DKR Commodity Arbitrage Fund over the summer and wound down its offshore fund last month.
In a statement, DKR blamed poor performance and significant redemptions in 2003. DKR's assets fell to
$105.5 million at of the end of October, down almost 78% from $470 million at the start of the year, according
to data from HSBC.

--- Page 85 ---
Page 85 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Dealers say DKR made a huge bet on cocoa and then a classic mistake. Its position was so large that DKR,
in effect, became the market. The fund was unable to unwind the trade and lost big as a result. A DKR
spokesman says the fund was managed by Framework Investment Group. Carol O'Donnell, Framework's
general counsel, declined to comment.
Dealers and fund executives say DKR went into 2003 with big positions in cocoa-one reason for large gains
last year. In late January and early February, cocoa prices rose to near 17-year highs because of political
unrest in the Ivory Coast, the world's leading cocoa producer. But Ivorian politics have since stabilized, and
the West African country seems headed toward a larger-than-expected cocoa harvest of at least one million
metric tons, about 25% higher than analysts' earlier projections. Year to date, cocoa futures have plummeted
23.3% to $1,551 a metric ton on the Coffee, Sugar and Cocoa Exchange division of the New York Board of
Trade.
Both the Comex and the London Metal Exchange are experiencing record levels of trading in the futures
contracts, or agreements to buy or sell a given commodity at some time in the future. For example, at Comex,
a division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, gold and copper futures set monthly volume records in
November. Gold futures volume totaled 1,397,296 contracts, breaking a six-year-old record. And copper
futures volume totaled 363,865 contracts, breaking a two-year-old mark.
Nymex President J. Robert Collins Jr. attributed the surge to "the growing interest in metals markets
generally."
Funds that specialize in trading these contracts, known as commodity trading advisers, are experiencing brisk
inflows, says Sol Waksman, president of the research firm Barclay Trading Group, in Fairfield, Iowa.
Industrywide, Mr. Waksman said that money in managed futures accounts is up 45% to $75 billion this year,
including both inflows and trading profits, though many players aren't placing bets only on commodity prices.
For example, Graham Capital Management, of Stamford, Conn., has almost doubled its assets under
management this year to about $4.5 billion, including close to $600 million in commodities, say people
familiar with the firm. Graham founder Kenneth G. Tropin says the firm's bets in the industrial metals and
gold market have turned out particularly well this year.
Hedge funds don't usually hold positions for a long time. But they can amplify price moves. Some observers
attribute much of the recent jump in the prices of gold, oil and platinum to the funds.
They can also push prices down. Copper for example, was up 35% for the year to a six-year high before
plunging the third week in November in the biggest weekly drop since March. In two weeks in November, the
number of contracts on copper on the Comex dropped almost 30%, according to data from Morgan Stanley.
"You cannot tell me that all that demand was from consumers in the U.S. or in China," says Fabio Savoldelli,
a managing director at the Merrill Lynch Investment Managers unit of Merrill Lynch &Co. "A lot is speculative.
It can turn very suddenly."

Worries About the Dollar Enter Stock Radar Screens
Abreast of the Market
Worries About the Dollar Enter Stock Radar Screens
By E.S. Browning
1,330 words
22 December 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ORDINARILY, IF YOU ASK a professional stock investor what worries him, he will give you one of two
answers: "earnings," or "interest rates."
Simplistic as it seems, those two indicators typically determine whether stocks rise or fall.
But these days, you might get a different answer to the question. Earnings and interest rates are looking
good. Instead, people are worrying about something that isn't usually on their radar screens: the dollar.
The dollar? Sure, it has lost about one-third of its value against the euro in the past three years, and
somewhat less against other currencies. It has hit record lows against the euro on 11 of the past 16 trading
days -- although it rose a tad on Friday and still isn't far from where it was when the euro was launched in
1999.
For many analysts, this is no cause for worry. In fact, it is good. It means that American multinationals can
charge lower prices for U.S. goods abroad, and it means that their foreign earnings are worth more when
translated back into dollars. That helps profits. A falling dollar is inflationary, but with inflation so low, some
economists consider mild inflation good.
"Erosion in the dollar and lower interest rates are good for U.S. companies short term," says David Briggs,
head of stock trading at Federated Investors. "But that is not a game you can play indefinitely." Mr. Briggs is
fairly optimistic on the stock outlook, but he notes that if the dollar falls sharply next year, it could hurt
confidence and send inflation up too fast, and that wouldn't be good for stocks.
Market analyst Ned Davis of Ned Davis Research in Venice, Fla., also calls himself "cautiously optimistic" on
stocks, but, he adds, "I worry that the dollar decline, while bullish if controlled, could turn into a dollar
collapse, which could cause foreigners to dump bonds, sending bond yields soaring, like they do in every
currency crisis."
That kind of disruption and loss of confidence would be bad for stocks. Mr. Davis has gone so far as to
compare the dollar trend against a basket of currencies in recent years to the trend leading up to late 1987,
when the floundering dollar helped trigger the stock-market crash. The two patterns are eerily similar.
Many things have changed since 1987, of course. Interest rates are much lower today and international
currency management arguably is more sophisticated. Few people are predicting a repeat of 1987. Mr. Davis
certainly isn't. He is urging clients to stick with stocks.
And stocks certainly aren't looking troubled. Last week, the Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 236.06 points
overall, or 2.35%, to 10278.22, a 19-month high. That marked its fourth consecutive week of gains, and
included a gain of 30.14 points, or 0.29%, on Friday. The Dow industrials are up 23% this year and the
Nasdaq Composite Index is up 46%, both headed for their first "up years" since 1999.
Analysts widely expect those gains to continue next year, at least into the first part of the year. But even
among the optimists, the dollar is near the top of their lists of "Things That Could Go Wrong in 2004."
"No one can say at what point it becomes a crisis or at what level," says Jon Brorson, head of growth-stock
investing at mutual-fund group Neuberger Berman. Mr. Brorson has been watching the bond market for early
signs that the dollar is rattling investors, and so far, he isn't seeing any.
"I just recall 1987, and that summer it seemed that the United States was losing control of the dollar," he
says. "Things are always different, of course. But by the same token, I bet the currency markets today are by
a factor of three, four or five larger than they were. The currency flow is susceptible to 24-hour trading and
susceptible to emotions and confidence."

--- Page 92 ---
Page 92 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
So, Mr. Brorson says, he will continue watching the bond market for early signs of disruptions.
In practical terms, a falling dollar could damage financial markets in two ways. First, it could make foreign
investors less eager to own U.S. securities. A foreign pullback from U.S. securities could weigh on stock
prices, as happened in the summer of 2002. And it could make it hard to finance huge and growing U.S. trade
deficits.
"We definitely have begun to see a decline in the rate of foreign investment" in U.S. securities, notes
economist Rebecca McCaughrin, who follows international money flows for New York brokerage firm Morgan
Stanley.
The U.S. needs to attract roughly $50 billion a month in net foreign purchases of dollars to finance its foreign
deficit, Ms. McCaughrin says. But the average level of foreign buying was $28.5 billion a month in September
and October (the most recent months for which data are available) -- down from $65.5 billion a month over
the period from January through August.
The September flow of money into dollar-denominated securities, in fact, was the lowest since 1998.
Meanwhile, American investors are spending more on foreign securities, exacerbating the problem.
The biggest foreign holders of U.S. Treasury bonds are the Japanese and Chinese, and the biggest foreign
holders of U.S. stocks are the Europeans. Now they face a problem. With the dollar falling, they need to
hedge their holdings. But if they sell heavily, they drive down the dollar even faster.
Oil producers face a similar conundrum. As long as oil is priced in dollars, they lose money when the dollar
falls. But if they suggest pricing in other currencies, they hurt the dollar even more.
This difficulty could help explain why gold, a classic hedge against dollar weakness, has been rising steadily.
It is in everyone's interest to keep the dollar up, but also in everyone's interest to shift to other currencies
since it is widely expected that the dollar will keep falling. That delicate balance stays in place only if
everyone plays along. If people sniff a serious decline and suddenly abandon their gentleman's agreement
and race out of the dollar, everyone suffers.
And that reflects the second aspect of the dollar problem: confidence. As long as the dollar decline is
considered orderly and limited, few will jump ship. But if confidence somehow is damaged and people fear a
run on the dollar, the fear could become self-fulfilling, and that would damage stock and bond prices.
"There definitely is the concern that if the dollar continues to weaken and there are sufficient opportunities
overseas, we won't be able to attract sufficient flows," Ms. McCaughrin says. "What could happen then? It
could trigger a crash in the dollar -- which isn't our scenario," she emphasizes.
The consensus scenario is for a gradual, orderly decline in the dollar.
"The probability of a meltdown scenario like we had [in 1987] isn't very high," says Chris Conkey, head of
stocks at Evergreen Investments, Wachovia's money-management unit. "A little bit of inflationary pressure at
the margin is what our economy needed. It is good. But the dollar is definitely a potential source of turmoil
because of the cash flows we need to finance deficits. It isn't impossible that it could become a problem next
year, but you would have to have a significant acceleration of the dollar decline for it to become a problem."

Red Hat, the Linux software distributor, surged $3.21, or 22%, to $17.49 after saying its fiscal third-quarter
net income rose from the year-earlier quarter as revenue climbed 36%.
McDonald's dropped 51 cents, or 2.1%, to 24.30. The McDonald's Japan unit nearly doubled its anticipated
loss for fiscal 2003 as it severed a longstanding management-consulting arrangement.
-- Cynthia Schreiber

Live-Cattle Futures Fall Sharply Amid Weaker Markets, Selling
Commodities Report
Live-Cattle Futures Fall Sharply Amid Weaker Markets, Selling
By Curt Thacker
480 words
19 December 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
CHICAGO -- Live-cattle futures on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange fell sharply amid weaker cash cattle
markets and selling from speculative funds.
Both the front-month December and most-active February contracts fell by their 1.50-cent limits -- the former
to 91.17 cents, the latter to 88.05.
Lower prices paid for cash cattle late Wednesday and early yesterday contributed heavily to the initial
declines in futures, floor traders and brokers said. After reports of sales of live cattle at 93 cents a pound sent
futures down, some packers then reduced their bids to 92 cents, so the weaker markets fed off one another,
the traders said.
The early losses were extended as futures prices fell to sell stops, or sales orders placed in advance.
February broke through the 50-day-moving-average -- a key level on price charts -- early in the session, then
later dropped through last week's low of 88.10. Those breaches fueled additional selling.
Dan Vaught, market analyst with A.G. Edwards, said the steep break in the nearby live-cattle contracts was
largely the result of weaker-than-expected prices in the cash cattle markets this week.
Cash-market sources said packers will need fewer cattle the next two weeks due to reduced slaughters
during the holidays. Also, the beef plants are trying to recoup from recent losses. Their per-head slaughtering
losses set new record daily and weekly average marks during the second week of this month.
Retail promotions of beef have declined significantly during the last few weeks after wholesale-beef prices
surged to all-time highs in October. Grocers could no longer afford to promote as much beef at the squeezed
margins so they switched to more pork and chicken instead. That resulted in slowed movement of beef and
depressed wholesale prices as well as demand for live cattle.
In other commodity markets:
COPPER: Futures rallied at the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange after news that
Freeport McMoRan Copper &Gold declared a force majeure on copper concentrate sales from its Grasburg
mine in Indonesia following an accident. Force majeure is a contractual clause absolving commodity
producers from delivery commitments in extenuating circumstances. Traders said the market was also helped
by gains in the stock market and by this week's solid U.S. economic data. March futures rose 1.85 cents a
pound to $1.0150, setting a six-year high of $1.0185.
WHEAT: Chicago Board of Trade futures rose after a late burst of speculative buying. News of strong weekly
export sales gave the market a boost at the open, but buying interest waned and the market succumbed to
the influence of weakness in the soybean market, before its final push higher. March futures rose 7.25 cents
to $3.9325 a bushel.

Hollywood Report: High Noon at HBO --- Bloody `Deadwood' Is Bid To End a Slow Streak; Cue the Man-Eating Pigs
Hollywood Report:
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Hollywood Report: High Noon at HBO --- Bloody `Deadwood' Is Bid To End a Slow Streak; Cue the
Man-Eating Pigs
By John Lippman
948 words
19 December 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
W3
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
HBO HAS SCORED some of its biggest hits by tapping the cutting edge of the zeitgeist, from "The Sopranos"
and its modern-day mobsters to those oh-so-now gals from "Sex and the City." Now, it's going back in time --
by more than 100 years, to the Old West.
That's the plan for "Deadwood," an expensive attempt by HBO to revive some of the buzz for its television
dramas. Set in a South Dakota frontier town in 1876, the $6 million pilot episode sets up a story of
fortune-hunters in the lawless West. And taking full advantage as usual of cable TV's "lawlessness," it won't
skimp on the sex and violence (the locus of Deadwood is its saloon and brothel). The man who created and
wrote the series is David Milch, co-creator of "NYPD Blue" and no slouch at pushing the creative envelope.
HBO thinks one of the reasons a western can catch on with viewers is precisely because the genre has been
so long out of favor. And the creative freedom that cable affords finally lets them make a western that has
more in common with brutal movies like "Unforgiven" than candified versions of the West as depicted in
"Bonanza" -- westerns' recent miserable record on TV notwithstanding.
The cable network, of course, needs a new hit. Finances are no problem at HBO -- profits are up 11% this
year to $900 million. HBO has racked up Emmys by the dozens and says short-term ratings reports leave it
cold. Instead, it relies on cachet -- lots of it. Chairman Chris Albrecht says he is always trying to maintain
"noise," a "must-have feeling" among subscribers.
But with "Sex and the City" heavily breathing into its last season next month, the network is down to a handful
of original, and aging, shows. New series "K Street" failed to find an audience, and "Carnivale" just barely
kept its tent for a second season. And while critics loved "Angels in America" (Al Pacino and Meryl Streep), a
less than heavenly 4.2 million viewers watched the debut of its first installment. If new "franchise" series keep
stalling, that could dig into the steady subscriber growth (up 15% since 1998).
Enter "Deadwood," mixing historical and fictional characters uneasily living side by side in the Gold Rush-era
town where Wild Bill Hickok was shot dead in a saloon by a man he beat at poker. Tony Soprano blasting
John Wayne full of holes? Not entirely. It's also a gloves-off version of "NYPD Blue." The town of Deadwood
is bursting with pornography, racial tension (especially with immigrant Chinese laborers), drug addiction,
prostitution and crime -- and dreams of the good life.
Besides the brothel -- expect to see HBO-level nudity and sexual behavior depicted in "raw fashion," Mr.
Milch says -- the town has its own Chinese ghetto where the bodies of those who trespass the unwritten law
of the West are fed to pigs. The characters use language among the most profane ever shown on the small
or big screen, with racial slurs used liberally, much as they would have been in the time. Codified laws?
Forget it. "There were a tremendous number of criminals," says Mr. Milch.
HBO hasn't forgotten the western's initial success on TV. In 1958, seven of the top 10 shows on the networks
were westerns, notes Robert Thompson, director of the Center for Popular Television at Syracuse University.
But, a decade later, the western hero's trademark machismo "seemed laughable," he says. On the big
screen, recent westerns have ridden triumphantly into theaters (Kevin Costner's "Open Range") and others,
like "The Missing," have just vanished.
Mr. Milch sees "Deadwood" as a way to tell the story of the birth of American law and capitalism. An avid
reader of western novels as a kid -- he rarely watched TV westerns or cop shows -- Mr. Milch vows to show
early American life without the whitewash.

--- Page 95 ---
Page 95 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
HBO has filled the show with less-than-household names, a habit at the channel. Ian McShane plays a
powerful saloon-keeper. In one scene, Mr. McShane's character is interrogating an opium addict who robbed
drug couriers before they could deliver a shipment to the town's Chinese dope dealer. Watching him force a
confession out of the bandit, terrifying him so that he loses control of bodily functions, is a bit like watching an
unfettered Lt. Sipowicz of "NYPD Blue": an example of what he might do if he operated free of the rules of
law . . . and network TV.
Fandango's Forecast
FOR AN INDUSTRY saturated with high tech, Hollywood has taken a surprisingly old-style approach to
predicting which movies will sink or swim at the box office, using telephone surveys or canvassing passersby
at shopping malls and street corners.
Until now. For the industry, the real potential in Fandango, the online movie ticket-purchasing Web site, may
lie in pre-opening weekend reports based on advance ticket sales. Owned by a group of theater owners,
Fandango has 10,500 screens "wired" for ticket sales, or about 30% of all screens in the country. The
company says it shares pre-opening data "in some cases" with the studios, but won't say which.
Based on ticket sales for next week, every movie out now might as well pull out and head for the film-stock
silver extraction plant. Fandango says 97% of its advance online ticket sales are for "Lord of the Rings: The
Return of the King."

'Tis Season of Cheer: Santa (Rally) Is Here, At the End of the Year
Thursday's Markets
'Tis Season of Cheer: Santa (Rally) Is Here, At the End of the Year
By E.S. Browning
628 words
19 December 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE MUCH-AWAITED Santa Claus rally seems to be on its way down the chimney.
For the second session in the past three, the Dow Jones Industrial Average rose more than 100 points,
pushing above 10200 to its highest finish in more than 19 months.
Yesterday's gain of 102.82 points, or 1.01%, to 10248.08, marked the blue chips' sixth session in a row above
10000 and their fifth gain in those six days. It was the first finish above 10200 since May of last year.
Stocks typically rise at year's end; the final few trading days of the year often are among the year's best, as
investors look forward to January inflows of money into retirement accounts, pension funds and the like.
Traders have dubbed this phenomenon the Santa Claus rally.
This year, that seasonal pattern was boosted by hopes for a strengthening economy, better corporate
earnings and continuing low interest rates, as well as a more stable world. Yesterday, a Federal Reserve
report on business activity in the Philadelphia region was better than expected. Leading indicators were
strong and new unemployment claims filed last week were the lowest in six weeks.
There were some clouds in the sky. The dollar fell to a record low against the euro, its fifth record low in a
row. Oil rose to a nine-month high amid fears that winter demand would be heavy. Treasury bonds also rose.
Gold, which has been hitting eight-year highs, fell a bit, however.
"I have clung to the idea that we were going to have a Santa Claus rally," said David Briggs, head of stock
trading at Pittsburgh mutual-fund group Federated Investors. "There has been an underlying current of cash
coming into the market. The economic numbers continue to reinforce it."
The week's gains have perhaps been exaggerated by today's "quadruple witching" expiration of options and
futures contracts. Some investors have bought stocks to cover options and futures positions. Some traders
worry that stocks might temporarily pull back once that artificial stimulus disappears.
Yesterday saw a resurgence in volatile technology stocks that had flagged after leading the gains for most of
this year.
The broad S&P 500 rose 1.18%, or 12.70 points, to 1089.18, now up 24% on the year and at a 19-month
high. The Dow is up 23% on the year.
But the Nasdaq Composite Index, heavy in tech stocks and up more than 46% on the year, isn't back to the
recent high it hit Dec. 1. It rallied yesterday, though -- up 1.81%, or 34.85 points, to 1956.18.
Analysts are debating whether the tech stocks will lead the market next year, or give way to other groups.
"Until the end of November, the one consistent feature of the rally off of the March lows was the leadership by
technology stocks," said Russ Koesterich, chief North American stock strategist at State Street Global
Markets, an arm of Boston financial group State Street Corp. "Over the past several weeks, that leadership
has been cracking." Transportation stocks, equipment makers and metals and mining stocks have taken the
lead recently, he said.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.55 billion shares traded, 2,445 stocks rose and 829 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 13/32, or $4.0625 for each $1,000 invested to push down the
yield to 4.129%.

--- Page 97 ---
Page 97 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The dollar was mixed. It traded at 107.55 yen, up from 107.42; the euro rose against the dollar to $1.2436
from $1.2403.

Dollar Hits Record Low Against Euro --- U.S. Currency Is Pounded By Report That Intervention By ECB Won't Come Soon
Currency Trading
Dollar Hits Record Low Against Euro --- U.S. Currency Is Pounded By Report That Intervention By
ECB Won't Come Soon
By Jamie McGeever Dow Jones Newswires
725 words
18 December 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C15
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- The dollar fell to historical lows against the euro, this time sent reeling by a report suggesting
intervention from the European Central Bank to halt the common currency's rapid rise is some ways off.
Quoting unidentified ECB sources, Market News, a financial-news service, said the bank won't consider
stepping into the market and selling euros until it trades as high as $1.35, and even then, only if the rise is
orderly. The ECB declined to comment on the report.
The market, which had been preparing for a relatively quiet session amid an absence of U.S. economic
indicators and maybe even a slight recovery in the dollar, was rocked from its slumber.
Eventually taking out stiff options-related resistance at $1.2400, the euro surged to a new high of $1.2420,
while sterling rocketed to a new 11-year peak of almost $1.77. Dealers reported aggressive buying from
Asian central banks and Middle Eastern accounts in particular.
The dollar also slumped to a new seven-year low against the Swiss franc but managed to advance on the
Australian and New Zealand dollars, which eased back after posting strong gains recently.
The dollar's decline coincided with falling bond yields and a rise in gold prices to fresh eight-year highs.
"The biggest message that came from [yesterday] is that there's a tolerance level . . . a pressure point . . . but
that hasn't been reached yet," said Tim Stewart, chief currency strategist at Morgan Stanley in New York.
"There's still more room on the [euro's] upside."
ECB chief economist Otmar Issing said that he is unconcerned with the strength of the euro, which has
appreciated 10% since the Group of Seven's September communique calling for more "flexibility" in exchange
rates.
"The current level of the exchange rate corresponds to its long-term average," Mr. Issing said in an interview
with Bloomberg Television, with his comments also reported on the news agency's Web site. "It is essentially
back where it started." The euro was introduced at about $1.17 on Jan. 1, 1999.
Following remarks last Friday from U.S. Treasury Secretary John Snow that the dollar's decline has so far
been orderly and that on a trade-weighted basis the dollar is as strong as at any time over the past 25 years,
it seems a one-way bet for traders to sell dollars and buy euros just now.
In late trading in New York Wednesday, the euro was at $1.2403, up from $1.2323 late Tuesday in New York,
and was also up against the yen at 133.25 yen from 132.52 yen.
The dollar was at 107.42 yen, only slightly lower than 107.52 yen late Tuesday in New York but that was
largely thanks to strong bids from Japanese banks which prevented a fall below 107 yen, dealers said. The
dollar was also at 1.2542 Swiss francs, down from 1.2601 francs Tuesday, while sterling was sharply up at
$1.7658 from $1.7541.
Market participants, mostly baffled by the reported comments from the ECB sources, said they were in effect
a green light to take the euro higher and sell the dollar lower.
"This surely risks triggering a disorderly rise in the euro, as traders are presented with a `one-way bet,' "
wrote ING Financial markets analysts in a research note. "It is hard to believe that the ECB wants an even
faster appreciation in the euro, which is likely to be the story's effect."

--- Page 99 ---
Page 99 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
All things being equal, the surging euro is effectively a tightening of monetary policy as it crimps external
demand, said Steven Englander, chief currency strategist for North America at Barclays capital in New York.
Meanwhile, the euro also got a lift from the Norwegian central bank's surprise decision to cut its benchmark
interest rates by 0.25 percentage point to 2.25%.
The narrowing rate differential with the euro zone -- where key interest rates are 2% -- helped the euro to
spike up to two-month high of 8.3230 kroner, sharply up from around 8.2260 kroner late Tuesday in New
York.

Dow Industrials Jump 106 Points To Cross 10100
Tuesday's Markets
Dow Industrials Jump 106 Points To Cross 10100
By E.S. Browning
623 words
17 December 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SADDAM HUSSEIN'S weekend capture didn't do it, but strong economic numbers did: The good economic
news yesterday pushed the Dow Jones Industrial Average above 10100, to its highest finish in nearly 19
months.
The news took many investors by surprise. November housing starts surged past two million, the highest
monthly figure since 1984. Industrial production posted its biggest monthly gain since 1999, rising at twice
October's pace. Businesses used some more of the excess production capacity that has been weighing them
down. The consumer price index actually declined in November.
The Hussein capture made investors feel good, but it didn't address their worries about the economic outlook.
That helps explain why some investors took profits on Monday, sending stocks down.
Yesterday's economic news, however, was exactly what people wanted. It suggested that economic
indicators that are central to the economy -- consumer spending and business investment -- remain strong.
And that strength isn't creating inflation, which means that low interest rates, which have been the main
juice powering the economic gains, may stay low for months to come.
"Those numbers were much stronger than expected," said Todd Clark, head of listed trading at Wells Fargo
Securities. Traders remained suspicious of stocks during much of the morning, because stocks had finished
weakly on Monday and people feared another pullback. But as the day wore on and stocks managed to move
ahead, more people began to buy, "and that's why the thing kind of levitated late in the day," Mr. Clark said.
The Dow industrials rose 106.74, or 1.06%, to 10129.56. The blue-chip index is up 21% so far this year, at
their highest level since May of last year.
Gold fell a bit, and the dollar hit another record low against Europe's young common currency, the euro,
which has existed only since 1999.
Treasury bonds rose following the favorable inflation news, because low inflation means that interest rates
may remain low longer than some pessimists had feared.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index hit its own 19-month high, rising 0.66%, or 7.09 points, to 1075.13, up
22% on the year.
The volatile Nasdaq Composite Index, which has led the stock gains for most of this year, rose more
modestly and didn't return to its recent highs. Some money managers have been selling some of this year's
big winners in order to book profits and lock in some gains.
The Nasdaq composite still rose 0.31%, or 6.03 points, to 1924.29, and is up 44% this year.
Trading volume was a bit heavier than in recent days, and some traders attributed some of the day's activity
to extra buying and selling due to the monthly expiration of options and futures contracts at the end of this
week, which they said may have exaggerated the market's upswing.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 0.68%, or 0.95 point, to 139.15.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.51 billion shares traded, 1,947 stocks rose and 1,360 fell.

Stocks Retreat As Early Rally Over Iraq Fades --- Blue Chips Drop 19.34 Points As Investors Turn Attention To Market Fundamentals
Monday's Markets
Stocks Retreat As Early Rally Over Iraq Fades --- Blue Chips Drop 19.34 Points As Investors Turn
Attention To Market Fundamentals
By E.S. Browning
1,284 words
16 December 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE STOCK MARKET celebrated Saddam Hussein's capture -- for a few hours.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average was up nearly 100 points a few minutes after the opening bell yesterday
morning. By late afternoon, investors had turned their attention back to market fundamentals, and as they
reflected on those, they sold stocks. They found themselves worrying whether the Dow can stay above 10000
-- a level it hit last week for the first time in 18 months. Investors are painfully aware that the industrials have
repeatedly found it impossible to hold the 10000 mark, which the Dow first crossed in 1999.
The Dow industrials finished down 19.34 points, or 0.19%, at 10022.82. It was the first down day for stocks
since the industrials pushed through 10000 on Thursday -- the 19th time the index had broken through the
10000 barrier and closed above there.
On each of the 18 preceding occasions, stocks have lost momentum and fallen back. And already, stocks are
moving the wrong way this time as well. Given all the past fizzles, money managers are painfully aware that
there is no guarantee that stocks will remain above 10000, and now that the year is almost over, some are
hedging their bets.
With the industrial average up more than 20% this year, some professional money managers, eager to show
big gains in year-end reports, are selling some stocks and booking some gains.
Some of biggest stock losers, both yesterday and in recent days, were speculative technology stocks such as
computer-chip makers, which are among those that have posted the biggest gains. Those are the kinds of
stocks that money managers are selling to book the gains. Chip makers as a group began the day by moving
upward, but finished down nearly 3%.
"There was an aberration on the opening with the Saddam news, and then it was back to reality," said Robert
Harrington, co-head of U.S. stock trading at UBS's brokerage arm in Stamford, Conn. "Iraq is going to be a
long-term endeavor." Right now, he added, Iraq simply isn't the main market driver. Investors are more
worried about retail-sales prospects, the effect of the dollar on various companies, and whether
computer-chip stocks have gotten ahead of themselves.
On those counts, the news was mixed. Retailers suffered during the day after Wal-Mart Stores said
December sales are running on the low end of its expectations. Wal-Mart itself fell more than 3% on the day.
But investors got some positive news after regular trading hours that could affect stocks today. Oracle
announced better-than-expected quarterly profits, and that stock rose 2.2% in after-hours trading.
In the many hours before regular trading ended in the U.S., however, investors saw euphoria over the
Saddam Hussein news gradually run its course. In fact, the reaction to Mr. Hussein's capture seemed to lose
momentum as it traveled around the world. Tokyo's Nikkei stock index celebrated, rising 3.2%, but European
indexes jumped less, and the Dow industrials, even at their high, never were up even 1%.
The dollar followed the same pattern -- up before U.S. markets opened, and then sliding backward during the
day until it showed a decline in late New York trading. Gold and Treasury bonds also pulled back slightly. Oil
prices, down at one point during the day on hopes that Iraqi oil production would rise, finished the day slightly
higher.
Back in the days before the Iraq war, and immediately after the war's start, worries about Iraq weighed
heavily on stocks. As investors agonized in February and early March over the risk of chemical warfare and
heavy casualties, stocks sank. But then, hopes for a quick victory helped to spark the resumption of the stock
gains in March.

--- Page 103 ---
Page 103 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
By summer, to some people's surprise, Iraq had become yesterday's news and investors were turning their
attention squarely to the economy. Despite continuing U.S. casualties, investors focused on the sharp
earnings gains that seemed to lie in store, and stocks surged around the globe.
Now, investors are dealing with the flip side of that euphoria. They are asking whether 10000 will serve as a
ceiling for stocks or as a new floor, and wondering how soon the pace of earnings gains will begin to wane.
"The Saddam Hussein news is great from a humanitarian standpoint," said Jon Brorson, head of growth-stock
investing at mutual-fund group Neuberger Berman. "But in our $40 trillion economy, I don't think it makes that
much of a difference." Investors are a lot more interested in whether November consumer-price data, due
today, indicate that inflation remains tame, he said.
Investors also noted that, while the capture of Mr. Hussein could make Iraq more stable, it won't remove the
more fundamental concerns about terrorism and Islamic extremism that have weighed on stocks since Sept.
11.
The day's biggest drops came in technology stocks, which are heavily represented in the Nasdaq Composite
Index. The Nasdaq composite fell 1.58%, or 30.74 points, to 1918.26. It still is up 44% this year, but with
investors now cashing in some of their recent paper profits in Nasdaq stocks, the Nasdaq index is moving
away from the 2000 mark that it seemed ready to cross just a few days ago.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.57%, or 6.10 points, to 1068.04, still up 21% on the year.
Stocks seemed on track to post gains for a good part of the day, despite the waning of the Saddam rally. One
positive for stocks was a comment from Alfred Broaddus, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of
Richmond, Va., who reiterated recent Fed indications that the central bank hasn't any plans to raise its target
interest rates soon. In a speech in North Carolina, he said he expects the economy to grow at an annualized
4% rate in the current quarter, and that low levels of inflation don't require any Fed action.
In addition, a survey of business activity in the New York region was strong.
But that didn't stop investors from selling stocks.
"Saddam's capture is certainly a big positive for the U.S.-led operation and potentially positive for the
longer-term stability of Iraq," said Harvey Hirschhorn, head of asset-allocation and strategy for Columbia
Management Group, a money-management arm of banking group FleetBoston Financial, in a report. But for
stocks to keep moving ahead, he said, "the market will need to see the fundamentals continue to come
through."
Mr. Brorson and Mr. Hirschhorn both forecast continuing stock gains, but both said those gains require
positive news from the economy.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 1.65%, or 2.28 points, to 140.10.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the Big Board, where 1.46 billion shares traded, 1,280 stocks rose and 1,997 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 5/32, or $1.5625 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, rose to 4.262%. The 30-year bond was down 5/32 to yield 5.103%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 107.57 yen, down from 107.74 yen, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.2310 from $1.2294.

Keeping the Bush Boom Alive
Keeping the Bush Boom Alive
By Brian S. Wesbury
1,035 words
12 December 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The booming U.S. economy is being fueled by what appears to be the most stimulative set of economic
policies in U.S. history. Monetary policy is more accommodative than it has been since the mid-1970s. The
Bush tax cuts were the most pro-growth of any since 1981. This stimulus, combined with technology-driven
increases in productivity, should boost 2004 real GDP by 5% and create three million new jobs next year
alone.
Nonetheless, beneath the surface problems are brewing. Government spending is soaring, business
regulation is on the rise and protectionism is gathering some momentum. At the same time, excessively
accommodative monetary policy threatens an increase in inflation.
While the historical precedent is not perfect, the U.S. seems to be traveling a similar path to that of the
mid-1960s. In 1964, Lyndon Johnson and Congress implemented the "Kennedy Tax Cuts," which slashed the
top marginal income- tax rate to 70% in 1965, from 91% in 1963. At the same time, the Johnson
administration gave birth to the Great Society, promising "guns and butter."
Non-defense government spending soared by 42% between 1965 and 1968 and federal regulation grew
dramatically. While many predicted a negative impact on the economy from government growth, the tax cuts
and a very accommodative Fed policy masked the damage. Between 1964 and 1966, real GDP growth
averaged 6% and non-farm productivity was strong.
Incomes rose rapidly, as did corporate profits. The Dow Jones Industrial Average jumped 14.6% in 1964,
10.9% in 1965, and brushed 1000 for the first time in history in early 1966. The Fed, and most economists,
estimated that the economy's potential real GDP growth was close to 4%. Inflation averaged just 1.5% in
1964 and 1965 and the 10-year Treasury bond yield was 4.2% -- optimism was widespread.
In retrospect, early 1966 was a crescendo. The Dow ended up falling 18.9% that year, hit 1000 again in 1972,
but did not cross 1000 for good until 1982. Productivity growth slowed and inflation accelerated. Interest
rates jumped, and eventually, "stagflation" became a well-known word.
What happened is really very simple to understand. Great Society spending crowded out private-sector
investment and regulations created rigidities. The entrepreneurial spirit was drained from the economy and
potential growth slowed. Nonetheless, the Fed managed monetary policy as if potential growth was still 4%.
Big government and easy money is the perfect recipe for inflation. By 1970, consumer prices were climbing
by 6% annually. This inflation boosted effective capital-gains tax rates and caused bracket creep. A viscous
cycle began that was made worse by the closing of the gold window, the institution of wage and price
controls, higher taxes and stagnating economic growth. These lessons are so clear that it is hard to imagine
this ever happening again.
But federal government spending as a share of GDP has climbed from 18.4% to 20% in the past two years.
While fighting the war against terrorism is essential to the future of our nation and the Bush administration
gets high marks for taking on this battle, government spending is rising across the board. Between 2002 and
2004, education spending will rise by 31%, housing and urban development spending by 19.1%, agriculture
and EPA spending by 13.5%. Federal spending may not be growing as fast as it did during the Great Society,
but it is still increasing sharply.
It is estimated that the Medicare drug benefit will cost as much as $2 trillion over the next 20 years. The
Sarbanes-Oxley bill has increased business costs, reduced the willingness of publicly traded companies to
take risks and undermined the desire of private companies to go public. And in a potential repeat of the
Microsoft antitrust action, the Justice Department is now reviewing the Oracle bid for Peoplesoft.

--- Page 105 ---
Page 105 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Protectionist tariffs and quotas have caused an inefficient allocation of resources and increased consumer
prices. If continued, the above policies will undermine productivity and reduce potential growth, which many
now estimate at 4% again, just as in the 1970s.
To make matters worse, the Fed has held the real federal funds rate below zero for the past 15 months, the
longest stretch of negative real rates since 1975-76. Fighting non-monetary problems (which include the
uncertainties of war and terrorism) with easy money has begun to increase inflationary pressures. Gold
prices are above $405 an ounce, commodity prices are up 30% across the board and the dollar is very weak.
In a replay of the mid-1970s, OPEC has announced that it will keep oil prices high to compensate for the
weak dollar.
So far, there are very few signs of damage to the economy. As in the 1960s, strong growth and low inflation
have created an environment where the impact of rising regulation, spending and protectionism is hard to
see. In order to keep it that way, the Bush administration must take every opportunity to move toward policies
that enhance individual freedom and support entrepreneurial activity.
Rescinding the steel tariffs is a great first step. Promoting the tax-free Health Savings Accounts that are part
of the Medicare Drug Benefit bill is also very important, and could help offset the costs of the new
entitlements. But more needs to be done. The Bush team should make the 2003 tax cuts permanent, fix the
AMT before it swallows up the entire tax base, and push its tax-free savings accounts again. By far the most
powerful idea on the table is Social Security reform that includes private investment accounts. Success in this
arena would offset damage from a whole host of other policy mistakes.
Without continued progress on the tax front and a serious effort to reign in growth of federal spending, the
gains in the economy we have seen this past year could be fleeting. For now, the Bush boom is not in trouble.
But the only way to make sure it stays that way is to avoid compromises in policy that continue to create
excessive growth in government.
---
Mr. Wesbury is chief economist at Griffin, Kubik, Stephens &Thompson, Inc., in Chicago.

Dow Industrials Push Past 10000 --- Fed News, Strong Retail Data Boost Blue Chips Over Threshold For First Time in 18 Months
Thursday's Markets
Dow Industrials Push Past 10000 --- Fed News, Strong Retail Data Boost Blue Chips Over Threshold
For First Time in 18 Months
By E.S. Browning
1,612 words
12 December 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IN AN ENDORSEMENT of the growing belief that the economy now is out of the woods, the Dow Jones
Industrial Average finished above 10000 for the first time in more than 18 months.
The return to 10000 was a relief to investors whose faith in stocks was sorely tested by the popping of the
technology bubble in 2000 and the three-year bear market that followed. Investors are still far from the giddy
mood of the late 1990s, and stocks are still far from their highs. But the rebound of many beaten-down stocks
has offered solace to wounded investors.
The immediate catalyst was the release of the minutes of October's Federal Reserve policy meeting at 2 p.m.
Eastern time. They raised the tantalizing possibility that, despite a recovering economy, the Fed could leave
its target interest rates at or near their current 45-year low of 1% longer than some investors had believed --
perhaps until the latter part of next year or even longer. Low rates are viewed as good news for economic
growth and corporate profits, as well as for stocks and bonds.
What the minutes actually said was that Fed policy makers anticipated that, even if the economy generates
above-average growth, there still would be too many unemployed people, and other underused resources,
"until perhaps the latter part of 2005 or even later." That suggests inflation could remain quiescent until that
point, although the Fed would almost certainly start to raise rates from their unusually low current levels
before that.
The Fed news came on top of the Commerce Department's morning announcement that retail sales rose
0.9% in November, more than the 0.7% economists had expected, which raised hopes that shoppers could
be preparing to indulge themselves even more than hoped during the Christmas period.
Already up 19% on the year, the Dow industrials jumped another 86.30 points, or 0.87%, to 10008.16, their
first finish above 10000 since May 24, 2002. The industrials now are up 20% on the year, and 37% from their
five-year low point of 7286.27 on Oct. 9, 2002. Treasury bonds also rose. And the dollar, which has been
hitting record lows against the young euro, Europe's common currency, managed to rebound slightly for a
second straight day.
"The 10000 number is significant because it shows how far we have come off the lows," said Matthew
Johnson, head of U.S. stock trading at New York brokerage firm Lehman Brothers. "People feel good. It has
been a good year. The markets have had a great year. To the psyches of traders and the psyches of market
players and investors, this is important."
The market could very well gyrate in the next few days, and the Dow industrials could fall back below 10000.
Some investors already are warning that stocks have gotten ahead of themselves and are overdue for a
pullback. But the majority view now is that stocks are headed higher.
The widespread opinion now, Mr. Johnson said, is that "by the end of the year we will be firmly through
10000, firmly through 1100 on the S&P 500 and firmly through 2000 on the Nasdaq Composite Index."
The latest crossing of 10000 ended a streak of 390 consecutive trading days below 10000, the longest such
drought since the industrials first closed above 10000 on March 29, 1999. The longest sub-10000 period
before this was 59 trading days in 2001, most of that in the period following the Sept. 11 terrorist attacks.
This marked the 19th time the Dow industrials have crossed 10000. After each of the previous 18 finishes
above 10000, the blue-chip average sooner or later ran out of steam and fell back below.
The return to Dow 10000 reflects a growing belief among an increasingly bullish investing public that this
stock recovery is for real and will continue for months -- some people believe years -- to come.

--- Page 107 ---
Page 107 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
At the same time, however, the return to Dow 10000 served as a reminder for many investors of a grim
reality. Although the Dow industrials now are up 2,721.89 points since hitting their five-year low in October of
last year, they still are back only to where they were nearly five years ago. They are far from their record
close of 11722.98 on Jan. 14, 2000. All these gains, in other words, leave stock investors with almost nothing
to show for themselves in five years.
Some investors, in fact, fear that the attainment of the 10000 milestone could serve as a cautionary signal for
investors, making them re-evaluate the market's prospects after its huge gains. The Nasdaq composite, for
example, with its many technology stocks, is up more than 45% this year alone, trading near a two-year high.
It rose 1.98%, or 37.67 points, to 1942.32 yesterday. But an index of 100 of its biggest stocks is trading at 66
times the stocks' earnings for the past 12 months, and 37 times forecast earnings for the coming 12 months,
according to research and money-management group Birinyi Associates in Westport, Conn. That is a high
level, although still short of the moonshot levels hit during the tech bubble.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index, which rose 1.15%, or 12.16 points, to 1071.21, is up 22% on
the year. It trades at 27 times earnings for the past 12 months, compared with a historical average of 16, and
at 19 times forecast earnings.
But the widespread expectation among investors is that stocks are due to rise, at least into next year. The
reason: Corporate profits are continuing to rise even faster than analysts expect, and when they do that,
stocks usually rise as well.
Because low interest rates are such a powerful economic stimulus, "the rally that goes through 10000 will be
powerful," said David Kotok, president of money-management firm Cumberland Advisors in Vineland, N.J. "I
have positioned my portfolios based on that," with a heavy exposure to economically sensitive stocks.
Inflation and interest rates can be expected to remain low, he said, while "profits will be rising and analysts
will be raising estimates. I expect the stock market to go through 10000 and carry higher into the first portion
of next year." Although outside events such as terrorism could jolt the market, he said, "as long as the profit
share is rising in an economy that is rising, stocks have tended to go higher during the past century."
Said Carol McMullen, who heads Eastern Investment Advisors, the investment-management arm of Eastern
Bank in Boston, "There are no near-term problems" in the economy or the stock outlook. Analysts have been
repeatedly lifting earnings forecasts. Interest rates and inflation are low. While economic growth should
slow next year, she expects growth of 4% to 4.5%. Corporate investment is rising so that the economy isn't
overly dependent on the consumer.
Although price-earnings ratios are high and the dollar is falling, "I think it will be a steady, continuous recovery
[in the stock market] for at least the next 12 months and probably longer than that," she said.
In the short run, investors will be watching closely now whether stocks can build on the day's gains, or instead
begin to waver. With stocks up so much going into year's end, professional money managers are eager both
to hold on to current gains and to be sure they take part in any year-end rally. That means they don't want to
sell, but are poised to do so if they see the market beginning to fade.
In the past, the Dow industrials sometimes have wavered when they hit 10000, and other times have held up
strongly. How they behave now could help determine how the last days of the year turn out.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.67%, or 0.92 point, to 137.94.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.44 billion shares traded, 2,453 stocks rose and 810 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 18/32, or $5.625 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, rose to 4.246%. The 30-year bond was up 21/32 to yield 5.110%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 108.05 yen, down from 108.27, while the euro fell
against the dollar to $1.2209 from $1.2216.

Beazer, Netbank, and Dime Community Decline
Small-Stock Focus
Beazer, Netbank, and Dime Community Decline
By Cynthia Schreiber
830 words
11 December 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Home builders and home lenders continued to fall and led small-capitalization stocks lower for
a second straight session.
The catalyst for the sector declines came on Tuesday, when large-cap home lender Washington Mutual
slashed its earnings forecasts, blaming weakness in its mortgage business.
Home-building stocks were the worst performers, despite an upbeat earnings report from Toll Brothers.
Beazer Homes USA lost $4, or 4%, to $97.25, and Champion Enterprises eased 14 cents, or 1.9%, to $7.09,
both on the New York Stock Exchange.
As for savings-and-loan stocks, Netbank lost 47 cents, or 3.6%, to 12.53, Dime Community Bancshares fell
55 cents, or 1.9%, to 29.22, and Staten Island Bancorp (NYSE) declined 45 cents, or 2%, to 21.81.
Analysts said the Federal Reserve's comments on Tuesday, which hinted at rising interest rates down the
road, gave investors an excuse to cash in profits after nearly a year of sharp gains.
"As we get toward the end of year, investors feel it's a good time to lock in profits," said Michael Sheldon,
chief market strategist at Spencer Clark LLC.
Small stocks have done particularly well in 2003, but in the latest session, they underperformed the broad
market. The Russell 2000 Index lost 6.05, or 1.1%, to 528.49, and the Standard &Poor's SmallCap 600 Index
shed 2.89, or 1.1%, to 258.17. The Nasdaq Composite Index of large-cap and small-cap stocks fell 3.67, or
0.2%, to 1904.65.
Gold-related stocks fell as the price of the commodity declined. Golden Star Resources lost 84 cents, or
12%, to 6.38, and Iamgold fell 85 cents, or 11%, to 6.90, both on the American Stock Exchange. Meridian
Gold (NYSE) shed 75 cents, or 5.1%, to 14.05.
Consumer-services stocks struggled, with Priceline.com down 56 cents, or 3.2%, to 16.84. J.P. Morgan
started coverage of the stock at underweight, saying competition is proving detrimental to the online-ticket
seller.
Steel stocks climbed a session after large-cap Nucor raised prices on its steel-sheet products by $40 a ton.
AK Steel Holding rose 24 cents, or 6.6%, or 3.90, on the NYSE.
Upbeat comments by Cisco Systems and a stock upgrade of Corning helped some, but not all,
communications-technology stocks to rise after Tuesday's weakness. Mindspeed Technologies (AMEX) rose
14 cents, or 2.2%, to 6.42, while Harmonic fell 66 cents, or 8.9%, to 6.73, and Remec slid 58 cents, or 6.7%,
to 8.07.
Network Engines, a distribution partner for software- and network-equipment providers, plunged 3.92, or
39%, to 6.10, after saying it expects a newly amended agreement with EMC will hurt gross profits and
operating margins starting in 2004.
Hurco soared 88 cents, or 31%, to 3.74, as the Indianapolis maker of interactive computer controls reported
fourth-quarter net income of 10 cents a share, compared with a net loss of 32 cents a share a year earlier.
ESS Technology rose 1.56, or 11%, 15.35, as the Fremont, Calif., semiconductor company said accelerating
shipments of camera-phone image sensors and DVD products have improved its fourth-quarter earnings and
revenue picture.

Korn/Ferry International (NYSE) rose 1.23, or 12%, to 11.22, as the Los Angeles executive-recruitment
company posted second-quarter net income of six cents a share, compared with a net loss of 48 cents a
share a year earlier. The company said it is beginning to see a recovery in the corporate-employment market.
Shares of aaiPharma rose 3.59, or 19%, to 22.75, after Banc of America Securities started coverage of the

Birth-control-products maker Conceptus fell 69 cents, or 6%, to 10.86, after CIBC World Markets downgraded
