Page 144 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A Global Journal Report: U.S. Stocks' Rally Kicks Into a Higher Gear --- As Euro Touches Another Record, Dollar's Weakness May Persist; A...
Currency Trading
A Global Journal Report: U.S. Stocks' Rally Kicks Into a Higher Gear --- As Euro Touches Another
Record, Dollar's Weakness May Persist; A Deflating Moment at Michelin
By Michael R. Sesit in Paris, Tom Holland in Hong Kong and Michael M. Phillips in Washington
1,422 words
2 December 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
EVEN AS THE U.S. economy continues to show signs of a sturdy recovery, downward pressure on the dollar
is building and could hamper prospects of the U.S. rebound lifting the world's other major economies.
The dollar's weakness -- yesterday it briefly hit fresh record lows against the euro and multiyear lows against
several other currencies, but strengthened by day's end -- is contributing to the rebound in the U.S. economy.
It is doing so by makingexports less costly and international profits richer when translated back into dollars.
But the dollar's persistent weakness threatens to frustrate the world's two other leading economies, which are
dependent on exports for growth: the 12-nation euro zone that uses the European common currency and
Japan. Both economies are reaping the inverse effect of the falling dollar just as they are trying to emerge
from economic slumps.
In the financial markets, worries about a dollar crash simmer beneath the surface. Few traders anticipate
such an event, but a further weakening appears inevitable. A combination of the U.S. government's
laissez-faire attitude toward its decline, along with signs that traditional dollar backers, such as Asian central
banks and other foreign investors, may be losing their appetite for dollar-denominated assets will only add to
pressure on the greenback.
The dollar's decline comes amid more signs that the U.S. economy is racing ahead. A run of strong U.S.
economic indicators continued yesterday with a report that manufacturing activity in November jumped by the
biggest amount in two decades; European manufacturing data for November showed a modest increase, too.
Stock markets on both sides of the Atlantic rose on the news.
Since the start of 2002, the dollar has fallen 26% against the euro and 17% against the yen. Already Europe
is feeling the pain. In the first eight months of this year, euro-area exports fell 4% from the same period a year
earlier by value and exports to the U.S. tumbled 10%, according to the latest available data from Eurostat, the
statistics office of the European Union. Much of that decline has been because sales in dollars abroad are
worth less when translated to euros but a strong euro also can damp sales by making European goods more
expensive abroad.
French tire maker Cie. Generale des Etablissements Michelin said recently that net sales in the first nine
months of the year would have been up 4.5% but because of the dollar's weakness sales actually fell 4.6%
from a year earlier.
"If I were to summarize the key factors which have driven our performance to date I would certainly stress the
strong impact that exchange rates have had," Eric Le Corre, Michelin's head of investor relations, said in a
recent conference call with analysts.
One way to counteract the impact of a weak dollar and piggyback on the U.S. recovery is for governments in
the euro-zone and Japan to stimulate domestic demand. They could do so by persuading Europeans and
Japanese to purchase more, by increasing government spending, or by making their economies more flexible
to adjust to changing conditions. Some countries, such as Thailand and others in emerging Asia already are
taking steps to promote domestic buying.
"A weaker US dollar will help wean the world off U.S. demand and force other governments to deploy other
levers of growth -- tax cuts, investment credits, lower interest rates, budget deficit spending," says Joseph
Quinlan, chief market strategist at Banc of America Capital Management.
Over time, he adds, the weaker dollar could be one part of the equation that leads to a more-balanced global
economy, where growth is less dependent on the U.S. and greater sales of U.S. products abroad keep trade
deficits in check. Others expect the weaker dollar to help the current account stabilize in 2004, and probably

--- Page 145 ---
Page 145 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
shrink as a share of gross domestic product -- even if foreign flows into U.S. assets don't keep pace with past
years.
Thus far, efforts to counteract the dollar's decline have been modest in the major markets. France and
Germany, the two drivers of the euro zone, are expanding government spending over strict limits designed to
check the size of budget deficits in euro-zone countries. But that has created serious tensions with other
governments in Europe that are abiding by the rules. Other reforms such as tax cuts that could stimulate
consumer demand have been half-hearted.
Japan's economy, meantime, has shown signs of recovery after years of stagnation. But it remains dogged by
a banking system laden with hundreds of billions of dollars in nonperforming loans and overcapacity in
several industries, such as construction and retailing. Several of the reform measures analysts say are
needed are politically sensitive, because they involve shutting companies and firing workers. And Japan's
fiscal condition makes a big expansion in government spending politically difficult.
Without those reforms, a further drop in the dollar "will be suffocating" for the economies in Europe and
Japan, says George Magnus, chief economist at the investment bank of UBS AG in London. Currently,
Deutsche Bank predicts Japan's econony will grow 2.6% this year but slip to 1.9% in 2004. At best,
economists forecast the euro zone will grow by 0.5% this year and 1.8% next.
In late New York trading yesterday, the euro stood at $1.1975, compared with $1.1991 late Friday. The dollar
also was trading at 109.35 yen, compared with 109.61 yen before the weekend. The dollar was at 1.2977
Swiss francs, up from 1.2912 francs. Sterling was at $1.7189, down from $17216.
Earlier in the day, the dollar fell to $1.2040 to the euro, its lowest point since the common currency was
launched at the start of 1999. The dollar also fell to a five-year low against the British pound and a six-year
low against the Australian dollar. On Friday, it hit a 10-year low against the Canadian dollar.
UBS predicts that by the end of 2004, the dollar will be trading at $1.32 to the euro, a decline of about 10%
from now, and 100 yen, a decline of 9%.
One of the chief reasons behind the dollar's drop is mounting concern over the U.S. current-account deficit.
To finance that deficit, the U.S. must effectively borrow from foreigners through their purchases of U.S. debt
and other dollar assets.
But there are some early warning flags that one of the biggest purchasers of U.S. debt, Asian central banks,
and other foreign investors may be losing their appetite. In September, net inflows into U.S. securities fell to
just $4.2 billion, the lowest level since late 1998. And at November's auction of two-year U.S. Treasury notes
held last week foreign investors bought just 32% of the $26 billion issue. That compares with the 42%
foreigners snapped up at October's auction of the same size.
The Treasury auction results could be a one-time anomaly. Nonetheless, they have captured the attention of
economists and investors. Another pillar of the dollar's strength in the past has been the U.S. government's
line that it favors a strong dollar. But the Bush administration, under siege earlier this year from
manufacturers complaining that the dollar's strength had made it tough to compete with foreign companies,
signaled last spring that it wouldn't mind a weaker dollar, as long as the movement wasn't too abrupt.
Since then, the government has watched silently from the sidelines as the dollar fell against the euro and the
yen.
"This is not an administration that believes in intervening in exchange markets to try to manipulate the
exchange rate," said University of Chicago economist Randall S. Kroszner, who was, until July, a member of
the president's Council of Economic Advisers. "We've never been about that."
---
Christopher Rhoads in Berlin and Craig Karmin in New York contributed to this article.

While Gold Surges Above $400" -- WSJ Dec. 2, 2003)

Some Fear Inflation Is Ready for a Comeback
Heard on the Street
Some Fear Inflation Is Ready for a Comeback
By Ken Brown
1,496 words
1 December 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
JIM PAULSEN, the chief investment officer at Wells Capital Management, ticks off the ingredients needed for
a mean batch of inflation.
"I would flood the system with money far in excess of economic growth, I would take interest rates to the
lowest levels possible, I would have the government spend like a banshee, I would drop the value of the
dollar, and finally I would take any capacity growth or additional supply growth and stop it," he says. As a
garnish, he'dadd rising commodity prices and a growing trade deficit mixed with some rising trade tensions.
Sound familiar? As investors close the books on 2003 and look out into next year, some are worrying that
inflation, which had been banished in the past few years, is poised for a comeback. Were that to happen, it
would terrify bond investors and shake up the stock market, while giving a boon to some manufacturers,
which finally would be able to raise prices.
"Last fall we were all worried about deflation," says Mr. Paulsen. "What could happen by summer is everyone
could be panicked about inflation."
That panic may be based more on emotion and expectations than on anything else. There are lots of good
reasons why inflation should remain tame, mainly the lack of a strong jobs market. But as investors spend
much of their time trying to divine the future, expectations are what will drive stocks and bonds.
If investors collectively agree that inflation is looming, the angst will first show up in bonds, where yields will
rise and prices will fall. If the Federal Reserve shares that view, it will push up the federal-funds rate, which is
the interest on overnight loans between banks, from its 45-year low of 1%.
That would drive bond prices down, causing pain for bond investors. It could slow or stop the recent
economic rebound, which has boosted corporate profits. That means the stock market, particularly stocks that
do best in a fast-growing economy, like industrial and big ticket consumer-goods companies, could get hit.
Paul McCulley, a managing director at bond manager Pimco, uses a somewhat different metaphor, but also
comes to an inflation conclusion. "The cocktail now pouring into your glass is a 2.5% fed-funds rate by the
end of 2005," he says. "The straw in the beverage in 2004 will be fear."
Mr. McCulley, whose firm, Pacific Investment Management Co., oversees $304 billion in assets, predicts a
bond bear market next year -- which would come on top of a market that has been largely weak since early
this summer. The fed-fund futures market, where investors bet on the direction of short-term interest rates,
is saying short-term rates will start their reversal in May when they rise one-quarter of a percentage point.
Other markets are predicting inflation; investors in inflation-indexed Treasury bonds are saying inflation will
top 2.5% over the next several years. That's well above the 1.5% rate of the past 12 months, but almost
inconsequential compared to years past.
To investors who only a few months ago were wringing their hands over deflation, all this inflation talk might
seem odd. In a way, it's a predictable part of the cycle, now that the economy has crossed the threshold into
a self-sustaining recovery. Powerful economic data over the past few weeks have wiped away fears that the
economy might backslide into recession, though a period of weak growth, which would keep inflation in
check, is possible.
And there still are a lot of facts economists can muster to make their low-inflation case. First and foremost is
that unemployment remains high at 6%, and the jobs situation is worse if you include people who have
stopped looking for work. "Inflation is a lot like Elvis," says David Rosenberg, Merrill Lynch's chief North
American economist. "Lots of reported sightings, none confirmed."

--- Page 147 ---
Page 147 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Whether and how the market reacts to the possibility of inflation depends in part on where it comes from. So
far, the market has shrugged off the declining dollar -- though bonds fell Friday as the dollar hit a low against
the euro -- rising commodity prices and big budget and trade deficits because the jobs market, until recently,
was so weak. "The most important determinant for final goods-and-services inflation does not come from the
commodities market, it comes from the labor market," Merrill's Mr. Rosenberg says.
Most experts, the Fed included, believe the economy would need to create hundreds of thousands of jobs --
as many as 150,000 to 200,000 a month for the better part of a year -- for any inflationary pressure to rise.
But if coming jobs numbers, including Friday's employment report come in very strong, stocks and bonds
could both tumble.
Some economists look to 1993 and 1994 for parallels. The economy and the markets finally gained traction in
1993 after a long slowdown. Commodity prices jumped. At the start of 1994, the Fed started to raise interest
rates, ultimately boosting them by three percentage points. The bond market, caught by surprise, had one of
its worst years, but the stock market, after an early fall, made up lost ground to finish flat.
As the year ended, economists worried about inflation expected another three percentage points of
tightening by the Fed, says John Lipsky, chief economist at J.P. Morgan. Inflation rates never did go up and
neither did interest rates.
The lessons from that period provide a possible investor roadmap for next year. First, avoid bonds,
particularly long-duration bonds that are the most sensitive to interest rates. "People should learn to
appreciate the virtues of cash," says Pimco's Mr. McCulley, a suggestion that is particularly noteworthy
considering it comes from the firm that manages the largest bond mutual fund.
Stocks are a harder call. If the Fed starts to jack up rates, or if investors believe a rate move is imminent, they
will likely sell stocks. That's because with interest rates higher, stocks will look less attractive. But if the rise
in inflation is moderate and the fear factor is low, stocks could do well, particularly if companies are able to
raise prices, thereby boosting revenue and profits.
Looking at what's driving inflation could help investors find the most attractive sectors. The biggest
beneficiaries of inflation generally are commodity producers such as mining and energy companies. These
companies have been earning gobs of cash over the past year, so a boost in their share prices would be
justified. In the inflation-riddled 1970s, energy companies led the market.
The biggest winners this time around could be manufacturers that have suffered from declining prices, due in
part to rising imports. If the weakening dollar, which makes imports more expensive, gives these companies
breathing room to raise prices, their earnings and shares could do well. This group includes auto makers,
electrical equipment companies and even some technology companies. "The same thing that pushed those
tradable goods prices down is likely to push them up," Mr. Paulsen says.
Inflation also benefits debtors, because the dollars needed to pay back the loans become cheaper. That
argues against banks and for companies with lots of debt, usually in industries that require big capital
expenditures, such as cable-television companies and manufacturers.
The other big laggards would likely be service companies, which generally don't face foreign competition and
have been the only ones able to raise prices. When investors were worried about deflation, they turned to
companies that profited through innovation such as the tech and biotech industries, figuring they would be
able to buck the trend and raise prices. While they will still be able to boost profits and may still do well, they
won't be the standouts they would have been under deflation.
---
Feeding the Monster?
Some economists compare today's situation -- where the economy is finally
gaining traction after a long downturn -- with the end of 1993. Then,
interest rates rose rapidly, spooking the markets. Though the slowdown is
different this time, there are some parallels.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Japan's Consumer Price Index Rose in October
TOKYO -- Japan's core consumer price index rose in October for the first time in four years, indicating the
deflation dogging its economy is easing.
The nationwide core consumer price index rose 0.1% in October from a year earlier, the government said
Friday. But economists warned that this was largely due to one-time factors, including recent tax increases
and higher rice prices, and that the index will likely decline again in coming months. More-recent figures for
the Tokyo area showed prices in the capital declining, as the core CPI index fell 0.2% in November from a
year ago.
Other data also indicated that Japan's economic recovery is still on track, though mildly.
Industrial production rose 0.8% in October from September. The Ministry of Economy, Trade and Industry
said company forecasts point to stronger growth next month. Based on its company surveys, METI forecast
output would rise 3.1% in November over October, and then decrease 0.9% in December. However,
spending by wage earners fell 1.1% in real terms in October from a year earlier. And the jobless rate inched
up to 5.2% in October from 5.1% in September.
Nationwide core CPI is the most-watched indicator of deflation in Japan. The Bank of Japan uses it as its
chief price indicator in setting monetary policy. But the central bank has said repeatedly in recent months
that the recent improvement in the CPI is temporary. It has said it expects deflation will continue through next
fiscal year, which starts April 1, so there is no need to alter its ultra-easy monetary policy.
-- Dow Jones Newswires
---
EUROPE
EU Softens Plan for Rules on Cross-Border Deals
BRUSSELS -- European Union ministers agreed on a watered-down European takeover code that leaves
many of the barriers to cross-border mergers in place.
The European Commission, the executive body of the EU, originally aimed to enact a bold plan to remove
most obstacles to cross-border buyouts such as multiple voting rights, voting caps and poison pills. A
shareholder-rights plan, commonly known as a poison pill, is designed to make a threatened takeover more
expensive through the issuance of huge amounts of stock.
But opposition from Germany and Scandinavian countries, who feared losing control of their national
flagships, left most of the defense mechanisms in place. In 2001, Berlin fought against the initial attempt to
strike a deal, insisting management be allowed to fight off unwanted bids.
In Germany, a company can authorize existing owners to buy additional shares at a bargain price, raising the
cost of acquisition. A special law caps outside voting rights in Volkswagen AG at 20%, protecting Europe's
largest car maker from any takeovers.

--- Page 152 ---
Page 152 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A different defense -- multiple voting rights -- is popular in Scandinavia, giving shareholders the ability to
control a company without holding the majority of the capital. In Sweden the system protects the Wallenberg
family's control of Telefon AB L.M. Ericsson and household-goods maker Electrolux.
Defenders say the proposed law contains some advances. The law will force companies to inform the public
about shareholder structures. The legislation also aims to protect the rights of minority shareholders in case
of takeovers, ensuring they receive a fair price.
-- Dow Jones Newswires
BRIEFLY:
The European Commission proposed issuing wallet-size passports for dogs, cats and ferrets. Owners would
be able to use a standardized blue booklet stamped with the European Union's circle of gold stars, listing
vaccinations and certifying the pets are rabies-free. The document would replace pet travel papers issued
nationally by the 15 EU governments and would be valid in all EU countries.

THE AMERICAS
IMF Drops Program Offering Contingent Credit
WASHINGTON -- The International Monetary Fund dropped its plan to issue contingent credit lines to
developing countries -- a sort of preapproved national credit card -- to keep them afloat when they are rocked
by financial instability outside their borders.
The plan, hatched after the devastating global financial meltdown of the late 1990s, was intended to protect
countries with sound economic policies from volatility not of their own making. It had been a centerpiece of
the Clinton administration's effort to redesign the world financial system to keep financial crises from
spreading, the way the collapse of the Thai currency in 1997 subsequently rippled through South Korea,
Indonesia, Russia and Brazil.
Since the IMF began offering the credit lines in 1999, however, not a single country has accepted one,
despite U.S. and IMF efforts to round up a volunteer. Governments of developing nations apparently worried
that asking for contingent credit would be seen in financial markets as a sign of economic weakness, not
strength, and perhaps even spark a crisis. In addition, some IMF officials and rich nations were worried that
once a borrowing government secured a credit line, it might abandon the kind of prudent economic policies
the IMF endorses.
"It was nice in theory, but it was difficult to do in practice," said Edwin M. Truman, who was assistant
secretary of the Treasury for international affairs when the Clinton administration pushed the IMF to create
the contingent-credit program.
In part, the IMF said, such credit lines are less necessary now because many large developing nations "have
reduced their vulnerability to shocks" by stocking up on foreign currency, making their exchange-rate systems
flexible and adopting other economic changes to make them less susceptible to a sudden outflow of capital.
In addition, the credit lines were never a priority for the Bush administration. The administration, instead, has
put its efforts behind a program to make it easier for countries that can't pay their debts to negotiate with their
creditors.
-- Michael M. Phillips
Rate Cuts Help Brazil Pull Out of a Recession
SAO PAULO, Brazil -- Brazil's economy pulled out of its worst recession in five years during the third quarter
as a series of interest-rate cuts by the central bank resuscitated a stagnating industrial sector.
Gross domestic product rose 0.4% on a seasonally adjusted basis in the third quarter compared with the
second. This was lower than forecasts in a Dow Jones Newswires survey of 18 economists, whose estimates
ranged between 0.8% and 2.5%.
The result ended six months of economic contraction. But GDP fell 1.5% in the third quarter when compared
with the same period a year ago. This, too, was lower than survey estimates, which ranged between 0.8%
shrinkage and 1.1% growth.
The government's IBGE statistics institute also revised second quarter GDP results. GDP shrank 1.2% in the
second quarter from the first, less than an initial report of 1.6% growth. Manufacturing led the growth from the

--- Page 153 ---
Page 153 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
second quarter, while agricultural output was weak because of the period between harvests, said Roberto
Ramos, an IBGE director. GDP for industry rose 2.7% in the third quarter from the second.
On Wednesday, Brazil's Senate gave first-round approval to a much-anticipated pension-overhaul bill,
sending the country's benchmark stock index to another record closing high yesterday.
The pension bill aims to save the government more than $16 billion over the next 20 years. Many economists
say final approval of the bill, which is expected in the coming weeks, is crucial to ensuring Brazil's ability to
service its $250 billion public debt.
-- Roundup

Nasdaq Leads Stocks' Rally on Economic-Growth Outlook
Monday's Markets
Nasdaq Leads Stocks' Rally on Economic-Growth Outlook
By E.S. Browning
593 words
25 November 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
RENEWED OPTIMISM about the economy sent the Nasdaq Composite Index up nearly 3%, its biggest point
or percentage gain since July and its seventh-largest percentage rise of the year.
Anticipating strong economic data during the Thanksgiving week, which historically is a strong week for
stocks, investors put an end to two weeks of stock declines. Yesterday, they bid down the prices of gold, oil
and Treasury bonds, which had been rising. They pushed up the beleaguered dollar, which hit a record low
against the euro last week.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 119.26 points, or 1.24%, to 9747.79, its biggest gain since Oct. 28. It
is up almost 17% on the year, within range again of the 18-month high of 9858.46 hit early this month.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 2.81%, or 53.26 points, to 1947.14, up almost 46% on the year. It was the
index's third biggest point gain this year.
Investors are looking past the violence in Iraq and Turkey, and focusing on the positive business outlook, said
Matthew Johnson, head of U.S. stock trading at New York brokerage firm Lehman Brothers.
"The economy continues to gain steam. The fundamentals continue to look good. Although we do see higher
interest rates down the road, nothing is imminent," he noted.
Some economists now project that revised third-quarter economic-growth figures, due today, could show an
annualized gain of 8%, up from the already high 7.2% estimated earlier. Positive reports from retailers are
fueling hopes for a strong Christmas season.
Thanksgiving week has been a positive one for stocks almost two-thirds of the time since 1952, according to
Ned Davis Research. The average Thanksgiving-week gain in the Dow industrials -- 0.66% -- is more than
four times that of a typical week. That knowledge helped chase away short-sellers, who bet on stock declines
by borrowing stock and selling it. (The U.S. financial markets will be closed on Thanksgiving; the stock market
also closes early, at 1 p.m. EST, on Friday.)
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index yesterday rose 1.62%, or 16.80 points, to 1052.08, up almost 20% this
year.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.17%, or 0.22 point, to 132.76.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.30 billion shares traded, 2,434 stocks rose and 846 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 22/32, or $6.875 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, rose to 4.240%. The 30-year bond was down 31/32 to yield 5.072%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 109.45 yen, up from 108.77, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.1762 from $1.1917.

Assessing Threats to the Global Recovery
Global View
Assessing Threats to the Global Recovery
By George Melloan
1,078 words
25 November 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A19
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Two days after the Bush administration imposed quotas on Chinese-made bras, dressing gowns and knitted
fabrics last week, Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan warned of "emerging protectionism" in
America. An election year is coming up and the Bushies have shown themselves capable of playing politics
with trade, most notably with the steel tariffs that the World Trade Organization declared illegal this month.
The administration took the warning to heart. U.S. Commerce Undersecretary Grant Aldonas said in Brussels
yesterday that the U.S. is not on a protectionist path. And there are rumors that the U.S. will withdraw the
steel tariffs soon. The dollar strengthened on this news yesterday, confirming that traders had shared Mr.
Greenspan's worries and were relieved.
The dollar's slump was an exception to an otherwise cheerful outlook generated by the robust 7.2% growth of
the U.S. economy in the third quarter. Some forecasters are predicting an above-average 3.5% to 4% next
year. The U.S. recovery has been reinforced by dramatic growth in China, Singapore and other parts of Asia,
and even some glimmers of light in the European Union. So it bids fair to roll on and give Mr. Bush another
four years in the White House.
The president's political strategists no doubt reasoned that limited restriction on textile imports would please
Carolinians and insure Mr. Bush of a solid South next year. They can argue that higher prices on a few items
of apparel won't be noticed at all in a $10 trillion economy. This little gesture will draw off some of the
anti-China heat in Congress, fueled, by the way, by some injudicious remarks from Mr. Bush's Treasury
Secretary, John Snow.
But beware of hubris, as the Greeks used to say. The danger of serious errors is greatest when you're riding
high. The gods of economics could still conspire to mess up this rosy election-year scenario. Once you open
the Pandora's box of protectionism all kinds of banshees come screaming out. Mr. Greenspan clearly feared
that granting protection to Burlington Industries will bring dozens of other petitioners to Mr. Bush's door
demanding relief from international competition.
The chairman is no doubt aware as well that a wave of U.S. protectionism would be inopportune at this stage
of the economic recovery, even aside from the bad example it would set for the likes of Brazil or Japan.
Protectionism could disrupt the ability of markets to pare down the very large U.S. trade deficit.
That process is being played out by means of a weakening dollar, which hit a record low against the euro last
week. As the dollar weakens, so does U.S. purchasing power in overseas markets. The willingness of foreign
investors to buy or hold dollar-denominated securities, which already are floating around the world in huge
amounts, declines as well. That contributes to further dollar weakness. The end result is to curb American
buying abroad, bringing the U.S. trade account back into better balance. This process is painful enough
without it being made more painful with protectionist measures.
One principal way Americans could feel the pain is through inflation generated by rising dollar costs of
imports. They have been sheltered from inflation for the last several years by the combination of low-cost
imports and sharp improvements in the productivity of American manufacturers. To be sure, these gains help
cause layoffs, which increases the political pressure for protectionism. But protectionism is a false god. It
ultimately lowers living standards and destroys more jobs than it saves as trade, and hence economic activity,
slows.
Inflation, as measured by the Consumer Price Index, still appears to be low, at around 2%. But the CPI is not
an entirely comprehensive measure of what is happening in the U.S. economy. House prices in some areas
have zoomed upward, a phenomenon widely described as "asset inflation." Commodity prices are up
sharply from a year ago and the price of gold climbed above $400 last week to its highest level in seven
years.

--- Page 157 ---
Page 157 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The effect of the dollar's weakness on demand for dollar-denominated assets also has had an impact on the
U.S. economy. Weakened demand from abroad for U.S. bonds has threatened to raise bond yields, boosting
interest rates generally. The American recovery, particularly the housing boom, has been fueled by low
interest rates. Higher rates could put a damper on the use of credit, with predictable consequences for
economic growth.
Mr. Greenspan and the Fed could pre-empt the market by raising the federal fund rate. This applies to
interbank trading of short-term credit, the only interest rate the Fed influences directly. Raising it might shore
up demand for the dollar and, paradoxically, slow the climb in long-term rates, on bonds, for example. But
because of the widespread illusion that raising the fed fund rate in itself increases credit costs, that is not a
very politic thing to do when the CPI is still low and the stock markets are in a cheerful mood. So Mr.
Greenspan will probably sit tight with his fingers crossed.
But it is true that the economic outlook is promising, despite these dangers. China, thanks in large part to its
booming exports to America and American investments in manufacturing to take advantage of low wage
costs, has become something of an Asian economic dynamo. If its exports are costing the U.S. jobs, China
also is creating jobs as it contributes to the overall growth in global trade. It has become a big importer, if not
directly from the U.S., from other countries that are markets for U.S. goods and services. World trade is not a
zero-sum game, but beneficial to all who engage in it. Why else would rational humans do it, and why else
would the history of world economic development be told in terms of the search by traders and investors for
new opportunities?
There are few quicker ways for a government to get itself into trouble than by interfering with this natural
human process. It can be hoped that the Bush administration is sincere in its reassurances and will go no
further than raising the cost of intimate wear. But Mr. Greenspan's warning was timely. In politics, it's possible
to be too clever by half.

Stocks Stop Skidding On Dollar's Rebound, Strong Housing Data
Wednesday's Markets
Stocks Stop Skidding On Dollar's Rebound, Strong Housing Data
By E.S. Browning
411 words
20 November 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS REBOUNDED, ending a four-day losing streak as a recovering dollar brought a respite in the
stock-selling pressure.
Treasury bonds, which had risen during the recent stock decline, reversed ground and fell sharply. News of
strong October housing starts was good for stocks, but bad for bonds because it rekindled fears of higher
interest rates to come in a strengthening economy.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 66.30 points, or 0.69%, to 9690.46, recouping most but not all of
Tuesday's 87-point decline. The index is up 16% for the year.
Because stocks have risen so much, money managers are nervous about not losing their annual gains, and
that could provoke more selling if they get more unsettling news.
"We seem to be trading in line with the dollar" again, said Todd Clark, head of listed trading at Wells Fargo
Securities, a San Francisco brokerage arm of Wells Fargo &Co. Yesterday's gain followed a decline Tuesday,
when the dollar hit a record low against the euro.
Other investment vehicles retraced Tuesday's moves, with gold and oil falling and cotton rebounding. Gold
pulled back after briefly surpassing $400 an ounce, which would have been a 7 1/2-year high if it had held.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.95%, or 17.90 points, to 1899.65, up 42% on the year. The broad S&P
500 rose 0.80%, or 8.29 points, to 1042.44, up 18% this year.
Stocks also were helped by news that General Electric will sell shares in its mortgage and life-insurance
companies, sees an improving business climate, and will raise its dividend.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 1.57%, or 2.1 points, to 131.54.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose. On the Big Board, where 1.32 billion shares traded, 1,916 stocks rose and 1,304 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 26/32, or $8.125 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, rose to 4.247%. The 30-year bond was down 1 12/32 to yield 5.092%.
The dollar rose. It traded at 109.28 yen, up from 108.07; the euro fell to $1.1885 from $1.1949.

Volatility Draws Sellers of Options For Short Term
Options Report
Volatility Draws Sellers of Options For Short Term
By Kopin Tan Dow Jones Newswires
464 words
19 November 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Stocks continued to slip despite upbeat earnings reports from retailers like Home Depot Inc.
Staples Inc. and Saks Inc. and the fear gauge crept higher.
After jumping 10% on Monday, the Chicago Board Options Exchange volatility index, or VIX, increased 0.51
or 2.8% to 19.11. The CBOE Nasdaq volatility index, or VXN, rose 6.6%.
The rise in volatility and option premiums attracted some sellers of short-term options, traders said. Although
volatility and option premiums remain near their lowest levels in years, these option sellers believe volatility
could remain subdued or even ease further as the economy and stock prices improve. Some also are looking
ahead to the holidays, a potentially quiet time. Because options lose value over time, these sellers likely are
trying to let options' "time decay" work in their favor -- after option buyers had seen their holdings languish in
the generally muted market of recent weeks.
There were signs of market jitters as the dollar declined and investors flocked to the safe haven of gold. Call
options on gold stocks traded briskly as some of these stocks pushed to new 52-week highs.
Peter Dunay, chief market strategist at Wall Street Access, said he believes these stocks will continue to
move and draw option buyers. "Volatile stocks don't just get to a high and then sit there," he said. "They
attract attention and will either move further up or come down," possibly benefiting those who hold the
appropriate puts or calls.
Newmont Mining rose $1.88 to $45.30. The December 45 calls traded 2,700 contracts, compared with 12,454
contracts outstanding, and gained 90 cents to $2.15 at the International Securities Exchange.
Cautious investors continued to browse for downside insurance amid worries about rich stock valuations.
Hewlett-Packard Co., which will report quarterly earnings today, fell 35 cents to $21.65. Its December 20 puts
traded 10,473 contracts, compared with 6,655 contracts outstanding, and were at 35 cents at the Philadelphia
Stock Exchange. The December 22.50 calls traded 2,359 contracts, compared with 33,630 contracts
outstanding, and eased 10 cents to 65 cents at the International Securities Exchange.
Puts traded actively in eBay, whose popular PayPal online-payment service is battling an email virus that
seeks to defraud customers by asking for confidential information -- from credit-card data to social security
numbers -- commonly sought by identity thieves.
EBay, which has skidded 17% since reaching a 52-week high last month, fell 4.1%, or $2.17 to $51.11. Its
December 50 puts traded 5,660 contracts, compared with 10,143 contracts outstanding, and gained 70 cents
to $1.70 at the CBOE.

Forex View: Dollar's Fall Slows, but Pressure Stays --- U.S. Currency Is Showing Some Signs of Stabilizing After a Weeklong Slide
Currency Trading
Forex View: Dollar's Fall Slows, but Pressure Stays --- U.S. Currency Is Showing Some Signs of
Stabilizing After a Weeklong Slide
By Tom Barkley Dow Jones Newswires
733 words
17 November 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Dollar bears have little reason to reconsider expectations that the currency will continue to
head lower after failing to draw support from a recent string of solid U.S. data, but they may have to be
patient.
Coming off a weeklong slide in which the dollar fell 3.4% against the euro and about 1.9% against the yen,
the market is showing signs of stabilizing before targeting new historical lows for the U.S. currency.
"I think that a dollar-bearish sentiment is absolutely appropriate going into this week, but you are starting to
come up against some pretty big levels against the euro and the yen," said Lara Rhame, senior economist at
Brown Brothers Harriman &Co. in New York.
She expects pressure to remain on the dollar, but adds, "I just don't know if we're going to see the data or
enough market activity to give the euro the momentum to strengthen through its all-time high [around
$1.1935] or to give the yen the oomph to push past" the Bank of Japan. The central bank has been widely
suspected of intervening to defend 108 yen in recent sessions.
The pullback in the dollar began Nov. 7, when investors used an unexpected jump in October payrolls as an
excuse to take profits. With subsequent upside surprises from U.S. data unable to stem further losses in the
dollar, many analysts consider an economic recovery to be largely priced in and are waiting for the next step:
rising interest rates. But recent statements by Federal Reserve policy makers make clear that rate increases
aren't likely until well into 2004.
"You've got rising yields elsewhere in the world, and the Fed is keen to damp yields in the U.S.," said Sean
Callow, currency strategist at New York-based financial market research firm IDEAglobal, noting recent rate
increases by central banks in the U.K. and Australia.
With interest-rate differentials growing increasingly unfavorable for the dollar, he said that "leaves the U.S.
essentially trying to draw capital on the basis of its strong growth and perhaps its equity markets, and less so
on its yield attraction."
Several speeches by Fed officials are scheduled for this week, including Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan on
Thursday, and they are expected to continue to play down chances for a near-term rate increase.
Risk aversion could also keep pressure on the dollar, with investors growing increasingly concerned about
escalating violence in the Middle East.
Gold, often a risk-averse investment, is trading just under $400, an area not seen in 7 1/2 years. A break
above that key level could lead to further dollar weakness, especially against so-called commodity currencies
of Canada, South Africa, Australia and New Zealand, say analysts. All of the dollar bloc currencies have
strengthened to historical levels in recent weeks.
Yet despite the overall bearish environment, Mr. Callow also doesn't expect to see significantly lower levels
for the dollar this week.
"The past week has probably encouraged people who have the patience to wait weeks and months for the
dollar to decline, given the fact that it has failed to surge on payrolls, et cetera," he said. "But I don't think it
means that it just steadily falls every week."
Friday afternoon, the dollar was at 108.33 yen, up from 108.14 yen late Thursday in New York. The euro was
trading around $1.1771, up from $1.1731 Thursday. It was at 127.51 yen, above 126.86 yen. Against the

--- Page 161 ---
Page 161 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Swiss franc, the dollar was lower at 1.3279 francs from 1.3374 francs. Sterling rose to $1.6868 from $1.6856
Thursday.
From a technical perspective, the dollar remains weak over the medium term, according to Andrew Chaveriat,
foreign-exchange technical analyst at BNP Paribas in New York. But it could be poised for a pullback before
returning to three-year lows against the yen below 107.80 yen and last month's lows against the euro around
$1.1860.
"A lot of people bought the euro on the way up, and they may be getting a little nervous and taking money off
the table," said Mr. Chaveriat.

What Sleeps in Class, Stays Up Very Late And Loves Sweets? --- It's the New Pet on Campus, The Little 'Sugar Glider,' A Pocket-Size...
What Sleeps in Class, Stays Up Very Late And Loves Sweets? --- It's the New Pet on Campus, The
Little 'Sugar Glider,' A Pocket-Size Marsupial
By Russell Gold
1,240 words
14 November 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SAN MARCOS, Texas -- Lauren Wilkins attends classes at Texas State University with the usual accessories
of a college student: pens, cellphone, textbooks. Less noticeable are her pocket pets -- two tiny marsupials,
one asleep in her blouse pocket, the other in her cargo pants.
"I'm the kind of person who if things get hectic, I like to feel fur," says Ms. Wilkins. So she shares her dorm
room and her pockets with Jax and Dawson, exotic creatures known as sugar gliders.
The popular new campus residents, illegal in some states and banned in many dormitories, are nocturnal
mammals (Petaurus breviceps) native to Australia. They get their common name from their penchant for
sweets and their ability, in the wild, to soar more than a hundred feet. They can live to be 15.
Though they have serious drawbacks -- don't try to housebreak them -- sugar gliders fit in well on campus.
They sleep through classes, stay up late and have odd, protein-rich diets -- not unlike college students.
"People think they're either heinous or really, really cute," says Toni Kwan, who lived with two sugar gliders in
her Duke University dorm room last year. The tiny animals fit in a hand and weigh less than a pound. They
have pink noses, long fluffy tails and marble-size black eyes.
Keeping them well-fed is tricky. The emergency animal hospital at Texas A&M University's vet school sees
about one sugar glider a week, usually for malnutrition, particularly calcium deficiencies. "Some make it,
some don't," says David Phalen, an associate professor of veterinary medicine. A modified protein-rich diet
developed for possums -- a mash of wheat germ, baby food and honey -- seems to work. Most students feed
them fruit, often squirreled out of university dining halls.
As college roommates, sugar gliders have some compatibility issues. They have sharp claws, good for
climbing acacia trees. They usually wake up and become frenetically active around 10 p.m., and don't settle
down until much later. They bark and, when startled, they may let loose a noise very much like an electric
pencil sharpener. "I learned to be a really sound sleeper. Let's just say I don't get up with alarm clocks
anymore," says Jasmine Jorgensen, who kept gliders in her college apartment at the University of
Maryland-Baltimore County in the mid-1990s. Furthermore, she says, her gliders "smelled like rotten bacon."
Breeders, most located in Sun Belt states, sell hand-raised babies for between $150 and $250. Mike
Sandridge sells about 1,000 sugar gliders a year from his home a few miles outside Dallas. About 30% go to
college students. "Are they doing it with permission or not? I don't want to know," he says.
Sugar gliders have been around in this country since the early '90s but aren't well known outside college
campuses. Mr. Sandridge got hooked on sugar gliders a decade ago after seeing one at a pet show in
Phoenix. He found one advertised for sale in the Thrifty Nickel, a free newspaper full of classified ads, and
drove to Tulsa, Okla., to pick it up. Soon after, he began breeding them. The gestation period for sugar-glider
babies, or joeys, is two to three weeks. Mothers usually have just one baby, sometimes two. They live in her
pouch for two to three months, at which point they are ready to be adopted and to bond with human owners.
Mr. Sandridge, who says he is a registered breeder with U.S. Department of Agriculture, started his breeding
business in his garage, then moved to a strip mall and finally to a 1,350 square-foot warehouse. When he has
more sugar glider babies than buyers, he ramps up his advertising on the Web.
Breeders could soon get a lot busier, thanks to a sugar glider that climbed from dorm room to executive suite.
Earlier this year, Malcolm Bird, senior vice president for kids and teens at America Online, was looking for an
unusual animal to incorporate into a new AOL service for children. During a brainstorming meeting, a staffer
mentioned that her boyfriend had just graduated from college and was looking for a new home for his sugar
glider. "I said 'what's a sugar glider?' " recalls Mr. Bird.

--- Page 163 ---
Page 163 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The sugar glider, named Ariel, now lives in a cage outside Mr. Bird's office in Dulles, Va. Mr. Bird has bought
Ariel a companion, a sugar glider named Bronx. Last month the Time Warner Inc. unit launched KOL, a kids
online section of AOL. Ariel &Bronx will have their own section of KOL, with a Webcam in the cage, message
boards, chat rooms about them as well as educational information on sugar gliders and marsupials
Mr. Bird says the Web site will make clear that owning a sugar glider isn't for everyone. "They will nip and bite
you if they don't know you," he says. But Mr. Bird has bonded with his sugar gliders and sometimes takes
Ariel to meetings in his shirt pocket.
Some college officials understand the allure of owning an exotic pet, even if they don't approve. Jim Settle,
head of residence life at Texas State, says that 15 years ago, he and his roommates kept a miniature alligator
in his room at the University of Kansas. "It was cool," he says, because he and his friends were famous on
campus for having the alligator. "Students have to find a way to fit in," he says. "Having a pet can help them
do that." But these days, Mr. Settle thinks pets in residence halls are definitely not cool.
They also are illegal in several states that classify them as wild animals. Possession of sugar gliders in
California is punishable by a $500 fine.
At Texas State University, the only pets allowed are fish. Elizabeth Ann Munoz has run afoul of this rule twice.
She bought her first sugar glider while a first-year student at Texas State "because I was kind of lonely and
wanted companionship," she says. She tucked one of her gliders into her sports bra during the day. Before
the end of the semester, someone tattled, so she had to make the 560-mile roundtrip to McAllen, Texas, to
take her pet to her parents' home.
The next year, she sneaked her pet back onto campus. This time, the animal disappeared one night into the
drop ceiling and reappeared four floors away in another student's room. Now a senior, she lives off campus.
In addition to using her pockets, Ms. Wilkins carries her pets in a baby-blue pouch she knitted that fits into her
purse. One afternoon recently, she opened the pouch and Jax poked out his head before bounding onto her
shoulders. She tried to retrieve him, but he quickly squirmed away. "He likes to be on me more than in the
pouch," she says.
She gives her two gliders free rein in her residence-hall room. They play on her metal-wire bookshelves and
wooden dresser. And the floor is spread with newspaper. "My room is a big sugar glider jungle gym," she
says.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Pets in a Pocket" -- WSJ Nov. 28, 2003)

Stocks Shake Off Losing Streak; DJIA Gains 111.04 Points
Wednesday's Markets
Stocks Shake Off Losing Streak; DJIA Gains 111.04 Points
By E.S. Browning
450 words
13 November 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AFTER THREE down days, a fresh dose of optimism about the business outlook sent major indexes back
near their recent highs.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average jumped 111.04 points, or 1.14%, to 9848.83, up 18% on the year. It now is
barely below the 17-month high of 9858.46 hit last week.
That followed upbeat comments on the business climate from Jeffrey Immelt, chief executive of General
Electric, and Samuel Palmisano, chief executive of International Business Machines, at an IBM conference in
San Francisco.
"There is so much optimism around that the sellers to a certain extent are just waiting for the market to get to
10000," said Bob Basel, senior listed trader at Citigroup's New York brokerage arm. "Maybe that's where your
sellers reappear, but for now, they are nowhere to be found."
There were signs of nervousness. The Dow industrials and Standard &Poor's 500-stock index both edged
above their 17-month highs, but fell back once they reached those psychologically important levels. Amid
incipient worries about inflation and continuing Iraq violence, gold rose to $394.90 an ounce in late New
York trading, its highest level in 7 1/2 years. Money also moved toward Treasury bonds, another safe haven.
But the end of the year historically has been positive for stocks, and few investors were willing to bet against
more gains. Computer-chip stocks were among the leaders following a report from market-research firm
Gartner that world semiconductor sales should grow more than 20% next year.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 2.19%, or 42.36 points, to 1973.11, up 48% on the year. The S&P 500
rose 1.14%, or 11.96 points, to 1058.53, up 20% this year.
Applied Materials, a big producer of chip-making gear, rose more than 2% in advance of its quarterly earnings
release, which came after regular trading hours. The earnings news was better than expected and the stock
rose an additional 2.9% in after-hours trading.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, 1.32 billion shares traded, 2,513 stocks rose and 759 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 11/32, or $3.4375 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, fell to 4.401%. The 30-year bond was up 23/32 to yield 5.214%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 108.79 yen, up from 108.78, while the euro rose against
the dollar to $1.1639 from $1.1507.

Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
414 words
13 November 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Today's Market Forecast]
Two Roads Diverge
Some say the world will end in deflation, some say in inflation.
The bond market seems too confused to know which path to take. True, long rates have been creeping up in
the past month or so. The 10-year is yielding 4.40%, a bit off its highs for the year.
This at a time of ample supply. Today, the government is selling $17 billion of 10-year paper. Traders and
bond-market pundits have congratulated themselves for having withstood this week's barrage of newly
supplied government debt. It's only continuing, given the huge budget deficits.
The bond market's signal seems that there may be problems with the economic expansion, say in the second
half of next year. That's ominous for stocks. But if the consensus about the economy is right, and the U.S. is
looking at a growth rate next year of roughly 4%, that would be good for stocks but bad for bonds. Could the
10-year yield jump into the 5.5% to 6% range? Higher?
Inflation remains nowhere to be seen, according to the Fed, which appears not to be looking for signs of it.
Gold futures traded at a seven-year high yesterday, almost hitting $400 an ounce. Oil, stubbornly, is around
$31 a barrel, thanks to continued unrest in the Middle East and bungling in Iraq. Commodity prices in general
have had a quiet 30%-odd move up this year. Further, the dollar keeps falling. At some point this could scare
off foreign investors, on whom the U.S. depends to fund the trade deficit.
Some inflation would mean companies finally have welcome pricing power, enabling them to increase profits
though means other than cost-cutting. But what if, for instance, only the commodity-based companies realize
such power, and companies that sell to consumers remain bereft?
Wells Fargo's Jim Paulsen is a believer in economic expansion through next year and is bullish on stocks. But
he sees trouble on the horizon, as the consumer peters out. Recent consumer buying has been driven by the
low rates borne of disinflation and the weak economy. The coming rise in rates will crimp indebted
consumers, and the expansion's attendant price inflation will pressure real wages. He says: "The worst thing
that could happen to the consumer would be a recovery in the economy."

Unglamorous Axle Maker Is Good as Gold
Unglamorous Axle Maker Is Good as Gold
By Joseph B. White
1,098 words
10 November 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AMERICAN AXLE &Manufacturing Holdings Inc. produces some of the auto industry's most unglamorous
hardware, including axles and drive shafts. Its biggest factory is a union shop in the heart of Detroit. Its
primary customers are Detroit's cost-slashing Big Three auto makers.
With that profile, American Axle might look like a sitting duck for competition from China. But the company
and its co-founder, chairman and chief executive officer, Richard E. Dauch, are making money, and gearing
up to grow.
American Axle is forecasting a 10% increase in profit for 2004. In 4 p.m. composite trading on the New York
Stock Exchange Friday, the company's shares stood at $35.71, close to a 52-week high and double the
$17-a-share initial public offering price in January 1999. At a time when many U.S. manufacturers are cutting
jobs, American Axle has expanded its work force by 60% since 1994. Its productivity has more than doubled
during the same time by one measure.
Mr. Dauch, a former Chrysler Corp. executive whose career included stints at Volkswagen AG's U.S.
operations and General Motors Corp., expresses optimism that soon he will win an investment-grade debt
rating from major credit-rating agencies that will clear a path for further growth through acquisitions. In the
meantime, he is adding production lines and looking for more work.
"We just resourced business from a competitor. They were doing it in China," Mr. Dauch says. "We'll do it in
Michigan."
As Mr. Dauch zooms around his Detroit plant on a personalized yellow golf cart, he is charged up, shaking
hands with workers and pointing out red, white and blue signs proclaiming records for months of zero-defect
quality. Just down the street, workers are finishing an imposing new headquarters building that looms over a
gritty industrial stretch of Detroit's I-75 freeway. Mr. Dauch, his voice booming over the factory's hum, has
choice words for those who say American manufacturers are toast.
"I got fed up with everybody giving up on Detroit, and saying you couldn't work with union labor," Mr. Dauch
says. "That's bulls---."
The outlook wasn't so clear-cut in 1994, when an investor group led by Mr. Dauch bought what is now
American Axle from General Motors. On the plus side, Mr. Dauch had a substantial chunk of guaranteed
business from GM, tied to the life cycles of high-volume models such as the Chevrolet Silverado pickup. But
he inherited plenty of problems. American Axle's factories, he says, "were a hellhole of GM," with outdated
equipment, old products and poorly trained workers.
Some other former GM parts-making units have struggled to survive on their own. But American Axle has
averaged 8% compounded annual growth during the past nine years, riding a surge in sales of the GM trucks
for which it makes axles and drivetrains.
Mr. Dauch has also done very well. He controls 8.1 million American Axle shares, or 15% of the total,
according to the company's most recent proxy statement. His shares have an indicated value of $289 million
based on Friday's 4 p.m. price. Until last month, American Axle's biggest shareholder was the
leveraged-buyout firm Blackstone Group. In October, Blackstone sold 7.5 million shares to Morgan Stanley,
reducing its stake to about 12% of the company.
To keep Wall Street on his side, Mr. Dauch must prove that a unionized U.S. manufacturer can thrive over the
long haul in the face of global competition and relentless pricing pressure from the big auto makers.
A large part of American Axle's success in recent years is due to GM's successful effort to boost sales of light
trucks and to American Axle's own ability to add more features to those vehicles. American Axle now supplies
about $1,175 worth of components per vehicle to the GM models it serves.

--- Page 167 ---
Page 167 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Going forward, American Axle must win GM business on its merits, and develop new customers and new
products. High on the agenda: engineering new, lightweight axle and drive systems to serve the fast-growing
"crossover" sport-utility vehicle market (SUVs that are more carlike), which is now dominated by GM rivals.
Landing new business from these rivals will be critical to American Axle. The company still derives 82% of its
revenue from its former parent, and will depend on GM for significant business for years to come. Mr. Dauch
says American Axle recently won a contract to supply axles to GM's next generation of large pickups and
SUVs, code-named the GMT 900 program. That program begins in 2006 and should run through 2012. "We
had to earn this," Mr. Dauch says.
Mr. Dauch is wooing new customers in Germany and Japan and increasing American Axle's
research-and-development spending by 10% a year to broaden the company's offerings, including adding
electronic brains to suspension components and developing lighter-weight drivetrain systems.
"They have proved themselves with GM," says UBS Warburg automotive analyst Robert Hinchliffe. "It's a
matter of getting somebody else to take a chance with what still is a relatively young company." Mr. Hinchliffe
doesn't own American Axle shares. American Axle is a UBS client.
Another challenge for American Axle will be to sustain the edge the company has in quality and product
technology over competitors in lower-wage countries. United Auto Workers-represented hourly workers at
American Axle's U.S. plants earn $22 to $25 an hour straight time. Including benefits, that goes up to about
$43 an hour. But inside American Axle's Detroit plant, a red, white and blue banner proclaims, "Low Cost
Country."
Mr. Dauch says American Axle is a low-cost producer, despite its pay scale, because its workers are more
productive, and build better-quality products than competitors in low-wage countries.
American Axle now requires 3.8 hours of labor to generate $1,000 of revenue, compared with 10 labor hours
per $1,000 of revenue in 1994, Mr. Dauch says. The Detroit complex's Plant Two produced 1,900 axles a day
under GM, he says. Today, it ships 4,600 axles a day, with no increase in production space, and with far
better quality.
During the past nine years, improvements in American Axle's quality have saved GM $250 million in warranty
costs, Mr. Dauch says. A GM spokeswoman wouldn't comment directly on American Axle's performance.

STRONG ECONOMIC news, coupled with favorable earnings news from Cisco Systems, put an end to two
days of stock weakness and drove the Nasdaq Composite Index to a 21-month high.
After beginning the day with a slump -- amid worries that the year's stock gains have been too much, too fast
-- stocks climbed back as the day wore on. As they have repeatedly over the past eight months, investors
treated the declines as an opportunity to buy, and the indexes began to recover around midday.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average finished ahead 0.37%, or 36.14 points, at 9856.97. That represents a gain
of 18% so far this year and left the average just short of its 17-month high of 9858.46, hit Monday.
"The market just has a huge underlying bid to it. Any time there is any weakness, buyers just show up," said
John O'Donoghue, senior stock trader at Credit Suisse First Boston.
The next big indicator investors will be watching, he said, is the Labor Department's employment report for
October, due this morning. With economic growth, earnings and corporate investment all seeming to be on
the mend, Mr. O'Donoghue called the jobs news "the final part of the jigsaw puzzle" for the economy. Any
sign that companies are hiring again would reinforce hopes that a real recovery is under way, and could have
a big impact on stocks.
Investor willingness to bid stocks up strongly after the morning declines yesterday spurred renewed optimism
about stocks. Stocks had been mildly down on Tuesday and Wednesday, and investors were wondering
whether that would be the start of a longer decline, or just a pause in the gains. The sight of stocks recovering
helped pull more money into the stock market and boosted investor optimism. Gold and treasury-bond prices
fell and the dollar rose.
Among the positive economic news was a weekly employment report indicating that new unemployment
claims were fewer than expected last week. Investors also received strong third-quarter productivity numbers.
In addition, Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan said in a speech that the odds now favor job
creation.
Treasury-bond prices were hurt by more signs that interest rates could feel upward pressure in the future.
The Bank of England became the first major central bank to raise its benchmark interest rates during the
current economic recovery. The U.S. Treasury is expected to sell an additional $57 billion in three-, five- and
10-year Treasury notes next week, putting more upward pressure on bond yields. And Mr. Greenspan's
comments, while indicating he is able to be patient in raising rates, nevertheless intensified speculation that
an improving economy will force the Fed to raise its target interest rates sooner or later.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.87%, or 17.00 points, to 1976.37, up 48% this year and the highest
finish since January 2002. The S&P 500 index rose 0.59%, or 6.24 points, to 1058.05. That represents a gain
of 20% this year and leaves that index just below a 17-month high.
The Russell 2000 small-stock index hit a three-year high, rising 0.75%, or 4.03 points, to 542.94, up 42% so
far this year.
Meanwhile, the 10-year Treasury note fell 18/32, or $5.625 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which moves
inversely to price, rose to 4.423%. The 30-year bond was down 1 2/32 to yield 5.257%.

Putting Pork Bellies in Your Portfolio --- With Commodity Prices Up, Investors Try Range of Options From Futures to Gold Funds
Putting Pork Bellies in Your Portfolio --- With Commodity Prices Up, Investors Try Range of Options
From Futures to Gold Funds
By Peter A. McKay
1,240 words
5 November 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AS HOT AS THE STOCK MARKET has been lately, investing in some commodities has produced far better
returns this year. The prices of cotton and copper, for example, have risen more than 30%, easily outstripping
the nearly 18% rise in the Dow Jones Industrial Average.
Overall, commodity prices are lagging behind stocks with the Dow Jones-AIG Commodity Futures Index, a
measure of the prices of 20 commodities, up 13.5% for the year to date. But the commodities index hasbeen
up for two of the past three years while the Dow Jones Industrial Average is coming off three losing years.
Professional investors use commodities to provide a cushion against stock-market drops. But for individuals,
investing in commodities can be a risky strategy. One of the most common techniques involves borrowing
heavily to buy a future shipment of a particular commodity, like pork bellies. If you guess wrong -- and the
price is far different from what you expected -- you can end up owing more money than you put in initially.
This is also an expensive time to get into the market, with so many commodity prices up sharply this year.
John Elliott, an engineer from Las Vegas, has been investing in commodities for about 20 years. Mr. Elliott
says he's made a small profit over the long run trading options and futures pegged to currencies, interest
rates, and commodities like grains and livestock. "But most people don't realize, you spend a lot more time
keeping up with this sort of investment than you do with stock trades," he says.
Still, many investment experts recommend that individuals keep 5% to 10% of their portfolios in commodities.
That's because when stocks are sinking, commodities like gold or oil -- both of which thrive in rocky
economies -- are often climbing. Last year, for example, gold prices rose 25% while stocks tumbled.
Because of the potential for big losses, investing in commodities is most commonly done by high-net-worth
individuals with seven-figure portfolios. But there are a variety of tactics, from investing in mutual funds that
specialize in commodities to buying gold outright, that are better suited to conservative investors or those
with smaller sums. Here's a rundown of different approaches.
FUNDS
The simplest way to own commodities is to own a broad-based commodity fund like the Rogers Raw Material
Fund or the Price Fund. Both of these invest directly in baskets of commodities, have minimum investments
of $5,000 to $10,000, and are regulated by both the Securities and Exchange Commission and the
Commodity Futures Trading Commission. These two funds are relatively new, however, and they don't yet
have all the necessary approvals from state regulators to be sold in every state.
There are also traditional mutual funds focused on shares of commodity-producing companies. Fidelity
Investments, for example, offers the Select Portfolios family of funds: It has separate funds for agriculture,
gold, natural gas, and utilities and numerous other commodities. Other sector-specific funds include the
Oppenheimer Real Assets Fund, which makes investments that track an energy index.
Investors who want more information on commodity funds can find rankings and other information free of
charge on the site of asset management and consulting firm Barclay Trading Group, at www.barclaygrp.com.
The industry group Managed Funds Association also has investor-friendly areas on its site, www.mfainfo.org.
METALS
This is perhaps the easiest area of commodities for the average investor to get into. Gold tends to thrive
during times of political unrest, war or other uncertainty that causes investors to lose faith in "paper assets"
like stock or currency.

--- Page 171 ---
Page 171 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Gold has rallied since the 9/11 terror attacks, and after a brief downturn this spring, is back near a
seven-year high. Gold futures are up 9.1% on the Nymex's Comex division so far this year, leading other
metals higher. Silver futures are up 4.4%. And platinum futures are up 26.3%.
For investors who want direct exposure to metals prices without owning mining shares, the question is
whether to store the metal, which can be cumbersome, or use one of several financial instruments.
The U.S. Mint sells American Eagle coins made of each of those metals via its online store at
www.usmint.gov, though the coins sell at big premiums to the price of the underlying metals. There's also a
surging mutual-fund sector focused on mining companies, especially gold producers. Funds include the Van
Eck International Investor Gold Fund, based in New York, and a variety of offerings from U.S. Global
Investors Inc., in San Antonio.
According to the market-data firm Lipper, gold-oriented stock funds are up 41.8% this year, compared with a
23.2% rise among all diversified U.S. stock funds.
As an alternative to gold futures, portfolio manager Peter Schiff, of Euro Pacific Capital Inc., offers his clients
certificates sold through a recently launched program operated by the government-owned Perth Mint in
Australia. The minimum investment in the certificates, which convey ownership of gold in the mint's vaults, is
$10,000. "It's handy, because it saves you any transportation or storage costs, plus it's held outside the U.S.,
in case you're worried about anything bad happening to the financial system," says Mr. Schiff.
COMPANY SHARES
The most common way for investors to play rising energy prices is to buy shares of an energy company such
as Exxon Mobil or BP. Exxon Mobil is up about 5% so far this year while BP has risen 4.5%.
To invest in the lumber industry, one of the best approaches is to buy a forest-products company such as
Georgia-Pacific Corp. or International Paper Co.
There's a downside to this method, according to some investors: Buying shares in a specific company
exposes investors to things like management problems or corporate governance issues that have nothing to
do with commodity prices.
TRADING FUTURES
Investors who want direct exposure to commodities typically buy and sell futures, the riskiest of all commodity
investments because so much leverage is used.
To play the futures market, investors generally go through a broker like Cargill Investor Services in Chicago
or Fimat USA in New York. The broker then places orders for them on exchanges such as the Chicago Board
of Trade, where future contracts are traded for everything from soybeans to Treasury bonds.
Before choosing a brokerage firm for futures trades, check its regulatory history with the National Futures
Association. NFA is the commodity industry's self-regulatory organization, sort of the equivalent of the
National Association of Securities Dealers in the stock market. NFA's online database of broker arbitrations
and disciplinary actions is at http://www.nfa.futures.org/basicnet/Search.aspx . The association's home page,
www.nfa.futures.org, has a lot of general background about trading futures.

STOCKS HIT THEIR highest levels in months -- in the case of some stock indexes, their highest levels in

for economic gains.
Technology stocks once again were among the leaders, with the Nasdaq Composite Index hitting its highest

this year alone. Makersof computer chips and related products rose nearly 4% on the day, amid talk that
strong demand for chips is a classic sign of an economic recovery.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, less dominated by tech companies, rose 57.34 points, or 0.59%, to
9858.46, its highest finish in 17 months. It is up 18% this year.
"The construction and manufacturing data were way stronger than expected," said Todd Clark, head of listed
trading at Wells Fargo Securities, a brokerage arm of Wells Fargo &Co. "The evidence that the economy is
accelerating has given people enough confidence in coming earnings to be buyers at these levels."
He also pointed out that last Friday marked the end of the fiscal year for mutual-fund companies. Mutual-fund
managers sometimes take the opportunity to sell stocks at the end of October and realize profits. Now, he
said, with November under way, those same money managers are putting money back into stocks.
The Russell 2000 small-stock index hit its highest level since September 2000, more than three years ago. It
rose 1.82%, or 9.62 points, to 537.84, up 40% so far this year. The combination of low interest rates and a
rebounding economy is thought to be particularly good for small stocks, which depend more on loans than do
larger companies.
The dollar rebounded on the signs of economic strength, while gold prices fell.
Treasury bonds fell as money shifted toward stocks. The signs of growth reinforced worries that interest
rates are unlikely to fall further and, sooner or later, will have to rise, which is bad for the prices of existing
bonds.
The economic news was stronger even than some optimists expected. Economists had projected a 0.4% rise
in construction spending for September, and instead it rose 1.3%, with spending on private homes again
taking the lead.
Manufacturing activity for October jumped to its strongest level since the end of 1999, according to a
manufacturing index compiled by the Institute for Supply Management.
This gave investors more confidence in last week's preliminary Commerce Department estimate that the
economy grew at an inflation-adjusted 7. 2% in the third quarter, the fastest quarterly growth in nearly 20
years. Investors wondered whether such high growth might be hard to sustain, and the strong construction
and manufacturing data reinforced hopes that, while the economy isn't likely to keep growing that fast, growth
may remain robust.
Now, investors are awaiting the October employment data, due this coming Friday. Those data will give
insights into one element of a recovery that hasn't yet kicked in, which is job creation.
The 10-year Treasury note fell 16/32, or $5 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which moves inversely to
price, rose to 4.355%. The 30-year bond was down 21/32 to yield 5.178%.

Gold Prices Decline on Selling By Dealers, Speculative Funds
Commodities Report
Gold Prices Decline on Selling By Dealers, Speculative Funds
By Gavin Maguire
543 words
4 November 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Gold futures fell on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange as dealers and
speculative funds were spurred to sell by gains in the dollar.
The December contract dropped $7.50 to $377.10 a troy ounce.
The dollar was propelled higher by better-than-expected manufacturing and construction data that offered
further signs that a recovery was laying roots in the U.S. economy.
This in turn tarnished gold's allure as a safe-haven asset,while at the same time the rising currency rendered
U.S. dollar-denominated gold more expensive to non-U.S. consumers.
As a result, sellers grew in number and the amount of buyers dwindled as the session progressed.
Losses were extended by stop-loss sell orders -- sales orders placed in advance -- just below recent lows
around $380. That source of selling engineered the December contract's slide to a 10-day low of $376.50.
Bernard Hunter, a director at Scotia Mocatta in Toronto, argued that further weakness could be on the
agenda for gold prices over the coming days should the U.S. dollar extend its gains.
"It all depends on the dollar for the next while. If it keeps rising we could definitely get more selling," he said.
However, he added that physical buying from centers of demand such as India and Southeast Asia should
remain steady, if not pick up as prices drop, ensuring that gold doesn't gather too much more speed to the
downside.
"I don't see demand just dropping away anytime soon, so I don't expect prices to fall. Maybe just grind lower,"
he added.
Leonard Kaplan, president of money-management firm Prospector Asset Management, argued that further
resting sell orders just below $375 may be targeted over the short term by bullion banks.
In the event those orders are activated, he allowed for prices to drop to the $372 region or potentially $370
before steadying again over the longer run.
"Today's move was not surprising given the dollar's immense gains, and we could certainly see a little more
[selling] before this move is done," he argued.
"But beyond there I think there's still uncertainty about the U.S. economy, and that will keep the funds long in
gold," he added.
Speculative funds held a net long position, or collective bet on higher prices, of 90,613 lots as of Oct. 28.
In other commodity markets:
CORN: Chicago Board of Trade futures fell as previous buyers sold some of their holdings. The December
contract fell 7.25 cents to $2.40 a bushel. "The aggressive accumulation of long positions by large speculative
traders during the market's recent run to five-month highs was hard to sustain in the face of a lack of fresh
supportive inputs," said Shawn McCambridge, senior grain analyst with Prudential Securities in Chicago.
NATURAL GAS: Nymex futures fell after trading above $5 a million British thermal units in early electronic
trade. Warm temperatures in the Northeast, ample storage and bearish weather forecasts continue to weigh
on the market. December futures dropped 18.8 cents to $4.705.
