THE ECONOMY GREW faster than it has in nearly 20 years, but investors shrugged.
Traders blamed the fact that stocks already had jumped in anticipation of a strong economic number, that
many mutual funds were ending their accounting years this week and that indexes are bumping up against
recent highs. Furthermore, even optimistic economists expect economic growth to slow now.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose only 12.08 points, or 0.12%, to 9786.61, up 17%on the year and less
than 30 points from its 17-month high of 9812.98, hit Oct. 14.
The dollar, which had hit a three-year low against the yen on Wednesday, rebounded. Treasury notes fell on
news of the economy's 7.2% third-quarter annualized growth rate. The jump reinforced fears that an
economic recovery will push interest rates up, hurting bonds. Gold fell.
But the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.11%, or 1.17 points, to 1046.94 in heavy trading. Four times
during the day, the big-stock index edged through its 17-month high of 1050.07, also hit this month. It fell
back each time, but still is up 19% on the year.
"We still haven't seen the pickup in jobs. That still might scare people a little bit," said Todd Leone, head of
listed trading at New York brokerage firm SG Cowen. "And it is the end of the fiscal year for mutual funds."
Some mutual-fund managers sell losers, take profits or rebalance portfolios at the end of their fiscal years.
The Nasdaq Composite Index fell 0.20%, or 3.87 points, to 1932.69, still up 45% on the year.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.14%, or 0.19 point, to 133.92.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. On the Big Board, where 1.62 billion shares traded, 1,647 stocks rose and 1,634 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 13/32, or $4.0625 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, rose to 4.351%. The 30-year bond was down 11/32 to yield 5.206%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 108.74 yen, up from 108.26, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.1635 from $1.1670.

So Much for Recession
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
So Much for Recession
506 words
31 October 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Yesterday's blowout 7.2% third-quarter growth report surprised even Wall Street's most optimistic forecasters.
Preliminary estimates from the Commerce Department are often later revised up or down, but news of the
fastest growth since 1984 is hard even for the pessimists to explain away.
Parsing the numbers a bit, we'd note that consumer spending kept up its end by rising at a 6.6% clip, the
fastest pace in 15 years. But the better news is that business spending grew by 11.1%, the fastest rate since
the first quarter of 2000 and a sign that the long-anticipated hand-off from consumers is taking hold. Exports
were also up 9.3%, suggesting a global recovery.
It's also significant that government spending was not as large a growth component as it has sometimes been
since the 1990s bubble began to burst in the last year of the Clinton Presidency. Defense spending was
unchanged in the quarter after rising nearly 46% in the second, and overall federal spending was up just
1.4%.
Of course, this sizzling pace probably won't continue for long. Future obstacles include oil at $30 per barrel
and ongoing U.S. currency agitation, which continued yesterday. On the other hand, corporate profits seem to
be rebounding strongly, though we'll have to wait on next month's more detailed Commerce data to know for
sure.
All in all, the signs are that this recovery is not the flash in the pan that some have suggested. Inventories are
still low, and sooner or later all of the rapid growth in productivity will result in more jobs. Jobless claims are
falling, which is another good portent, but don't be surprised to see rising employment accompanied by a
rising unemployment rate.
One reason the rate is still a relatively low 6.1% is that a lot of discouraged people have stopped looking for
jobs and hence are no longer counted as unemployed. An upswing like the current one will have them
searching again. The politicians will pick the numbers that suit their arguments, but the critical issue here is
growing employment, not the unemployment rate.
The fiscal and monetary policy levers are finally doing what they are supposed to. The Bush marginal-rate
income tax cuts this year, made retroactive to January 1 and not phased in like the 2001 cuts, are having the
intended effect. The fear of deflation has also vanished. With the price of gold (nearly $390 an ounce),
commodities and other leading indicators rising, inflation may now be a bigger concern. The Fed may well
have to tighten next year, something it's historically hesitant to do in an election year. But the reason will be
credit demand from expansion, not the size of the budget deficit.
Given all that the American economy has endured the past three years -- the bursting bubble, corporate
scandals, 9/11, the war -- the miracle is how well we've done. Good policy really does matter.

Stocks Keep Rising On Hopeful Outlook For Jobs, Low Rates
Wednesday's Markets
Stocks Keep Rising On Hopeful Outlook For Jobs, Low Rates
By E.S. Browning
412 words
30 October 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS ROSE for a third consecutive day, despite a sagging dollar and another jump in gold prices.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average built on the Federal Reserve's indication Tuesday that it will keep interest
rates low for some time and that the employment picture is improving. The blue-chip average advanced
26.22 points, or 0.27%, to 9774.53. It is up 17% this year and is less than 100 points below its 16-month high
of 9812.98, hit Oct. 14.
"People are comingto grips with the idea that the economy is improving and I think that is why the market is
so resilient," said Robert Harrington, co-head of U.S. stock trading at brokerage firm UBS.
Some investors are betting that third-quarter economic-growth numbers, due today, will be strong.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index advanced 0.13%, or 1.32 points, to 1048.11. It is up 19% this
year and is less than two points short of its 16-month high of 1050.07.
But Treasury bonds gave back Tuesday's gains as some investors worried the Fed won't cut rates further and
that strong economic growth could make investors shift more money into stocks.
The dollar continued its slide, hitting a three-year low against the yen and a five-year low against the British
pound.
Gold rose to $386.80 on New York's Comex, just short of the seven-year high of $388.90 on Friday.
Stocks also got a boost from news that quarterly results at Boeing Co. and Northrop Grumman Corp.
exceeded Wall Street expectations, as both contractors benefited from military spending.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.22%, or 4.30 points, to 1936.56, up 45% for the year.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose. On the Big Board, where 1.52 billion shares traded, 2,053 stocks rose and 1,176 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 26/32, or $8.125 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, rose to 4.301%. The 30-year bond was down 1 6/32 to yield 5.183%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, the currency traded at 108.26 yen, down from 108.33 yen, while the
euro rose against the dollar to $1.1670 from $1.1668.

Chip Shares Rise on Upbeat Outlook
Small-Stock Focus
Chip Shares Rise on Upbeat Outlook
By Cynthia Schreiber Dow Jones Newswires
923 words
29 October 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Small-capitalization stocks rose with the broad market, finishing at session highs with the aid
of semiconductor issues, after the Federal Reserve left interest rates unchanged.
Semiconductor-equipment makers advanced after Taiwan Semiconductor Manufacturing, the world's largest
chip foundry, posted better-than-expected third-quarter earnings.
Axcelis Technologies rose $1.21, or 14%, to $10.01, after the chip-equipment company said it expects an
increase in fourth-quarter orders as the industry enters a recovery stage. The Beverly, Mass., company
reported a loss of 32 cents a share for the third quarter, compared with break-even results a year earlier.
Rudolph Technologies, a provider of process-control metrology systems used by semiconductor-device
makers, jumped 3.20, or 16%, to 23.25, after the Flanders, N.J., company reported better-than-expected
third-quarter earnings.
Small-cap stock indexes finished at their highest levels of the session following the Federal Reserve's widely
expected decision to leave its key interest rate at a 45-year low of 1%. The central bank also indicated that
interest rates would remain low "for a considerable period," which relieved investors, said Bill Strazzullo,
market strategist at State Street.
The Russell 2000 Index rose 10.50 points, or 2%, to 525.85, and the Standard &Poor's SmallCap 600 Index
jumped 4.54 points, or 1.8%, to 254.78. The Nasdaq Composite Index of large-cap and small-cap stocks
gained 49.35 points, or 2.6%, to 1932.26.
Cigarette companies rose after R.J. Reynolds Tobacco Holdings and British American Tobacco's Brown
&Williamson agreed to combine their operations. Carolina Group jumped 45 cents, or 1.8%, to 25.10, and
Vector Group advanced 26 cents, or 1.7%, to 15.54, both on the NYSE.
Stock in the nation's railroads rose in value after Bear Stearns upgraded the group to "overweight" from
"market weight," saying the shares reflect high fuel prices and operational issues. Kansas City Southern
gained 57 cents, or 4.4%, to 13.50 on the Big Board, though the brokerage firm said it sees modest upside for
small-cap stocks in the group.
Gold-related issues slipped as the price of gold eased. Meridian Gold shed 37 cents, or 2.9%, to 12.38, and
Ashanti Goldfields fell 12 cents, or 1.1%, to 11.08, both on the Big Board.
Advertising stocks were strong performers after both Omnicom Group and WPP Group turned in positive
third-quarter results. However, the small-cap performance was mixed, with Catalina Marketing rising 53
cents, or 3.2%, to 16.93 on the NYSE, and DoubleClick easing two cents to 8.58.
Valassis Communications lost 91 cents, or 3.4%, to 25.95 on the NYSE, after reporting an 18% decline in
third-quarter net profit and lowering its guidance for the fourth quarter.
Merger-and-acquisition deals pushed two small-cap stocks higher. On-Site Sourcing shot up 69% to 2.45, up
1, after the Alexandria, Va., office-management outsourcing company agreed to sell itself to closely held
Docuforce in a cash deal valued at about $16 million, or $2.75 a share. This represents a premium of 90%
over On-Site's closing price Monday of $1.45 a share. On-Site also narrowed its loss during the third quarter.
Bank of Bermuda advanced 4.89, or 12%, to 44.89, as HSBC Holdings agreed to buy the Hamilton, Bermuda,
financial-services holding company for $1.3 billion, or $45 a share in cash, representing a 13% premium over
Bank of Bermuda's closing price Monday of $40.

Ditech Communications jumped 2.48, or 28%, to 11.30, as the Mountain View, Calif.,
telecommunications-equipment provider raised its second-quarter revenue outlook, citing a rise in key
accounts and an increase in year-end budget spending.

to $73.5 million, helped by sales of the company's cholesterol products.

China Saps Commodity Supplies
China Saps Commodity Supplies
By Peter Wonacott
1,678 words
24 October 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
CHINA imported 110.67 million tons of iron ore and 9.11 million carats of diamonds in the first nine months of
the year. A chart accompanying a Money &Investing article on Friday about China's emergence as a major
importer of raw materials incorrectly gave the volume of iron ore in billions of tons and the diamond volume in
millions of tons.
(WSJ October 29, 2003)
(END)
Beijing -- FROM STEAK TO IRON ore to cotton to diamonds, China's rising urban incomes, changing
consumer tastes and increased exports are reshaping the world's commodity markets. The country's
emergence as a major importer of raw materials is driving global prices higher and catching some suppliers
flat-footed.
Consider the Texan Bar and Grill in Beijing, tucked away in a cavernous Holiday Inn. The waiters wear
cowboy hats, boots and blue jeans to serve Beijing diners the restaurant's signature dish: grilled U.S. rib-eye
steak. In a sign of the prosperous times in China, a growing number of Chinese can afford a substantial steak
lunch, and have developed a substantial taste for one as well. China's beef imports hit record levels last year.
And global beef exporters are licking their chops.
U.S. and Canadian agricultural officials expect China to import 30,000 metric tons of beef next year -- and
more than 350,000 tons in 10 years, making China "our No. 1 engine of growth," says Ted Haney, president
of the Canada Beef Export Federation.
Similarly, on a small island near Shanghai, Baogang Steel Group Co., the nation's biggest steel producer, has
carved a new deep-water harbor to accommodate huge freighters from Brazil and Australia supplying iron
ore, a crucial material used in making the steel for the auto and construction industries. This year, China will
import 150 million tons of iron ore, surpassing Japan as the world's largest consumer of the commodity.
"China has sucked the cupboard bare of raw materials," says Jim Lennon, executive director of commodities
research at Macquarie Bank Ltd. in London. Because of its fast growth, Mr. Lennon cautions, China "is
starting to place severe strains on the global raw-materials supply chain." Those strains are driving
commodity prices sharply higher.
The global rise in commodity prices has reduced fears of a very different China-related idea: that the nation's
cheap exports would push the global economy into a deflationary spiral. In the U.S., prices of nonpetroleum
imports fell 0.9% in the 12 months ended September 2002. But in the 12 months ended September 2003,
nonpetroleum import prices rose 0.9%. "We were importing deflation from China a year ago and now we're
importing inflation," says Gerald D. Cohen, senior economist at Merrill Lynch &Co. in New York. (The
deflation pressures that Mr. Cohen discusses existed only in the durable-goods sector; the U.S. economy
didn't experience economywide deflation.)
The deflation debate has waned because prices of many natural resources and metals are surging, thanks to
heavy Chinese demand. In the past year and a half, the cost of alumina, used to make aluminum, and nickel,
which is used to make standard steel, has doubled, according to metals traders and analysts. Though it is the
world's largest steel producer, steel-hungry China can't get enough scrap metal, contributing to a world-wide
rise in steel-scrap prices. In the U.S., Nucor Corp., which relies on scrap steel, cited rising scrap prices for a
59% drop in profit in the third quarter ended Oct. 4 and the expectation of tough fourth-quarter results.
World-wide metal inventories are at their lowest level ever, because big metal suppliers were reducing their
capacity due to the economic slowdown in the U.S., Japan and Europe. But now, these companies are

--- Page 184 ---
Page 184 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
rushing to fill China's voracious needs. Global shipping-freight rates have skyrocketed in response to Chinese
demand.
China's auto and construction craze is underpinning the global metals buying. Aside from iron ore, the country
is so starved for copper that Chinese companies are importing French francs and melting them down,
according to a Beijing official at China Metals &Minerals Corp. Even old computers are being picked clean of
components and melted down for copper, gold and platinum. China's imports of scrap copper, which can be
used in air conditioners, have increased 40% in the first eight months of the year.
"Many people in this industry are doing whatever they can do to meet the crazy demand," says Chen Tong, a
researcher at Antike Information Co., a trading company in Beijing.
In part, China's surging commodity imports are a reflection of its booming exports, as factories import raw
materials they need to make their finished goods. With China's textile factories hoping overseas quotas will
soon be lifted, China's cotton-import volume has climbed nearly sevenfold in the year's first nine months. As
China has become the world's largest wood-furniture maker, it has replaced Japan as the biggest consumer
of Siberian timber, taking in 80% of those exports.
But urbanization and rising household incomes in China are also behind the surge. In September, Chinese
crude-oil imports grew almost 60% from the previous year, leaving year-to-date imports up about 30%. The
9.8 million metric tons of oil China imported in September was the most in one month ever. China is expected
to import a record 80 million tons of crude oil this year, up from 70 million tons in 2002.
The oil is being burned for electricity to power China's factories. But it is also fueling China's growing embrace
of the automobile. Vehicle sales in China increased 30% in the first nine months of this year from year-earlier
levels.
Chinese manufacturers are exporting furniture, but they are also racing to meet growing domestic demand as
city dwellers decide it is now chic to have wooden furniture in their homes. Gold and platinum jewelry are
becoming popular, increasing those imports. China has become Asia's second-largest consumer of diamonds
after Japan. Heightening the fad: dental clinics in China's big cities that now glue the glittering stones to
patients' teeth.
China's imports of polyethylene are also growing as China's industry responds to the growing popularity of
plastic products and plastic packaging. Polyethylene imports into Shanghai reached 150,000 tons in Januarya
105% rise from more than double the year-earlier level.
Rising disposable incomes and mushrooming restaurants are creating markets barely visible a few years ago.
Despite problems with China's agricultural inspection and quarantine regime, U.S. farm exporters are selling
large quantities of cotton, soybeans and apples to China.
And, now, plenty of potatoes. In the past two years, China has climbed from nowhere to become the
fourth-biggest market for frozen potatoes, according to Pat Boss, executive director of the Washington State
Potato Commission.
"They are buying the french fries they couldn't afford before," he says.
China's cutting of import tariffs, in line with promises it made to join the World Trade Organization, has also
reduced costs of foreign goods and increased the incentive to buy them. China's import growth this year has
outpaced the country's booming exports.
For now, all the buying is helping to prop up prices at a time when the biggest economies, North America,
Japan and Europe, remain in the doldrums. But the rise in demand has been so sharp that some analysts are
starting to worry about what happens when China's economy cools. Some hope that middle-class Chinese
could pick up the slack when industrial demand tapers off.
Average urban-household incomes in China have increased 11% a year since 1999, according to a report
from Goldman Sachs published this week. And paying on credit is becoming common. Almost nonexistent
five years ago, consumer loans now account for about 8% of China's total.
But as suppliers build up capacity to meet China's demand, they are tying themselves to an economy many
fear is feeding on easy credit that could soon dry up. A sharp decline in loans could derail the property and
auto sectors and undercut purchases of raw materials. Just as China single-handedly created a global
shortage for several commodities, some analysts fear global prices could collapse with a pullback in Chinese
buying.

--- Page 185 ---
Page 185 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The softening in commodity prices may happen as early as January, predicts Andy Xie, regional economist in
Hong Kong for Morgan Stanley. Those declines, in turn, could signal a broader slowdown. "The ultimate test
for cooling is when commodity prices begin to decline," Mr. Xie says. "We have not seen that yet."
In the meantime, Chinese companies are scrambling for supplies. Through the 1990s, Wei Fangzhi, head of
steel supplier Shanxi Sanlian Group, produced pig iron to make simple metal castings. The boom in auto
demand prompted him last year to start producing high-grade steel for car engines. Profits are exponentially
higher. Yet the industry's competition has become so intense and the scramble for imported materials so
fierce that the company is fighting to survive the scramble.
"There are already too many companies making almost the same products," says Mr. Wei. "The market will
crush some."
---
Karen Richardson in Hong Kong and Phelim Kyne in Beijing contributed to this article.
---

Gone in the Blink of a Bull's Eye --- Short-Sellers See Profits Dwindle As Market Turns for the Better; Their Ranks Are Sure to Shrink
Heard on the Street
Gone in the Blink of a Bull's Eye --- Short-Sellers See Profits Dwindle As Market Turns for the Better;
Their Ranks Are Sure to Shrink
By Ken Brown
1,252 words
22 October 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THEY HELPED UNMASK Enron, they were the first to scream about insane stock-market valuations, and
they made money for investors during the bear market. Short-sellers have been heroes these past few years.
Now they are back to their old roles: the market's goats.
Short-sellers are on track to have their worst year in at least the past decade, a consequence of the market's
rebound but also due to low interest rates, the astonishing breadth of this year's rally, a misreading of the
corporate-debt markets and just plain bad selection.
Through the end of September, short-sellers are down nearly 23%, according to the CSFB/Tremont
Dedicated Short Bias Index, their worst performance since the index began in 1994.
The reason behind most of the short's pain is obvious: The stocks they are betting against have gone up, a
lot. The 50 stocks in the Standard &Poor's 500 stock index that had the largest short positions at the
beginning of the year are up an average of nearly 33% through Friday, according to the quantitative group at
Credit Suisse First Boston. If you had shorted those 50 stocks at the start of the year, you would be trailing
the S&P 500 by 41%, CSFB says.
Short-sellers borrow shares of stock from a broker and then sell them in the market. They are betting that the
share price falls and that they can buy the stock back at a lower price, return the borrowed shares and pocket
the difference. While critics of shorts view them all as a vast conspiracy, the reality is there are very few full
time short-sellers. Harry Strunk, a West Palm Beach, Fla., investment consultant who tracks short-seller
performance, estimates that full-time short sellers control about $1 billion, spread primarily among eight or
nine major players.
After this year, that figure could dwindle. "The number of us are almost nonexistent and will be reduced
further," one major short-seller said.
Names such as nVidia Corp., Capital One Financial Corp., Calpine Corp., Pixar Animation Studios, Netflix
Inc., Nextel Communications Inc., Commerce Bancorp Inc., Sears, Roebuck &Co. and AmeriCredit Corp. are
the reason. All elicit streams of profanities from short-sellers who think the stocks are due for a fall. But in all
cases, they have risen, often strongly.
With the market going up broadly, choosing shorts can be counterproductive. "There are a lot of short
candidates, but it's almost as if I don't even want to play right now in the stock-selection game" says David
Tice, a well-known short-seller who manages the Prudent Bear mutual fund, which is designed to be a hedge
against a falling stock market. Mr. Tice, whose fund is down just 10% because of a timely bet on gold, is
shorting the S&P 500 and the Nasdaq indexes rather than individual stocks.
Even shorts that have worked -- meaning stocks that have fallen -- have been tough to profit on. Computer
Associates International Inc., Biovail Corp., and American Pharmaceutical Partners Inc. are all short favorites
and have fallen recently, but not until they had risen enough to make the shorts sweat.
"American Pharmaceutical is still at 29 bucks," Mr. Tice says. "Had you been [shorted it] any time before Aug.
7, you would have lost money; there's a lot of people who though that was a short from April 'til Aug. 7 who
are still down dramatically, so it's been slim pickings in terms of stocks that have been working."
Another example is Computer Associates, which the shorts have hated for years. Earlier this month, the
company, which is under investigation by securities regulators, said it made accounting errors, and its shares
tumbled. But that was after they had doubled this year. "You might have called it exactly right but you still
have lost money or maybe you made a point or two," Mr. Tice says. "It makes it pretty difficult."

--- Page 192 ---
Page 192 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Short-sellers didn't do that badly during the bubble. In 1999, with the market soaring, they were down 14%,
their worst year before this one, but in 2000, they rebounded strongly, rising nearly 16%. The difference,
short-sellers point out, is that during the bubble, tech stocks were the only ones rocketing, the rest of the
market struggled. Today, everything is going up.
At the peak of the market in March 2000, just 6% of the stocks on the New York Stock Exchange were trading
at 52-week highs, according to Ned Davis Research, while 16% were hitting lows. In some weeks, 2% of
shares hit highs. Last week by contrast, 24% of the stocks on the NYSE hit 52-week highs and only 0.6% hit
lows.
While short-sellers are a small group, so-called long-short hedge funds that both buy and short stocks are far
larger, controlling billions of dollars in assets. They, too, are having a weak year, at least relative to the
market, which raises the question of whether investors will continue their love affair with these largely
unregulated investment pools.
The CSFB Tremont Long/Short Equity Index was up nearly 10% through the end of September, compared
with a rise of 11% for the Dow Jones Industrial Average, 35% for the Nasdaq and 18% for stock mutual
funds. While the long-short funds did beat the market over the past three years, they still lost money in 2001
and 2002.
"The real question is whether the institutional investors who place money with hedge funds will be tolerant
when hedge funds underperform in a bull market," says Jason Trennert, a strategist with market research firm
International Strategy &Investment.
Long-short hedge funds are dragging because they were at their most bearish when the market bottomed in
March. According to ISI's monthly survey of hedge-fund managers, bullish sentiment reached its low in
March, just when the market began to rise. Since then, managers have played catch-up, becoming
increasingly bullish, meaning they covered their shorts and used the money to buy stocks outright.
With the market already rising based on improving earnings, the short-covering just added further juice to the
rally. "We saw these things coming together and thought the sum of these two things could be greater than
their individual parts," Mr. Trennert says.
The bond market also played a role in the short-sellers' suffering. First, bond investors suddenly started
lending money to struggling companies, giving many of them a second chance at survival. Companies such
as Calpine rebounded once they got their balance sheets in order.
Second, low interest rates cut off one steady source of income for shorts. When these short-sellers borrow
stock, they need to put up collateral, and they earn interest on that money. In the past, shorts could make 7%
to 8% or so on their collateral, but now its just 2% to 3%, says Mr. Strunk, whose firm is Aspen Grove Capital
Management.
Not surprisingly, short-sellers say the market is heading down and expect solid returns for their funds in the
coming months. "We do feel like it's a lead-up to a decline," says Mr. Tice. "It's not that unusual in these
volatile markets for there to be rallies and manias and then a collapse."
Document J000000020031024dzam000ct

--- Page 193 ---
Page 193 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A Snow Job Caused Bush's Currency Gaffes in Asia
Global View
A Snow Job Caused Bush's Currency Gaffes in Asia
By George Melloan
1,061 words
21 October 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A27
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Both China and Japan on the weekend flatly rejected demands by George W. Bush that they adopt "market"
pricing for the yuan and yen. These refusals were mild embarrassments for the president on what has
otherwise been a successful Asian tour.
Rejection was predictable in part because Mr. Bush was on shaky ground. Guided by Treasury Secretary
John Snow and other political advisers, he had blundered into a policy arena that American presidents should
avoid like the plague. Grasping the dynamics of exchange-rate relationships is a gift granted few politicians or
anyone else. Their public pronouncements on the subject often have unwanted consequences and, at best,
sound a bit silly.
First of all, there is no "market" in fiat currencies. Their relative values rest on the arbitrary decisions of central
bankers about how much money to create. The dollar has been weak this year mainly because the U.S.
Federal Reserve Board has had, for some time, an easy money policy.
A currency's value does respond to demand, of course, but demand also is guided by market assessments of
central bank policy. If it appears that the policy is to cheapen the currency, investors head for the exits,
which of course cheapens the currency all the more.
The second myth is that a country can obtain economic advantage by devaluing its currency. If that were
really true, there would still be frequent waves of those "competitive devaluations" that were the fashion in the
1960s and 1970s before policymakers finally wised up to the nasty consequences, such as inflation, the
erosion of capital and reduced global trade and investment due to monetary uncertainties.
To be sure, a cheapening dollar increases the cash flow of multinational firms that do their bookkeeping in
dollars. They get more cash when they convert stronger currencies earned abroad to dollars.
But while this helps explain political demands for devaluation from corporate executives who want to look
good to shareholders, it is only an accounting identity, not the product of improved competitiveness. Indeed,
"earnings" thus obtained may mask the damage done to the company's market position abroad by the rising
costs, in dollars, of materials and labor. The third-quarter improvement in earnings of many U.S.-based
corporations most likely had a weak-dollar component.
Mr. Bush wanted Japan to quit supporting the dollar by buying dollars with yen. This sounds like something
out of the playbook of the 1970s, before Japanese auto companies decided to counter the threat of American
protectionism by building factories in the U.S. to produce for the American market. Japan does in fact try to
manage its currency to best serve its own economic needs, but so does every other country. Right now, the
effort to keep the yen from shooting up has little to do with seeking trade advantage and a lot to do with trying
to stop the rise of the yen from plunging Japan back into a deflationary spiral.
The Bush demands on China were even more mysterious. Treasury Secretary Snow, who presumably incited
the president's ill-fated currency crusade, has the idea that the Chinese yuan is "undervalued," which he
thinks explains the large U.S. trade deficit with China and lost U.S. jobs. But the yuan has been pegged to the
U.S. dollar since 1994, a linkage that has served China and Asia well. China has become a big market for
other Asian nations; its imports soared 40% in the year to September, compared with a 31% rise in exports,
and it may run an overall trade deficit next year. China's sales to the U.S. derive from the fact that its
producers, many of which are partly U.S. owned, utilize low production costs to offer attractive buys, thereby
helping the U.S. keep a lid on consumer prices.
Using the logic of China's critics, a case can be made that it is the dollar that's "undervalued." There is no set
standard for exchange rates, so a money's value can only be measured against real goods. Commodity price
indices denominated in dollars are up sharply from a year ago, with the Dow Jones-AIG spot index showing

--- Page 194 ---
Page 194 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
an increase of nearly 15%. Gold, another useful price standard, is up 20%. Against these standards, the
dollar has undergone a significant devaluation. Should this be a time for an American president to charge
other chieftains with "undervaluing" their currencies?
An artful term, "core inflation," masks the effect of commodity price rises on the U.S. consumer by excluding
food and energy. Is this supposed to mean that groceries and gasoline are not important in the family
budget? Americans can thank low-cost producers abroad for offsetting the effects of a weakening dollar and
holding down rises in U.S. living costs.
Mr. Bush is not the first president to go wandering off unguided into the currency maze. Lyndon B. Johnson
badgered the Fed to finance the war in Vietnam and the "War on Poverty" simultaneously. The result was the
collapse of the dollar-based Bretton Woods international monetary system. Richard M. Nixon compounded
the error with price controls, which ultimately exploded into a burst of inflation. Jimmy Carter had no wisdom
available either from his Treasury secretary or his initial central banker, and wondered whether a weak or
strong dollar was better. After it was too late to save his presidency, he learned that a weak dollar meant
inflationary poison and hired Paul Volcker to fix things.
Ronald Reagan wisely opted for a strong dollar, and kept Mr. Volcker on, with excellent economic results. Bill
Clinton, tacitly acknowledging his ignorance, wisely left monetary policy in the competent hands of Alan
Greenspan.
The younger Mr. Bush has not broken any crockery so far. But his Asian venture doesn't speak well for the
quality of the advice he is getting from Treasury. He can thank Hu Jintao and Junichiro Koizumi for warning
him away from this dangerous area of policy. The U.S. recovery is going nicely, even in manufacturing. The
stock market seems happy. And inflation is still under control. Trying to keep the dollar sound is labor
enough for an American president. Giving monetary lectures to trading partners just makes the markets
nervous.

Fading Green: Brazil's President Sees New Growth In the Rain Forest --- Dismayed Environmentalists Find Former Ally Aiming Many Projects at...
Fading Green: Brazil's President Sees New Growth In the Rain Forest --- Dismayed Environmentalists
Find Former Ally Aiming Many Projects at Jungle --- Pipeline and Dam in Amazon
By Matt Moffett
2,007 words
16 October 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
MANAUS, Brazil -- When Luiz Inacio Lula da Silva became Brazil's first elected leftist president in January,
environmentalists cheered. They regarded the co-founder of the Workers Party as progressive and "green" in
his politics.
"We believed in Lula," says Jecinaldo Satere Mawe, an indigenous leader who worked for years with
environmentalists to block Brazil's giant state oil company from running two natural-gas pipelines through part
of the Amazon rain forest.To the dismay of environmentalists, however, Mr. da Silva believes in the pipelines.
Driven by economic pressures, energy concerns and political compromises, Mr. da Silva has made reliable
domestic fuel supplies one of his government's priorities and has outlined an ambitious development program
that ranges across the Amazon region. Petroleo Brasileiro SA plans to start construction early next year of
the $1 billion project to bring gas from the vast Urucu field to the teeming cities at the jungle's edge.
Activists are mounting a last-ditch struggle to halt Petrobras, in what is shaping up as one of the first of
potentially many environmental battles for Mr. da Silva's administration. The president wants to pump billions
of dollars into highways, railroads, airports, waterways and other projects that could change the face of the
rain forest. He's even taking a second look at a long-stalled project to build one of the world's largest dams in
the Amazon.
"This region can't be treated like it was something from another world, untouchable, in which the people don't
have the right to the benefits," Mr. da Silva said in a June speech.
Opposition to pipelines has prevented Petrobras from recouping its $7 billion investment in Amazon
exploration and development. Meanwhile, energy consumers in the rest of Brazil must pay $500 million a
year to subsidize consumers in Amazon cities who use costlier and dirtier diesel fuel.
The president's program has surprised and delighted industrialists and big farmers, who expect Mr. da Silva's
leftist credentials to help break taboos over Amazon development. The pipelines are "easier to do with Lula
because he has a lot of credibility," says Virgilio Mauricio Viana, the Harvard-trained environment minister for
the state of Amazonas, which is more than twice the size of Texas and has managed to keep 98% of its rain
forest intact.
Mr. da Silva, 57 years old, is a former union leader who gained respect among Brazilians for his opposition to
the military government that ruled during the 1970s and part of the 1980s. His ties to environmentalists stem
from his friendship with his Workers Party co-founder Chico Mendes, an Amazon rubber tapper who became
an international symbol of the fight against predatory development after his murder in 1988.
Mr. da Silva made three runs for the presidency in the 1980s and 1990s, but he was considered too radical to
be elected president. His triumphant campaign last year unleashed panic on Latin American financial
markets, even though he had clearly shifted toward the center, naming a textile magnate as his running mate.
And after his election, Mr. da Silva at first cheered greens by naming as environmental minister Marina Silva,
a former senator who came from a family of Amazon rubber tappers.
On the Urucu project and many other policy matters, Mr. da Silva has advanced a more-conservative agenda
-- albeit with progressive touches -- than some of his more right-wing predecessors ever did. In his first year
in office, he has wrung inflation out of the economy with high interest rates, pressed social-security cuts
that have incensed his erstwhile union allies and drastically reduced spending to achieve a high budget
surplus. Brazil's financial markets have rallied sharply, but the economy is still weaker than Mr. da Silva would
like, which is one reason he's interested in promoting infrastructure projects.
By nature, "Lula is a pragmatist," says Paulo Adario, Amazon campaign coordinator for Greenpeace. Mr.
Adario says that Mr. da Silva's aggressive energy-development policy is colored by the experience of his

--- Page 196 ---
Page 196 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
predecessor, Fernando Henrique Cardoso, who endured an energy crisis that forced drastic nationwide
rationing and sapped support for his administration.
The president's pro-development stance also reflects his Workers Party's newfound political pragmatism. The
party had shunned alliances with other parties in Mr. da Silva's three failed presidential bids, but last year it
formed coalitions. One of its partners, the leftist Popular Socialist Party, elected Eduardo Braga, an electrical
engineer and staunch pipeline advocate, as governor of Amazonas.
Mr. da Silva chilled environmentalists last month when he unveiled his $66 billion, four-year
infrastructure-development plan. The "Brazil for Everyone" program includes plans to double the current
generating capacity of the Tucurui hydroelectric project in the eastern Amazon, as well as a review of a
long-shelved plan to build the $4 billion Belo Monte dam. Located on the Xingu River, Belo Monte would be
the third-largest hydroelectric project in the world in terms of power generated, and would entail the flooding
of roughly 155 square miles, about half of which would be the river bed itself.
Though the government may not find financing for all of the projects, the plans alone can lead to deforestation
as land speculators and settlers pounce on prime properties, says Roberto Smeraldi, head of the Brazilian
chapter of Friends of the Earth. There is evidence that destruction had been occurring at a faster pace even
before the government's infrastructure plan came out. Recently released satellite surveys showed that a slice
of the rain forest about the size of Vermont was wiped out by farmers, cattlemen, loggers and other settlers in
the 12 months up to August 2002, the second-highest level of destruction in the 14 years records have been
kept.
The government, working with local authorities and business, is moving quickly in its efforts to launch some
projects. One priority is paving a 460-mile stretch of highway BR-163, which connects the central farm state
of Mato Grosso with the heart of the Amazon. Soybean growers say the refurbished road will allow them to
boost exports by almost $2 billion a year, but environmentalists argue that it will promote settlement and
large-scale farming of mostly untouched areas.
Petrobras, a Big Board-listed company with revenue of $22 billion last year, has earned a shaky
environmental reputation in Brazil. In 2000, separate oil-pipeline leaks in southern Brazil fouled Rio de
Janeiro's Guanabara Bay and the Iguacu River. In the latter case, Brazilian environmental regulators fined
Petrobras the equivalent of about $50 million, the largest such penalty in the country's history. The company
is still contesting the fine.
Those disasters, plus staunch opposition from Amazonas politicians and ecologists, had just about scuttled
the Urucu pipeline plan by last year. But it popped back on the agenda after the election of Mr. da Silva.
The president has tried to reduce the state oil company's monolithic, all-business reputation. He named a
geologist and Workers Party senator to run the company and placed other party loyalists in key positions.
Under the new leadership, the company has boosted its community efforts, including a hunger-eradication
campaign.
Gov. Braga says Petrobras has committed to creating a $15 million compensation fund for the affected area
that will spur social programs and sustainable industries. Petrobras says it is still negotiating on the exact
figure.
Petrobras says it knows the responsibility involved in operating in the rain forest. "The Amazon region is a
very critical region in environmental terms, [and] any intervention has to have the necessary caution," says
Ildo Luis Sauer, head of Petrobras's natural-gas operations. He says the company aims to "guarantee the
preservation of the environmental equilibrium" and "to preserve the social and cultural interests, which are an
immense patrimony."
The Urucu field, discovered in 1986, represented 20% of all of Brazil's gas reserves, until an even bigger find
earlier this year off the coast of the state of Sao Paulo. There is already one pipeline linking Urucu to the town
of Coari. Petrobras wants to build one of the new pipelines to run from Coari 280 miles northeast to the
manufacturing center of Manaus. The other new pipeline would run from Urucu 340 miles south to the
commercial and natural-resources center of Porto Velho.
Government officials say the Urucu project would create about 5,000 construction jobs, at a time when
one-third of the state's 20,000 construction workers are unemployed. But development can bring social
problems along with economic gains. Petroleum royalties are helping to build schools and hospitals in the
town of Coari, but they're also igniting frenetic growth, as well as a surge of drugs, disease and child
prostitution.
Since Petrobras began building the crude-oil pipeline in the 1990s, Coari, the Amazon's former banana
capital, has seen its population double to around 70,000. Even Gov. Braga, the pipeline booster, allows that
oil brought to Coari "problems that the American West had in the era of the gold rush."

--- Page 197 ---
Page 197 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
There are about 10 reported cases of child prostitution each month, says social worker Ediane Santos. Amid
the stilted houses on the river, police are fighting an influx of "honey," marijuana blended with coca powder.
Economic benefits have been slow to trickle down. A former mayor is under investigation by state auditors for
misuse of funds. The current mayor, Adail Pinheiro, has used the roughly $15 million in annual oil royalties to
build several air-conditioned schools, a new municipal auditorium, a hospital and 11 neighborhood health
centers. But the mayor finds that every time he solves one problem, a new one emerges. The city is coping
with a malaria epidemic that afflicts about 7% of the populace.
(MORE)
"I am amazed by these new buildings, but I always question whether we are better off than we were before
Petrobras came," says Cidronio Soares, owner of a general store. Sales have tripled since the mid-1990s and
he has branched out into selling saw blades and wheelbarrows. Yet Mr. Soares has been robbed twice and
sleeps with a tire iron close by.
"You never used to lock the door in Coari," said Mr. Soares. "I don't know if we'll survive another pipeline and
more growth."
Petrobras has been flying groups of reporters, academics and politicians to the Urucu production field, which
Petrobras has turned into an environmental showcase in recent years. The company's operations have won
numerous commendations for their high environmental standards. To replant damaged areas of the rain
forest, Petrobras maintains a nursery with 55 species of trees and 95,000 seedlings.
A bigger concern for environmentalists is what would happen deeper in the jungle, along the route where the
pipeline would be built and maintained. The pipeline routes run near Indian territories and archeological sites,
and ecologists question the company's ability or interest in keeping settlers out once the construction is
finished. Antipipeline groups also are concerned that the environmental enforcement agency, along with other
government bodies, has been hit hard by budget cuts that Mr. da Silva imposed to satisfy financial markets
and the International Monetary Fund.
On the Urucu-Porto Velho pipeline, Petrobras has been hamstrung by a federal-court decision to hold up
licensing due to deficiencies in the company's environmental-impact study. For the segment from Coari to
Manaus, Petrobras this week released its environmental-impact study, which will now be subject to public
debate.
Environmentalists hope to trap the pipelines in a procedural thicket. It takes only 50 signatures on a petition to
force a hearing, and the Pastoral Land Commission, a social group linked to the Roman Catholic Church,
says it will circulate petitions in every hamlet in Amazonas, trying to instigate many hearings.

Give Up Caesar: Court Tests Its Limits in Fraud Case
Give Up Caesar: Court Tests Its Limits in Fraud Case
By Mitchell Pacelle
1,322 words
14 October 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THREE YEARS AND eight months ago, a federal judge in New York sent Martin A. Armstrong to jail in an
effort to coerce the New Jersey investment adviser to cough up about $15 million worth of missing gold bars,
ancient coins and other valuables connected to an alleged financial fraud.
So began a standoff that is now testing the limits of the court's power to pressure accused corporate
wrongdoers to surrender assets or information. Mr. Armstrong's lawyer has complained that his lengthy
detention for civil contempt not only violates his Fifth Amendment privilege against self-incrimination, but has
also lost its coercive power, becoming merely punitive.
If Mr. Armstrong's legal challenge succeeds, it could affect the government's ability to use contempt-of-court
rulings to pressure accused corporate wrongdoers who are reluctant to turn over information to government
lawyers.
"Generally speaking, the government doesn't want the courts defining what is too long" to detain someone for
contempt, said Nancy Luque, a Washington criminal defense lawyer.
Lawyers say Mr. Armstrong's stay in jail for contempt is the longest such detention they can recall in a federal
case. In a state court proceeding, former Philadelphia lawyer H. Beatty Chadwick has been sitting in jail for
more than seven years for civil contempt for refusing to divulge the location of $2.5 million of marital assets in
a nasty divorce case.
The latest appeal by Mr. Armstrong, who is 53 years old, turns on several knotty areas of law. The law states
that such detentions can't last forever -- and are limited to 18 months in certain circumstances -- but it leaves
considerable discretion in the hands of the court. In addition, the appeal involves the complex intersection of
Fifth Amendment rights and Supreme Court case law holding that corporate custodians can't use the Fifth
Amendment to refuse court orders to turn over corporate items.
Both issues are important to the government's efforts to prosecute corporate fraud, according to Thomas
Sjoblom of Chadbourne &Parke who represents Mr. Armstrong on his appeal. "The SEC knows that if I
prevail on this issue, one of their enforcement tools has been taken away from them," he said.
Wayne Carlin, head of the New York office of the Securities and Exchange Commission, said he disagrees.
"This is a case that will turn on its own highly unusual facts," he said. "We don't envision it having any wider
programmatic ramifications."
Lawyers for the SEC and Mr. Armstrong have exchanged briefs on the issues, but the federal appellate court
hasn't yet scheduled oral arguments.
U.S. District Judge Richard Owen, an 81-year-old jurist with a reputation for toughness -- who also happens
to be the judge in the current trial of Wall Street financier Frank Quattrone -- has declined thus far to release
Mr. Armstrong, as has the appellate court.
That pleases the New York lawyer serving as receiver of Mr. Armstrong's company, who has complained that
Mr. Armstrong's statements about a hoard of missing assets -- including 102 gold bars, 699 gold bullion
coins and a $750,000 bust of Julius Caesar -- have been misleading and incomplete.
As a result, Mr. Armstrong remains confined to a jail in lower Manhattan in the company of criminals deemed
too dangerous to be granted bail. In his periodic court appearances, he has shown no signs of wavering.
"Your Honor, this is a Mexican standoff," he told Judge Owen in June. "It's going to stay that way."
Mr. Armstrong, a Princeton, N.J., market forecaster and investment adviser, was arrested in September 1999
and charged with criminal fraud for allegedly bilking 139 Japanese institutional investors out of as much as $1

--- Page 199 ---
Page 199 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
billion. Simultaneous fraud suits by the SEC and the Commodity Futures Trading Commission prompted a
federal judge to turn over control of his company, Princeton Economics International Ltd., to a receiver. (The
company had no relation to Princeton University.)
Almost from the moment Mr. Armstrong was ordered to surrender assets, his story has been marked by
peculiar plot twists. In January 2000, Judge Owen ruled that the order wouldn't violate Mr. Armstrong's Fifth
Amendment rights.
Days later, Mr. Armstrong pulled up to the Manhattan office of the receiver, Alan M. Cohen, in a borrowed
car. He unloaded a hoard of antiquities, including six ancient Roman busts, several boxes of gold coins,
ancient bracelets and earrings, and some small bronze knives that looked like an ancient shaving kit.
But the receiver later told the court that about $15 million worth of gold bars and coins, the bust of Caesar,
and a computer hard drive were still missing. During a court hearing that Jan. 14, Mr. Armstrong maintained
that after first storing the gold bars under a sofa in his living room, he had given them to a business associate
in Japan. He said he had given some of the coins and the Caesar bust to an associate in Australia, and had
given the gold bullion coins as "gifts and bonuses to employees around the world."
Judge Owen, finding the explanations not credible, held him in contempt for his failure to produce "a lot of
stuff," including a computer hard drive and other assets. He ordered Mr. Armstrong detained for as many as
18 months, until the assets were produced or "we get better explanations of why we don't have them."
Mr. Armstrong appealed, arguing, among other things, that the turnover order violated his Fifth Amendment
rights. The appellate court dismissed the appeal, saying it lacked jurisdiction because Judge Owen's
contempt finding wasn't a final order.
When the first detention period ended, Judge Owen extended the confinement for an additional 18 months,
ruling that Mr. Armstrong had waived his Fifth Amendment rights when he had testified earlier about some of
the assets. Mr. Armstrong's lawyer argued that he testified only after the judge ordered him to do so. Again,
Mr. Armstrong's appeal was dismissed. When that period ended, his detention was extended again.
During a court hearing last month, debate arose about whether the value of the missing assets -- about $15
million -- should have any bearing on Judge Owen's analysis of how long Mr. Armstrong could be held.
Judge Owen suggested that it might, "if you then end up getting out at some point and you can call [the
missing assets] all back."
"Well then, I guess the question is, is there a mathematical formula that is to be applied?" Bernard Kleinman,
another of Mr. Armstrong's lawyers, asked rhetorically. "A lot of people would consider a million dollars a year
quite a lot of money. So does Mr. Armstrong stay in jail for 15 years, or does he stay in jail for 30 years?"
Such analysis, Mr. Kleinman argued, was inappropriate.
Moments later, Judge Owen noted: "We're looking at $15 million, with inflation, which is now 20 [million
dollars]. That's a big pot of gold down in some backyard."
An SEC lawyer suggested that a review of the question was in order.
"The SEC would like Your Honor to consider whether the confinement of Martin Armstrong has ceased to
have a coercive effect on him," SEC lawyer Dorothy Heyl said.
A lawyer for the CFTC argued in favor of continued detention. The receiver added that if Judge Owen
decided that he could no longer hold Mr. Armstrong on civil contempt, the judge should refer the matter to the
Justice Department's U.S. attorney in Manhattan to prosecute Mr. Armstrong for criminal contempt. That
would entitle him to a trial, and the prospect of a prison term.
Judge Owen left the contempt ruling in place. The appellate court will take up the issue next.
Mr. Armstrong's criminal trial on the underlying fraud charges is currently slated for next spring.

THE OUTLOOK: Isn't This Rich? Wealthy Boost Economy
THE OUTLOOK:
The Economy
THE OUTLOOK: Isn't This Rich? Wealthy Boost Economy
By Jon E. Hilsenrath
888 words
13 October 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
New York -- WILLIAM BENBASSAT recently walked into Gearys of Beverly Hills, planning to buy a Rolex
watch. Instead, the Los Angeles businessman bought two, one gold and one platinum, spending $50,000.
Mr. Benbassat is also in the market for LeRoy Neiman paintings and is planning to take a luxury cruise to
New England.
"My spending is up quite a bit," says Mr. Benbassat, founder and chief executive of Reliable Health Care
Services Inc., an outplacement firm. That's a recent change in attitude, he says, and "I feel good about it."
It would also please some economists, who believe that spending by affluent consumers such as Mr.
Benbassat -- who have benefited more than others from this year's tax cuts, a resurgent stock market, and a
springtime home-refinancing boom -- is playing an important and overlooked role in the economy's rising
fortunes.
Dean Maki, an economist at J.P. Morgan Chase &Co., estimates consumer spending grew at a 6.1% annual
rate between February and August, the fastest pace in 16 years. And upper-income households, he says,
were the primary drivers of that surge. "Other households are getting tax cuts and their spending is rising, but
the biggest percentage gains in income and spending are being done by the rich," says Mr. Maki, a former
Fed researcher who spent years studying how consumer spending responds to changes in household wealth.
If the trend continues, it could provide a significant boost to some areas of the economy that have struggled in
the past year, including entertainment, travel and financial services, says Mr. Maki. And because affluent
consumers are big buyers of durable goods, such as cars and household appliances, their spending is an
important boost to those industries, too. Between January and August, the seasonally adjusted annual
spending rate on durable goods rose 13.5%, compared with a 1.7% increase in nondurable goods.
SALES OF PORSCHE CARS were up 96% in September from a year earlier. Coach Inc., the high-end
leather retailer, says consumers were snapping up its "Hamptons" handbags, which sell for up to $368 each.
Revenue at luxury hotels in New York and San Francisco is on the rise, while Bergdorf-Goodman, the
high-end department store on Fifth Avenue in Manhattan, reports that the six-figure shopping spree is back.
And while many average Americans remain wary about the economy, wealthy Americans feel more confident.
The University of Michigan's monthly surveys of consumer sentiment show that confidence among the
top-third of income earners in the U.S. is up 24% since February. Confidence in middle-income households is
up 10% and in the poorest households is down 2%.
The wealthy may be getting a lift from their finances. According to estimates by the Brookings Institution and
the Urban Institute, households earning between $200,000 and $500,000 in pretax income receive an
average income-tax reduction of $5,000 as a result of the tax-law changes enacted in May. Households
earning between $500,000 and $1 million receive an average reduction of $17,000, and households earning
more than $1 million, including the superrich, on average will see their tax bills go down $93,000.
But the apparent spending splurge is a puzzle for economists trying to figure out how much the affluent
classes' spending surge is due to tax cuts, versus other factors, such as stock-market gains and the
home-refinancing boom.
When the tax cuts were enacted, some economists argued that wealthy households -- who pay the most in
taxes and got the bulk of the tax cuts in dollar terms -- were unlikely to spend much of their cash windfall.
Economic theory holds that wealthy households, already flush with liquidity that they could use for
consumption at any time, are likely to sock away the bulk of any gains from tax cuts into savings. By contrast,

--- Page 201 ---
Page 201 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
theory holds that lower-income households save very little, and instead plough cash windfalls right back into
the economy in the form of spending on necessities.
But the latest jump in spending, which coincided closely with the implementation of the tax cuts, suggests
fiscal policy has been a factor.
Mr. Maki says the conventional view about the propensity of different income groups to consume might need
some refining. One 2001 study by economists Joel Slemrod and Matthew Shapiro of the University of
Michigan found that low-income households and high-income households had similar tendencies to save and
spend. "There are a lot of upper-income, two-earner families that have very substantial incomes who don't
save very much, and there are very many low- to moderate-income households who are frugal," says Mr.
Shapiro.
Mr. Benbassat, the executive, says the tax cuts helped seal his spending, though they were only part of a
complex mix that motivated him. The overall economic environment, which seems to be turning up, played a
critical role. The stock market has helped. Moreover, Mr. Benbassat, who says he typically earns more than
$500,000 a year, doesn't see his purchases of long-lasting watches and paintings as reckless spending. He
sees them as investments that will hold their value over time.
---
Patrick Barta contributed to this article.

Dow Industrials Rise A Fifth Straight Day Amid Falling Dollar
Tuesday's Markets
Dow Industrials Rise A Fifth Straight Day Amid Falling Dollar
By E.S. Browning
603 words
8 October 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTORS SHOOK OFF news of a drooping dollar and propelled major indexes to within a hair of their
15-month highs, as optimism about coming earnings reports produced a fifth consecutive session of gains.
Late in the day, some investors appeared to be using computer-driven trades to shift money out of bonds and
into stocks, and Treasury bonds fell sharply.
The dollar hit its lowest point in nearly three years against the yen, and its lowest in nearly three months
against the euro, spurring fears that foreign investors will pull back from dollar-denominated securities. Gold
rose sharply.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 59.63 points, or 0.62%, to 9654.61, just short of the 15-month high of
9659.13 hit last month. It is up almost 16% this year.
Once again, the many technology stocks in the Nasdaq Composite Index led the gains. The Nasdaq
advanced 0.76%, or 14.39 points, to 1907.85. That is up almost 43% this year and just short of the 18-month
high of 1909.55 hit last month.
But the 10-year Treasury note fell 27/32, or $8.44 for every $1,000 invested. Its yield, which rises when the
price falls, moved up to 4.264%. That still was well below the level of 4.6% hit last month, which sowed
worries then that higher market interest rates could throw a wet blanket on the economic recovery.
The sharp bond decline "is evidence that there is further asset allocation going on from fixed income and into
stocks," said Andy Brooks, head of stock trading at Baltimore mutual-fund group T. Rowe Price.
The logic among investors is simple, he said: "Investors are saying, `If the economy is improving, I had better
buy stocks.' The higher stocks go in front of these earnings, the more out on a limb you are. But if earnings do
meet or beat expectations, the bold will be rewarded."
Two other reasons for the bond decline were worries about the falling dollar and that some investors were
making room for $25 billion in new five-year and 10-year notes due to be issued today and tomorrow.
After regular trading hours, investors got positive news. Alcoa Inc. reported better-than-expected earnings
and the stock, which had declined during regular hours, rose 2% in after-hours trading.
But PepsiCo fell during regular trading hours after its earnings only matched Wall Street expectations, an
indication that investors may insist on strong earnings before they will push stocks higher.
The broad S&P 500 index rose 0.47%, or 4.90 points, to 1039.25, up 18% this year and barely short of its
15-month high of 1039.58, also hit last month.
Trading on the New York Stock Exchange once again was below average, however, suggesting that some
investors are waiting to see what third-quarter earnings reports will contain.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.29%, or 0.38 point, to 130.94.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.28 billion shares traded, 1,951 stocks rose and 1,265 fell.
Bonds declined. The 30-year bond was down 1 13/32 to yield 5.159%.

The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 109.81 yen, down from 110.91, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.1770 from $1.1718.

Israeli Airstrike In Syria Triggers Wide Criticism --- Reprisal Follows Bombing In Haifa That Killed 19; Emergency Session at U.N.
International
Israeli Airstrike In Syria Triggers Wide Criticism --- Reprisal Follows Bombing In Haifa That Killed 19;
Emergency Session at U.N.
By Guy Chazan in Jerusalem, Hugh Pope in Istanbul, Turkey, and Christopher Cooper in Washington
898 words
6 October 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The United Nations' Security Council held an emergency meeting to discuss Israel's bombing of a suspected
terror-training camp in Syria, amid fears the raid could trigger a new, regional escalation of the
Israeli-Palestinian conflict.
With the White House calling on Israel and Syria to show restraint, there was concern the strike on Ein
Saheb, about 12 miles northwest of Damascus, could provoke a fresh Middle East crisis just as the U.S. is
seeking to build international support for a new U.N. resolution creating a multinational force for Iraq.
The operation, the first by Israel deep inside Syrian territory since the 1973 Yom Kippur War, came hours
after an Islamic Jihad suicide bomber blew herself up in a crowded beachfront restaurant in Haifa Saturday,
killing 19 Israelis.
The Israeli raid triggered widespread condemnation. France and Britain said it was a violation of international
law, and Russia said it would lead to a widening of the Israeli-Palestinian conflict. The U.S., while accusing
Syria of being on the "wrong side in the war on terror," urged both countries to avoid actions that "could lead
to hostilities." In the Middle East, normally pro-American states like Jordan and Egypt were also furious, with
Arab League Secretary-General Amr Moussa denouncing the attack as "state terrorism."
The airstrike appeared to have taken the Bush administration by surprise. Though Israeli officials often
consult with the White House when preparing responses to suicide bombings, a State Department official
said the U.S. had been given no advance warning this time. He said repercussions from the raid could affect
not only the "road map" to peace, but the push for a new U.N. resolution on Iraqi reconstruction. It also comes
as the U.S. engages Syria in talks on blocking Islamic militants who have been heading over its porous desert
border into Iraq to fight U.S. forces.
The strike is the latest of a series of Israeli actions that have raised hackles at the White House. The U.S.
was critical of last month's Israeli cabinet decision -- made in principle -- to "remove" Palestinian leader
Yasser Arafat. There has also been mounting concern at Israel's plans to build a security fence deep inside
the West Bank and to expand Jewish settlements, in defiance of the "road map" to peace.
Israel described Ein Saheb as a training base used by militant organizations like Islamic Jihad and Hamas to
teach sabotage, artillery training, guerrilla warfare, and even how to fly planes. Trainees later returned to
Palestinian areas "to establish an operational terrorist infrastructure."
An Israeli army statement accused Syria of "sponsoring terrorism" by providing a safe haven for members of
groups like Islamic Jihad. "Syria has clearly emerged as the hub of the axis of terrorism that extends from Iraq
to Gaza," said Dore Gold, an adviser to Israeli Prime Minister Ariel Sharon. "Israel cannot tolerate a situation
in which Syria supports attacks against its civilians with impunity."
But Palestinians said the attack was part of an Israeli strategy of prodding the U.S. into widening its war on
terror to take in other states in the region. "Israel wasn't satisfied that America only attacked Iraq," said Saeb
Erekat, the Palestinians' chief negotiator. "It now wants it to move against Syria and Iran, too."
Normally, Israel responds to suicide attacks with targeted killings of militant leaders, or army incursions into
Palestinian areas to arrest wanted activists. This is the first time it has retaliated by attacking another country,
and observers say the move reflects Israel's mounting frustration at its seeming inability to thwart hard-line
groups.
In a letter to U.N. Secretary General Kofi Annan, Syrian Foreign Minister Faruq al-Shara urged the Security
Council to "condemn, deter and prevent the repetition of this dangerous development that threatens regional
and international security and stability." But significantly, Syria avoided any specific threat of retaliation.

--- Page 205 ---
Page 205 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Similarly, when Israeli forces struck a Syrian position in Lebanon in April 2001, killing at least two Syrian
soldiers, angry words were followed by a quiet pullback disguised as a redeployment. Syria is likely to avoid a
head-to-head conflict with the Israeli army.
Palestinian officials in Beirut said the Ein Saheb base was in fact run by Ahmed Jibril, leader of another
militant group, the Popular Front for the Liberation of Palestine-General Command, and was no longer in use.
A spokesman for Islamic Jihad said the organization had no training camps outside Palestinian territory.
Reporters who managed to approach the site -- which was cordoned off by Syrian security forces -- said the
buildings that had come under attack appeared to be deserted. Syrians familiar with the camp said it had not
been used by radical groups for some years. Arab satellite stations reported one person wounded near the
site.
Observers said the Israeli raid was in fact a low-risk affair, with a Syrian military response highly unlikely and
little danger of a serious rift with the U.S., which has itself sniped at Syria for harboring militant organizations.
Israeli analysts doubted the raid would lead to a widening of the Israeli-Palestinian conflict.

No Reason to Say `Eeek,' So Far --- Cases of `Octoberphobia' Kick In As Investors Flip a Calendar Page, But the Month Isn't Always Scary
Abreast of the Market
No Reason to Say `Eeek,' So Far --- Cases of `Octoberphobia' Kick In As Investors Flip a Calendar
Page, But the Month Isn't Always Scary
By E.S. Browning
1,924 words
6 October 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IT IS OCTOBER, a month when some people's thoughts turn to witches, goblins . . . and stock-market
declines.
Even though the Dow Jones Industrial Average is up nearly 300 points in the first three days of this month,
some experienced investors begin biting their nails at this time of year. They think back to October 1929,
when stocks crashed, and to October 1987, when they crashed again, as well as to some smaller but
still-startling October declines in other years. Sure, the worriers say, it is a little emotional. But that doesn't
make them much more comfortable, especially given their jitters about corporate profits and the strength of
the economy.
"A lot of people are nervous," says David Briggs, head of stock trading at Pittsburgh mutual-fund group
Federated Investors. "I had it a little, too," he admits, pointing out that he recently wrote a market commentary
acknowledging his "Octoberphobia."
A look at market history, it should be noted, indicates that October has seen more stock gains than declines.
It is in September that stocks have been most likely to decline; they fell this September. Lately, in fact,
October has been a month for big rallies, as investors become (rightly or wrongly) hopeful about stock
performance in the coming year. Since 1998, stocks have shown gains in every October -- sometimes big
ones.
Many investors, in fact, dismiss October worries as goofy. They point out that there are a lot of differences
between 1987, the year of the most-recent crash, and now -- most prominently interest rates. In 1987, the
yield on the 30-year Treasury bond was rising fast and hit 10% right before the crash, contributing to the
pressure on stocks. Now, it is hovering around 5%, and the 10-year note, which is now the benchmark, is
yielding just over 4%.
But that hasn't stopped some people from passing around stock charts showing surprising parallels between
the patterns for the current year and for 1987. One of the charts bears the artistic name "Three Peaks and the
Domed House," because the pattern sort of looks like that.
Market-research group Ned Davis Research in Venice, Fla., even decided to publish an analysis of the
"Three Peaks and the Domed House" chart. Its conclusion: The pattern (showing three jumps in the shape of
inverted V's, a more gradual topping out and then, in 1987, a sharp drop) clearly can be seen both this year
and in 1987. Of course, this year's chart doesn't include the drop, and the two years are sharply different in
other ways.
Mr. Briggs says he doesn't expect a crash, and he has advised money managers at Federated not to sell their
stock positions. But he has suggested they consider rotating some money into their less-jumpy stocks for a
while, just in case of volatility -- especially given that quarterly earnings reports are on the horizon.
"My guess is that the market probably is going to be choppy into the early part of November," Mr. Briggs
says.
Still, there is no denying that, when crashes have come, it has tended to be in October. Of the 20 biggest
one-day percentage drops in the Dow industrials, eight have come in October. These include the two-day
crash of 1929; the 1987 crash; an 8% plunge one week after the 1987 crash; the "echo" or "minicrash" of
October 1989; and a 7% plunge in October 1997. Of the 10 biggest drops, half were in October, including the
two major crashes.
Is that just a coincidence? Perhaps. But there may be reasons for October to be dangerous. In October,
people are settling in after summer vacations. Governments are focusing on policy changes. Both

--- Page 207 ---
Page 207 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
governments and companies are looking ahead to the future, sometimes trying to accomplish things before
year's end. If they err, the errors sometimes occur in the fall.
This year, as was the case in 1987, the U.S. has become embroiled in a shoving match with foreign countries
over currency issues. In 1987, the dollar was near a seven-year low against the mark. Now, the dollar is near
a three-year low against the yen.
"I don't see a replay of '87," says Henry Herrmann, chief investment officer at mutual-fund group Waddell
&Reed in Overland Park, Kan. "But if I could be really wrong, it would be because of the currency market. It is
in nobody's best interest for the dollar to have a big decline."
But he, Mr. Briggs and others point out that, in many ways, the current situation is different.
Now, as in 1987, stocks are sitting on big gains. But in 1987, the Dow industrials had more than tripled from
their bottom in 1982. This time, they are up 31% from their low, which occurred just one year ago.
Then, investors were worried about inflation and an overheating economy, and both gold prices and
interest rates were rising. Now, gold is up, but interest rates remain low and inflation fears are almost
nonexistent.
Then, computer-driven selling contributed to the losses. Today, there still is plenty of computer-driven trading,
though traders insist it is more diverse and less prone to provoking a crash.
Another possible parallel is something analysts call investor sentiment. In 1987, investors had become
overoptimistic after five years of stock gains. Among investment-newsletter writers who expressed a bullish or
bearish view, about 75% were bullish by August 1987, according to a survey by a publication called Investors
Intelligence in New Rochelle, N.Y. (Bullishness actually declined after that.) Today, the survey shows
bullishness back to the 1987 level: 74%.
"There is a lot of risk out there," says Michael Burke, editor of Investors Intelligence. "Stock valuations are still
very high, and everybody is taking an optimistic spin on things." Heavy optimism can be bad for stocks,
because it means people have moved a large percentage of available money into stocks and that they are
susceptible to disappointment, which would cause them to sell.
But investment advisers say that many ordinary investors still have vivid memories of the bear market and
aren't as upbeat as the newsletter writers seem to be. Options selling also indicates a high level of
nervousness. On that score as well, the feeling is that the investment climate still isn't as extreme as it was in
1987.
"You don't have as much of a manic situation as in '87," says Tim Hayes, global stock strategist at Ned Davis
Research. He sees closer parallels between the mood in 1987 and that during the stock bubble of the late
1990s, he says. "Are we headed for a crash? I just don't see the evidence of that," Mr. Hayes says.
After rising almost 200 points Wednesday, the first trading day in October, the Dow industrials finished the
week up 259.23 points, or 2.8%, at 9572.31 -- including a gain of 84.51 points, or 0.9%, on Friday. Counting
just the three days of October, the Dow has risen 297.25 points.
Even some analysts who expect a stock pullback say they doubt we will see a crash.
"October is a month where we have had big breaks in the past. Anybody who has been around knows that,"
says Phil Roth, chief technical market analyst at New York brokerage firm Miller Tabak. "If you get the stock
market and the bond market and the dollar down, that can develop into a panicky situation, but that isn't
happening."
He adds, "The pattern since June looks like a top to me -- not a disastrous top, but a top. I think there is more
weakness ahead, and then a buying point."
Mark Twain, who often complained of his losses in the stock market (he was a much better writer than
investor), put it this way in a famous quotation: "October. This is one of the peculiarly dangerous months to
speculate in stocks in. The others are July, January, September, April, November, May, March, June,
December, August, and February."
