Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
426 words
6 October 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Today's Market Forecast]
Gold, Bullied
The gold trade looked on Friday as if it was getting old.
Gold futures had their worst drop in six years, falling to about $370, just after hitting a seven-year high two
weeks ago. It's almost getting painful to watch investors try to make up their minds about the near future.
Deflation is coming! Buy bonds. No, inflation is coming! Load up on gold. The economy is roaring back! Buy
the Nasdaq. But there's a debt bubble. Sell the dollar! In the movie version (working title: The Three Faces of
the U.S. Economy), the role of financial markets will be played by Joanne Woodward.
Gold had been rising in large part thanks to the group of pessimists who cast a cold eye on the dollar,
assuming that foreign investors will eventually lose faith in the currency. To these grim folks, the dollar is still
markedly overvalued. The U.S. government is running huge, farther-than-the-eye-can-see budget deficits.
Meanwhile, Alan Greenspan is determined to keep money cheap until the economy recovers strongly, raising
the specter of inflation. The view that Mr. Greenspan's chairmanship will wind up a miserable failure that
corrodes the value of the dollar is extreme, of course. Not only that, but it won't be determined to be right or
wrong for years and will probably wind up being heatedly debated and essentially unknowable.
The case for gold, along with the underlying argument that the supply-demand fundamentals are favorable
and gold producers are acting rationally, is that we are in the midst of a broad handoff: Financial assets were
the asset of choice for the past two decades, but physical assets will be the safer investments in the years to
come, given the deflationary pressures on the economy and the inflationary central-bank polices. These
folks argue that the gold move Friday was a trading blip.
Equity investors are by their nature optimists and risk lovers. But those who like a hedge can play this
scenario by buying what gold favorers see as the best-managed gold producer, Newmont Mining. After
Friday's 5% drop to $37.88, the stock isn't screamingly cheap. But there's a good shot that gold continues to
fall in the next couple of weeks and Newmont could slip into the low 30s. At that point the stock will be
attractive.

Blue Chips Fall, Ending Month With a Decline --- Disappointing Economic Data Send Industrials Down 105.18 As Treasury Yield Falls Below 4%
Tuesday's Markets
Blue Chips Fall, Ending Month With a Decline --- Disappointing Economic Data Send Industrials Down
105.18 As Treasury Yield Falls Below 4%
By E.S. Browning
998 words
1 October 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ANOTHER DOSE of disappointing economic data sent the Dow Jones Industrial Average down more than
100 points, leaving it with a decline for September and ending a streak of six monthly gains.
The yield of the 10-year Treasury note finished below 4% for the first time since July, as investors reacted to
the economic news -- soft numbers for consumer confidence and for manufacturing activity in the Chicago
area -- by shifting money toward the relative safety of bonds.The yield falls when bond prices rise, and the fall
in the benchmark yield is likely to help push down some market interest rates such as mortgage rates.
The Dow industrials and other major indexes squeaked out gains for the third quarter. But a drumbeat of
disappointing economic news lately has provoked a series of sharp stock pullbacks that have left many
traders and investors nervous.
Until last week, the Dow industrials hadn't endured a 100-point decline since early August. But yesterday's
drop of 105.18 points, or 1.12%, to 9275.06 marked the third 100-point decline in seven trading sessions. The
latest decline left the Dow industrials at their lowest level since Aug. 13.
"We have seen a very limp finish" to the quarter over the past few days, said John O'Donoghue, senior stock
trader at New York brokerage firm Credit Suisse First Boston. Unless there is a sign of improvement in the
economic data due today and later this week, notably on manufacturing activity and employment, "I think
things could be a little sloppy here," he said.
The Nasdaq Composite Index declined 2.06%, or 37.62 points, to 1786.94, a retreat of more than 6% since
its recent high on Sept. 18. The Nasdaq index, whose many volatile technology stocks have led both the
earlier gains and the recent declines, still is up 34% for the year so far and 10% for the quarter. But it has
fallen 1.3% in September, breaking a streak of seven consecutive monthly gains.
The Dow finished with a gain of 3.2% for the quarter and 11% for the year so far, but the index fell 1.5% in
September.
The broad S&P 500 index of large stocks fell 1.05%, or 10.61 points, to 995.97. That was down 1.2% for
September, but up 2.2% for the quarter and up 13% for the year so far.
The Russell 2000 small-stock index fell 1.02%, or 5.03 points, to 487.68. It is down 2% for September, and up
8.8% for the quarter and 27% on the year.
In late New York trading, the euro was at $1.1665, its highest level against the dollar since June 19, although
the yen fell against the dollar. Oil prices rose to the highest level in three weeks and the price of gold also
rose, finishing at $385.40 a troy ounce, just below its seven-year high.
In the stock market, it was a volatile day that left some traders exhausted. Stocks began with a plunge, in the
wake of the economic news and of the previous evening's announcement that Sun Microsystems expected a
weak quarter. All of that stoked fears that the economy and corporate earnings may not be recovering as fast
as hoped. That would be bad news for stocks, whose earlier gains were based on hopes for a recovery.
But then at midmorning on the East Coast, stocks reversed ground and rose. Traders said money managers
were buying stocks to avoid showing too much cash when they closed their quarterly books, a common
phenomenon. Some traders began to bet that stocks might actually finish up. But then, at about 2 p.m. EDT,
the buying ran its course and stocks sagged.

--- Page 211 ---
Page 211 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Earlier this year, "I had been thinking that the industrials could make their way to 10000. Now I've kind of
given up on that a little bit and I am thinking that the downside risk could be 8800 or so," said Henry
Herrmann, chief investment officer at mutual-fund group Waddell &Reed in Overland Park, Kan.
Even if quarterly earnings news is positive in the next few weeks, he said, signs are that employment won't
pick up until next year, which could put a damper on stock gains. In addition, worries about foreigners
withdrawing money from dollar-denominated securities are worsening as the dollar falls, he said.
"I am hoping that by the end of the year we will get back to the highs we established in September, but I'm
not looking for more than that," he said. "Earnings will be good, but people will doubt their sustainability"
because of weak jobs data.
One hopeful sign, said Mr. O'Donoghue of CSFB, is that Treasury yields have fallen below 4%. That means
that investors in Treasury bonds are sitting on a good profit now, and "if you get any kind of strength at all on
the economic numbers, there is a high potential that you could see some money come out of bonds and back
into stocks," he said.
Outside the U.S., stocks fell. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks, fell 0.64%, or 0.81
point, to 125.07.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks fell. On the Big Board, where 1.51 billion shares traded, 1,609 stocks rose and 1,642 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 1 4/32, or $11.25 per $1,000 invested. The yield fell to
3.934%. The 30-year bond was up 1 28/32 to yield 4.875%.
The dollar was mixed. It traded at 111.47 yen, up from 110.78; the euro rose against the dollar to $1.1665
from $1.1597.

The U.N.: Searching for Relevance --- Mission: Improbable: U.N. Peacekeeping Is a Troubled Art, Congo Mess Shows --- Rich Nations No Longer...
The U.N.: Searching for Relevance --- Mission: Improbable: U.N. Peacekeeping Is a Troubled Art,
Congo Mess Shows --- Rich Nations No Longer Send Troops, and Sometimes There's No Peace to
Keep --- Uruguayans in Devil's Caldron
By Robert Block and Alix M. Freedman
4,197 words
1 October 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Third in a Series]
BUNIA, Democratic Republic of Congo -- Earlier this year, while the world was transfixed by the war in Iraq,
the Congolese district of Ituri was on the verge of genocide and the United Nations was in trouble. For
months, the U.N. had searched for a military force capable of stopping another African holocaust. No
significant power accepted the challenge.
With nowhere else to turn, U.N. peacekeeping chiefs dispatched to the war zone 840 Uruguayan soldiers. It
was a moment of excitement for the men, who came from a country that hadn't fought a war in over a century,
says one of their officers, Lt. Colonel Waldemar Fontes.
Their mood soon changed as they dropped unsuspectingly into a place they would later term the devil's
caldron. Equipped only with rifles and a few armored personnel carriers, the Uruguayans were appalled to
find tribal militias rampaging through the dusty streets with machetes and machine guns. Child soldiers
chopped Hema residents to pieces and took body parts as souvenirs. Hundreds of cadavers littered the city.
Short of bullets and outnumbered, the Uruguayans could do little but defend their post and give shelter to
panicked residents who ran to them for help. "The situation was out of control," Col. Fontes says. "Two or
three soldiers had nervous breakdowns, and one officer went into a diabetic coma because of the stress."
Inside the U.N., the Uruguayan colonel says, "People thought the presence of U.N. troops would fix
everything as if it were some kind of magic solution to the problems here."
As the U.N. struggles for relevance in the wake of the Iraqi war, nowhere are its dilemmas and limitations
more stark than in its peacekeeping campaigns in forlorn lands. The troubled effort in Bunia shows how the
U.N.'s best-known function has languished, as world powers send money, not men, leaving the work to
soldiers from the developing world. In effect, the rich countries subcontract the actual soldiering to poor
nations.
Ralph Bunche, a Detroit native who served as the U.N.'s mediator in Palestine, invented the peacekeeping
concept 54 years ago to try to end hostilities between Arabs and Israelis. His first mission in 1948 set the
pattern: Unarmed observers or lightly armed peacekeepers deployed in buffer zones between warring armies,
keeping them at bay and buying time for diplomatic efforts.
But peacekeeping has changed dramatically since those early hopeful days. Instead of monitoring clearcut
cease-fire lines, peacekeepers increasingly are dispatched to disintegrating states where warlords have scant
respect for truces.
For many, the U.N.'s "blue helmets" now call to mind hapless, poorly armed soldiers from small countries,
who watch ineffectually as war crimes unfold around them. Among disasters that fostered this image, a
peacekeeping force of 270 was powerless to protect 800,000 people from genocide in Rwanda in 1994.
Later, peacekeepers stood by as Bosnian Serbs overran the U.N.-designated "safe area" of Srebrenica,
executing at least 7,000 Bosnian Muslim men and boys. Earlier, the U.S. blamed U.N. incompetence for the
deaths in Mogadishu, Somalia, of 18 Rangers, who were serving alongside the U.N.'s forces.
Dismayed by such events and fed up with the U.N. peacekeeping bureaucracy, nations with the
most-capable armies -- the U.S., Britain and France -- decided taking part in U.N. peace missions was a
losing proposition. Since 1995, no major power has put any of its combat troops under the U.N. flag. Instead,
countries with bloated armies such as Pakistan, Bangladesh and Nigeria lead the U.N. peacekeeper ranks,
lured by cash, on-the-job training and international prestige.

--- Page 213 ---
Page 213 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Western countries have essentially created a form of apartheid in peacekeeping," says David Malone,
president of the International Peace Academy, a think tank in New York. "The toll is much-less-effective
peacekeeping."
To be sure, U.N. troops have helped restore order in Cambodia, Namibia and Mozambique. Recent U.N.
peacekeeping missions in Sierra Leone and East Timor have also been successes. But in other cases,
unrealistic mandates from the Security Council and the U.N.'s slow-moving and politicized bureaucracy have
contributed to mission failure. This is the story of how the U.N. put together a peacekeeping force in Congo's
bloody Ituri region under terrible circumstances, why the mission was overwhelmed and how the U.N. has
attempted to salvage it in the aftermath.
The mission began in 1999 in this vast country with a troubled history. Steeped in mystery -- it was the setting
for Conrad's "Heart of Darkness" -- Congo was a Belgian colony before gaining independence in 1960. Under
dictator Mobutu Sese Seko, who renamed it Zaire, it was bled by misrule. Its colonial splendor crumbled.
Cities became jungle islands unconnected by roads, phone systems or electrical grids. What it always had
were gold, diamonds and cobalt, riches that proved a lure to neighboring countries and their armies.
In 1997, militias backed by Rwanda and Uganda ousted Mr. Mobutu and installed Laurent Kabila, who named
the country Democratic Republic of Congo. A year later, unhappy with him, they returned and tried to oust
him, too. Rushing to his side were Angola, Namibia and Zimbabwe. All five outside powers operated in large
part by supporting local armed groups, in what became a vast proxy war. Other African leaders, fearing the
vicious fighting could plunge the whole continent into conflict, cobbled together a peace plan and beseeched
the U.N. for help.
A peacekeeping operation in the lawless land was the last thing the organization wanted. The U.N. had just
overhauled its peacekeeping program, focusing on two principles: That the U.N. shouldn't send a force
unequal to the task, and that it should never send peacekeepers where there was no peace to keep. "If we
had gone strictly by the book, we probably never would have gotten involved" in Congo, says a senior
peacekeeping official. "But the U.N. said we can't turn our back on Africa."
Big-power support for a Congo mission was highly equivocal. The U.S. didn't want a robust mission, says
America's then-ambassador to the U.N., Richard Holbrooke. He says he personally thought the mission ought
to have a so-called Chapter 7 mandate that would permit the peacekeeping troops to use force if necessary.
But Clinton administration defense officials didn't believe the U.N. could make such a venture a success, he
says, and feared that American forces might be called on to pick up the pieces.
In the end, the U.N. designed the mission under gentler "Chapter 6" rules: Unarmed observers could monitor
the disengagement of foreign forces, while U.N. guards protected the U.N.'s assets and the observers. Under
the plan, more troops would arrive as peace took hold, to disarm militias.
But the Security Council took the unusual step of adding another duty. It said the peacekeepers should
protect civilians in their immediate vicinity who faced "imminent threat of physical violence." The author of this
clause was Robert Fowler, then Canada's U.N. ambassador.
From the start, U.N. peacekeeping officials blamed him for crafting an ill-defined and unrealistic mandate that
would scare off nations that were already skittish about contributing troops to Congo. But they had no choice
but to bow to the Security Council's wishes. Mr. Fowler says, "I was trying to avoid the front-page picture of
U.N. soldiers lounging on the wall while people got murdered in front of them."
Uruguay, Morocco, Tunisia and a handful of other nations offered a few hundred troops each. Their
equipment and training didn't rival that of the high-tech armies of the West. "It was really hard to get good
people to go to Congo," says Margaret Carey, principal director of the U.N.'s Africa peacekeeping division.
The Congo fighting did not end. Estimates are that it has cost the lives of at least three million Congolese
over five years, mostly from disease and starvation. One of the bloodiest regions was Ituri in the far northeast,
adjoining Uganda and Sudan. In Ituri, an estimated 50,000 have died, mostly in vicious massacres rather
than disease or famine. But Ituri's savagery was tribal -- Hema and Lendu tribes fighting over resources --
and had little to do with the foreign intervention at the core of the other fighting. The U.N. didn't want to get
involved in Ituri's ethnic strife.
By March 2003, Ituri could no longer be ignored. A broader Congo peace plan required the pullout of all
foreign troops, and that included Ugandan troops in Ituri. As the Ugandans played one tribe off against the
other, they managed to keep some limits on the ethnic fighting. If nothing replaced them, the region could
explode.
No nation wanted to send any new troops for an Ituri peacekeeping force. Even if they had, it would have
taken them at least three months to arrive, because of the intricate steps involved in the U.N.'s troop
recruitment. So the U.N. turned to the 840-man Uruguayan force, which was guarding U.N. buildings in the

--- Page 214 ---
Page 214 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
sleepy center of Congo. Accustomed to the slow life of guard duty, they weren't psychologically prepared for
duty in the violent region, says Col. Fontes, the Uruguayan officer. Worse, says the colonel, a veteran of U.N.
missions in Rwanda and Western Sahara, the U.N.'s own peacekeeping officials bungled the planning and
misread the situation on the ground.
On April 23, the U.N. flew the first 101 Uruguayans into the mud and dust of the Ituri city of Bunia. It was just
one day before Uganda's 8,000 troops in Ituri, including 3,000 in Bunia, had to start their withdrawal.
As the Uruguayans stepped off a white cargo plane onto the tarmac of Bunia's decrepit airport, an anxious
Ugandan commander, Brigadier Kale Kayihura, asked why there were so few of them. Col. Fontes said it
would take at least a month for all 840 Uruguayans and their 32 armored personnel carriers to arrive. Col.
Fontes says the Ugandan general warned that the newcomers were stepping into a maelstrom.
(MORE)
"He explained that if the U.N. didn't have enough troops on the ground to replace his forces, there was going
to be a power vacuum and war," recalls Col. Fontes, who is still stationed in Bunia. Col. Fontes says no one
had briefed him about how bad the situation in Ituri was.
No sooner had the Uruguayans begun arriving, and the Ugandans leaving, than tribal fighters mobilized for
more battle. Horribly wounded people began arriving at the U.N. troops' base with stories of massacres
elsewhere in Ituri.
Uganda and U.N. officials in Congo tried to work out a slower Ugandan pullout, to give the U.N. peace force
more time to deploy. But Rwanda was threatening to come back into Congo if Uganda delayed its withdrawal.
The Security Council, fearing that the wider Congolese peace plan was on the verge of unraveling, refused to
give the Ugandans any more time. Angered, the Ugandans then picked up the pace of their pullout. And the
situation swiftly worsened.
On April 29, Lendu militias in Bunia began kidnapping and killing local Hemas. Jerome Kabagambe, a
19-year-old student, was abducted from his home near the central market. He escaped and ran toward the
U.N.'s base through the rutted dirt strip that forms Bunia's main boulevard. The Uruguayans took him into the
base, a newly whitewashed mansion, and had a standoff with armed Lendus who had been pursuing him. "I
am alive today only because of" the peacekeepers, the youth says. The Lendus left, vowing revenge, and
four days later attacked the home of the U.N.'s regional director.
The last Ugandan soldier left Bunia just a week later, on May 6. That morning, military observers advised
U.N. officials in New York and the Congo capital of Kinshasa that all was calm. But within minutes of the
cable, Bunia exploded in an orgy of killing. Though most of the 230,000 residents fled, thousands of others
clamored for protection inside the Uruguayans' base. "They were like bugs on a wall," says Michel Kassa, a
U.N. humanitarian official. The barbed-wire-enclosed mansion became a giant refugee camp, as shells of the
warring militias flew overhead.
The U.N. peacekeeping force had been bolstered by 600 Congolese police sent from the Kinshasa. Worried
about these lightly armed allies, Col. Fontes now sent an armored car with a machine gun to reinforce them
north of the city. On the way, his men found terrified 200 policemen, some in their underwear, running toward
the mansion. "They were supposed to be fighting with us, but they gave their arms to the militias and became
refugees," Col. Fontes says. "They were hiding underneath our vehicles at a time when we didn't have a way
to adequately protect our own comrades."
Holed up inside the U.N. mansion, Alpha Sow, the political head of the U.N.'s Bunia operation, frantically
cabled New York: "Please send capable troops." It had none to send. In any case, the Bunia airport couldn't
accept more planes than the ones bringing Uruguayans. After each landing, the crumbling runway had to be
patched.
The Red Cross says 400 to 500 Congolese were slain in Bunia between May 3 and May 12, after which
fighters from the Hema tribe drove out the Lendu militias. Thousands more were believed killed elsewhere in
Ituri as the tribes fought.
Why did the U.N. send a peacekeeping force, to say nothing of a small, ill-equipped one, into a region rent by
tribal strife? Officials point to an embryonic peace effort, in which representatives of the armed groups met at
something called the Ituri Peace Commission. By citing this commission, U.N. officials in Congo were able to
convince the Security Council that there were sufficient prospects for peace to send peacekeepers.
In fact, it was a wager -- that this very tentative peace initiative would hold long enough for the Uruguayans to
ensconce themselves. "We decided to go ahead knowing it was very fragile but hoping it would turn around,"
says Jean-Marie Guehenno, the U.N.'s peacekeeping chief. "It was a kind of gamble."

--- Page 215 ---
Page 215 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
He adds, however, that "Peacekeeping is a gray area where you are never sure you are getting it right. If I
hadn't pushed the Security Council, we wouldn't have saved many, many lives."
Mr. Sow, the political head of the U.N. post in Bunia, says the Security Council should have allowed a delay
in the Ugandan withdrawal until the U.N. had built up a stronger peacekeeping force in Ituri. "We were caught
between two fires," he says. "We knew the withdrawal would cause some problems because [the U.N. force]
wasn't as ready, but for political reasons" the pullout of the Ugandans had to go forward. The result was that
the U.N. ended up launching a peacekeeping effort with "inadequate troops, inadequate mandate."
Uruguay's Col. Fontes says he believes the Security Council was honestly trying to avoid problems in the Ituri
region, "but it was a bad political decision because there was no will of the warring parties to solve this
peacefully."
As fighting worsened in early May after the Ugandan pullout, the U.N. peacekeepers in Bunia considered
falling back to the airport five miles away. But "we did not want a Srebrenica here," says the U.N.'s Mr. Kassa,
alluding to the risk of still-greater killing if they left. Staying within the city also protected the thousands who'd
taken refuge on U.N. grounds. "Many of these might not be alive today if it were not for this thin blanket that
the U.N. provided," Mr. Kassa says.
Col. Fontes says his troops did everything possible with their meager numbers and resources. "Our
consciences are completely clear. A lot of people died, people whose names we do not know, but we also
saved a lot of people," he says.
It doesn't look so straightforward to some Congolese. David Alicama, who works for a humanitarian agency,
says that on May 8, he and a group of 300 civilians sought protection from a small patrol of Uruguayans. "We
ran to where MONUC was," he says, using the acronym for the Operation Mission of the United Nations in
the Congo. "But they ran away with their tents to the airport."
A Belgian priest who has long lived in Bunia, the Rev. Jan Mol, says that on May 10, Lendu militiamen
attacked the Nyakasanza church. Phoned by a priest inside the church, Father Mol rushed to U.N.
headquarters but says he was kept waiting for four hours. By the time he persuaded the Uruguayan
peacekeeping troops to take action, two priests and 18 others who'd sought refuge in the church had been
murdered.
En route to the church, he adds, the U.N. forces did nothing to stop looting, rapes and killing taking place in
clear sight. "They told people openly that we aren't here for the protection of people," the priest says. "Their
only mandate was to protect themselves."
Col. Fontes confirms the church slaughter but says it happened on the worst day of fighting, when terrible
events were taking place all over the city. He says he knows nothing of the case Mr. Alicama describes. Mr.
Sow, political head of the U.N. post in Bunia, says the peace force had to maintain its position at the mansion,
where most refugees were. "You know 10 or 20 people are being killed on the other side of the city. But if you
send armored personnel carriers there, the [warring Ituri groups] could come here and kill thousands," Mr.
Sow says.
In some cases, the U.N. mission couldn't even protect its own. A Bangladeshi U.N. officer, Maj. Mohammed
Hamid, says two unarmed U.N. observers in the town of Mongbwalu began calling him, begging to be
evacuated. They called for days, ever more frantic, and he passed the messages on to U.N. officers in
Kinshasa, he says. After six days, a U.N. helicopter arrived at the town's airport to evacuate the two but
couldn't find them.
Five days later, the U.N. sent a recovery team, who were guided by locals to a shallow grave that held the
bodies of the observers, a Jordanian and a Malawian. An autopsy indicated they had been tortured and had
had organs removed. Mwisa Kambale, 19, says he saw Lendu militiamen ambush the two observers as they
headed to the airport to meet the evacuation helicopter. He says he watched as they were taken back to their
guesthouse, ordered to raise their hands and shot. A U.N. panel is investigating the deaths, including the
possibility of cannibalism, and looking into why the U.N. took so long to respond to its own observers' pleas.
The situation in Ituri grew so bad that major powers could no longer ignore it. As the Security Council took it
up, Secretary General Kofi Annan and Jean-Marie Guehenno, the U.N.'s peacekeeping chief, pleaded with
French officials for a rapid-deployment force. France was willing to send a battalion of marines, but not to put
them under U.N. command. It agreed they could stay just long enough for the U.N. to build up a bigger
peacekeeping force, this time under a tougher, "shoot at will" mandate.
France was uncomfortable going alone because some Africans harbor suspicions about its intentions, arising
from French support for a genocidal Rwandan regime in 1994. So the European Union flew its flag over the
operation, which came to include British, Belgian, Swedish and even South African contributions as well.

--- Page 216 ---
Page 216 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Arriving in Bunia in early June, the EU troops outlawed the carrying of guns and showed that they meant it by
shooting two militiamen who flouted the rule.
This and other recent interventions are seen by the U.S. as useful precedents. In another instance, British
troops marching under their own flag came to the rescue of a U.N. peacekeeping mission in Sierra Leone.
And Australian troops calmed things down in East Timor, handing matters there over to U.N. peacekeepers.
The White House sees this notion of peacekeeping efforts outside the auspices of the U.N. -- but with its
blessing -- as the right approach for Iraq. "We need to be flexible to have different models for different kinds
of problems," says Kim Holmes, Assistant Secretary of State for International Organization Affairs. "What we
are asking for Iraq falls under this new tradition or model. It's certainly not blue helmets under a U.N.
command."
(MORE)
By the time EU soldiers left Bunia in early September, France and the EU were boasting they had turned the
tide and restored calm. Some of Bunia's looted shops have reopened. Still, as recently as Sept. 4, a
suspected militia member was stoned to death in daylight just a few hundred yards from U.N. headquarters.
And after dark, the city's unlighted streets still jump to the sound of sporadic gunfire.
The U.N. was supposed to be building a force of almost 4,000 while the EU troops were in Bunia. So far,
Bangladesh has sent 1,200 and Pakistan 1,000. They join the 840 Uruguayans, who are still here. A
promised Nepalese contingent is expected in November, two months behind schedule.
Their numbers bolstered, the U.N. peacekeepers in Bunia have become much more aggressive. They're
scouring neighborhoods for hidden arms and patrolling the lush nearby hills with two helicopter gunships sent
by India. This posture has led to a test of the new U.N. force's mettle: Hema militias, angry at the arrest of
some of their leaders after the U.N. found an arms dump, attacked the U.N. mansion with machine guns and
rocket-propelled grenades. The fighting subsided after a U.N. helicopter blasted a truckload of Hema
gunmen.
Difficulties remain. Most peacekeeping troops don't speak French, crippling their ability to gather intelligence.
Partly to fix this, the U.N. tried to get Sweden to leave behind a special-forces unit that was part of the EU
force. The Swedes declined. Moreover, some 17,000 displaced Congolese are still huddled in camps and
expecting the U.N. peacekeeping forces to restore them to their homes in the interior of the Ituri district.
Yet the peacekeepers have yet to move into the interior of Ituri, where atrocities have continued unabated.
One Saturday in August, while Uruguayan troops organized races and soccer games for soldiers and U.N.
civilians in Bunia, the village of Bule 50 miles north was under siege. Lendu militias with machine guns and
machetes embarked on a 10-hour orgy of violence, murdering and mutilating dozens of women and children.
Afterward, as his family sifted through the ashes of their home, a 43-year-old teacher named Jean Baki
asked, "Why is the U.N. silent while we are here dying every day?"
A group of Bangladeshi troops arrived in the village a month later, assuring the remaining residents, and
refugees from other torched villages, that help was on the way. Then it started to rain, and the peacekeepers,
fearing their helicopter might malfunction, hastily left. They haven't been back.
"This is an unfinished business," says the U.N.'s Mr. Guehenno. "We still have a long way to go."

Anchor Exchange Rates To Something Real
Letters to the Editor
Anchor Exchange Rates To Something Real
284 words
29 September 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A19
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Your Sept. 23 editorial "Snow's Currency Job" was on the mark. With the dollar weakening back to 1996
levels against gold, further slippage in the dollar's value will only serve to increase inflation risk. Inflation
risk damages growth prospects through higher interest rates and automatic increases in the effective tax
rate on capital.
This is why free and open global trade must be prefaced by a truly pro-growth monetary system in which
major exchange rates are tethered together and anchored to a pilot currency fixed to something real. If
money holds its value over time, capital flows to its most efficient after-tax use, maximizing the standard of
living. In the absence of a truly pro-growth monetary system, central bank policy errors generate currency
risk -- inflation and deflation -- which disrupt global capital markets and generate pressure to lift trade
barriers.
Mike Darda
Chief Economist
Polyconomics Inc.
Parsippany, N.J.
---
"Snow's Currency Job," while otherwise excellent, implies that the only way governments may intervene in
currency markets is by tweaking the money supply, a deplorable practice.
However, massive currency reserves held by foreign governments are another potent and frequent
mechanism and do not involve warming up the printing press. It is this practice the Bush administration is
rightfully attacking.
Clearly, the practice should not be abandoned overnight; this would wreak economic chaos on many people.
It should be a medium-term process. Domestic pressure from manufacturing notwithstanding, it is still good
policy for the president to promote nonintervention in currency markets in favor of allowing economic
fundamentals to influence the relative prices of currencies to each other.
Matt Williams
Cincinnati

The Outlook: Latin America Gets Unexpected Boost From China's Success
The Outlook:
The Economy
The Outlook: Latin America Gets Unexpected Boost From China's Success
By Marc Lifsher
1,029 words
29 September 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
CARACAS, Venezuela -- Ever since China emerged as the world's manufacturing powerhouse, economists
and policymakers in Latin America have worried that China's success would come at their expense as
investment and jobs flocked to places such as Guangdong province and away from cities such as Lima, Peru.
And while there is little doubt that China has become the favorite destination for manufacturers looking for
cheap labor, Latin America hasn't been shut out completely. In an unexpected twist, it appears China and
other Asian countries are turning to Latin America as a source for commodities needed to feed Asia's
appetites and fuel their manufacturing machines. China, in particular, has stepped in as a major buyer at a
time when demand from the U.S. and Europe has been weak. That, in turn, has triggered a rally in commodity
prices, reversing two years of stagnation.
Copper, a bellwether metal, is leading the charge with a 15% price jump from last year. Gold production is
soaring as prices flirt with the level of $400 an ounce. Even sales of goods such as soybeans from Brazil and
beef from Argentina are heading higher.
Economists, meanwhile, are increasing their economic growth estimates for the region. Foreign reserves are
swelling or at least stabilizing, currencies are strengthening and inflation appears to be under control in most
nations. The only potentially bleak spot on the commodity horizon may be oil, where prices have been falling
since midsummer.
But the general improvement in other commodity prices and production should contribute to an "export led"
recovery for Latin America over the next few years, predicts World Bank economist Guillermo Perry, in a
report released this month. "The region's contraction is over," he wrote.
Chile is a prime example of Latin American exports to China. Over the past few years, China has become
Chile's third-largest trading partner, behind the U.S. and Japan. According to a report from UBS, China
accounted for 7% of all Chilean exports in 2002, compared with 2.3% in 1999. Chilean exports to China are
estimated to be around 10% of total exports for 2003, UBS analyst Ben Laidler said. Chilean exports to China
jumped by 53% during the first quarter of 2003, compared with the first quarter of 2002, and the trend
appears to be continuing for the rest of the year, the UBS report said.
The growth estimates for all of Latin America are reflecting export-driven economic gains. The World Bank is
forecasting that growth in regional gross domestic product -- the total value of the region's output -- will
increase to 3.7% in 2004 from 1.8% in 2003. (GDP growth during 2002 shrank by 0.8%.) Export volume is
expected to grow 11.2% in 2004 and 9.2% in 2003, the World Bank says.
Copper, which is mined primarily in Chile and Peru, is trading around 80 cents a pound, up from about 70
cents a year ago. Peruvian copper production rose 7% in July, compared with July 2002. The increase was
led by a 21% output jump by Southern Peru Copper Corp., a unit of Grupo Mexico SA, the world's No. 3
copper producer. Both Southern Peru and Australia's BHP Billiton Ltd. expanded operations or restarted
facilities mothballed because of previously low world prices.
In Chile, year-to-year copper production climbed 22% in July, due mainly to BHP Billiton's expansion of its
Escondida mine, says Juan Villarzu, chief executive officer of state-owned Corp. del Cobre de Chile, the
world's largest copper mining and refining company.
Andean gold miners also are raising production. Year-to-year gold output was up 28% in July in Peru, the
Latin American leader and the world's seventh-largest producer. The continent's biggest gold mine,
Yanacocha, owned by Denver-based Newmont Mining Corp. and Cia. Minas de Buenaventura SAA of Peru,
reported a year-to-year rise of 82% in exports for July. Yanacocha has been a winner for Newmont and

--- Page 220 ---
Page 220 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Buenaventura because it enjoys low operating costs at a time when gold is trading above $380 an ounce, up
about 20% from last year.
The rise in international gold prospects also is leading to increased development in Venezuela. Crystallex
International Corp., a based in Vancouver, British Columbia, recently concluded a successful feasibility study
for exploiting an estimated 10.2 million ounces of proven or probable reserves in Bolivar State. Hecla Mining
Co. of Coeur D'Alene, Idaho, says it expects to produce 100,000 ounces a year from four Venezuelan mines
that will be reactivated late this year or early 2004.
But minerals aren't the only profitable exports buttressing Latin America's bottom line. Argentina expects to
increase beef exports this year by 16% in volume and 7.1% in price over 2002. In Brazil, the soybean and
grain industries are even more flush. Brazil's National Agriculture Federation estimates revenue from exports
of soy products should rise by 25% this year to $8 billion. The jump would catapult Brazil past the U.S. as the
world's No. 1 soy exporter. Brazil's trade balance benefits from low production costs combined with increases
in prices and quantity. Brazil's trade surplus with the rest of the world reached a surprising $20 billion for the
first eight months of 2003, $2 billion greater than China's for the same period.
Improved exports don't address other major concerns in Latin America, expressed recently at the World
Trade Organization talks in Cancun, Mexico. And low-cost Chinese manufactured goods are creating
unemployment in countries such as Mexico that opened many manufacturing plants in the 1990s.
"When you have a country like China buying a lot of copper and mineral products, it's because China is
producing a lot of manufactured goods," say Luis Oganes, an analyst for J.P. Morgan. "Latin America is
finding it's still having a hard time competing with China" in value-added products.
But for now, he says, "if you're a commodities exporter, it's good."

Exchange Rates: a Primer
Thinking Things Over
Exchange Rates: a Primer
By Robert L. Bartley
1,031 words
29 September 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A19
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The time has come,
the Walrus said,
To talk of all this ken:
Of pounds and euros and dollar bills
Of renminbi and yen.
And how the winds have told me,
Let's all float, amen.
The American political elite knows almost nothing about exchange rates. Worse, much of what it does know
is wrong. This wouldn't be so bad if presidents, congressfolk and Treasury secretaries were content to leave
the issue alone, but recently they've been listening to the whining of a curious coalition of protectionists,
anti-China zealots and beleaguered manufacturers. So herewith a primer, aimed at dispelling myths that
befog the issue.
Myth #1: Floating currencies represent a "free market." Yes, you can go onto a trading floor and call up a
screenfull of quotes, and buy and sell the euro or renminbi. It looks a lot like trading wheat or stocks. But with
currencies, the prices are set not by the market, but by the central-bank cartel. Each central bank has a
monopoly on the currency it issues, and uses this monopoly to dictate the price; its monetary policy
decisions set its foreign exchange rate, whether intentionally or not. Currency is much less of a free market
than even oil, where OPEC's heavy thumb tilts the scale.
Economists specify that a free market requires "freedom of entry." Anyone can start producing wheat or
exploring for oil; when returns on those commodities rise, in time new producers spring up. But because this
cannot happen with currencies, there is no free market and never will be. The relevant question is, what
guideposts do central banks follow in deciding how fast to create dollars, yen, euros or yuan (in which
renminbi, "the people's money," is denominated)?
Myth #2: Floating exchange rates are a policy. Rather, floating represents the absence of a policy. A fixed
rate is a policy. The central bank of, say, China uses the exchange rate as a policy target; if the yuan starts
to rise against the dollar, it creates more of them; if it falls it creates fewer.
To "float," a central bank stops targeting the dollar. So it's without a policy until it finds another target. Under
the urging of the U.S. Treasury, the G-7 called for greater exchange rate "flexibility," meaning that the The
People's Bank of China should no longer target an exchange rate. But since the G-7 did not specify what
target China should follow instead, this is meaningless blather. Lurking behind is an even more preposterous
notion: that China should use its monetary policy to target its bilateral trade deficit with the U.S.
Myth #3: You can build prosperity by devaluing your currency. The notion, currently being pushed by various
U.S. manufacturers, is that a lower dollar will boost exports and curtail imports. In first-order calculations,
devaluation looks like a price cut for exports and a price increase for imports (if that's your idea of a good
thing). But it's been tried hundreds of times over history, and usually devaluation sets off a spiral of economic
decline.
The reason is no mystery, but is well known among economists as the law of one price. Say $1 is worth 8
yuan and each will buy a bottle of wine. Then say the exchange rate changes to $1 for 6 yuan. Suddenly the
wine will sell for $1 and 6 yuan. This arbitrage happens instantly with widely traded goods such as gold, and

--- Page 222 ---
Page 222 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
only over time with untraded goods such as haircuts. But it will defeat attempts to sell more abroad by
devaluing your currency.
This time around, of course, the devaluationists exploit official ignorance with some semantic confusion. They
don't talk of devaluating the dollar, but of "floating" the renminbi, on the not-so-certain assumption it would
float higher and thus produce a lower dollar. And they manage to accuse China of "manipulating" its currency
by targeting the dollar, where of course they are the ones who want to manipulate currencies for presumed
trade advantage.
While currency fluctuations aren't likely to have big effects on trade flows, they are a proven recipe for
financial chaos. Lay aside the Great Depression and all that, in 1987 Treasury Secretary James Baker
knocked 500 points off the Dow Jones averages with a few comments about interest rates and Germany.
With markets teetering, Fed Chairman Greenspan had to rush out a promise to supply liquidity, salvaging the
system.
The baht, what's that? Well, it's the currency of Thailand, and when it was set to "float" in 1997 the crisis
spread throughout Asia. Indeed, around the world, since the following year the Russian default shook the
U.S. capital markets, in particular Long-Term Capital Management. The Fed, which had been thinking about
tightening, instead had to supply liquidity. This set the stage for the 1999-2000 boom; subsequent tightening
and easings gave us today's economy.
All of which is to say that when Treasury Secretary John Snow makes himself point man for the
devaluationists he is an innocent playing with matches. The Bush administration needs a Treasury that would
understand and explain the dangers involved.
Clearly the administration's confused stance toward the dollar is a political calculation, intended as a display
of doing something about the "jobless recovery." But someone should tell Karl Rove that one of the few ways
the president can lose the impending election is by provoking a financial crisis that aborts the budding
recovery before it reaches the job-creation stage.
Ignorance and confusion are all the more worrisome because, to go beyond the first primer, the U.S. dollar is
the world's reserve currency. That is, it's widely held by foreign central banks, and disruption of the dollar
disrupts the entire world economy. Running a reserve currency is a privileged but dangerous role; if the world
loses its confidence in the dollar, we could find ourselves in the mother of all financial crises.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Currency Devaluation: Sometimes It Works" -- WSJ Oct. 17, 2003)

Page 223 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Anadarko's Fuel: Takeover Talk
Heard on the Street
Anadarko's Fuel: Takeover Talk
By Ken Brown, Russell Gold and Robin Sidel
980 words
25 September 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
There's nothing like a takeover rumor to put a little gas in the shares of a struggling company.
That is what has happened to Anadarko Petroleum Corp., whose shares jumped again last week when
rumors resurfaced that the oil exploration and production company might be on the block.
But so far, Anadarko has hit nothing but a dry hole in its summer-long quest for a suitor, approaching most of
the world's major oil companies but getting tepid responses, according to people familiar with the matter.
Even if there is a deal, the premium to Anadarko's share price is likely to be puny.
The market, on the other hand, seems to be predicting a rich deal and has pushed Anadarko ahead of most
other energy stocks since the rumors surfaced in August. Any disappointment could make the shares
vulnerable. Anadarko shares, which jumped 4% Friday, were up five cents to $44.16 each at 4 p.m. yesterday
on a big down day on the New York Stock Exchange.
Anadarko finds itself in a particularly tough spot. As one of the largest pure exploration and production
companies, with $4.6 billion in revenue for the past four quarters, it is too lumbering to move fast or grow
quickly. But it is too small to truly compete against the global behemoths that dominate the oil business.
Anadarko's quandary has fed the deal rumors, which intensified in August after the firm hired Credit Suisse
Group's Credit Suisse First Boston as an adviser. The company has made informal approaches to most of the
major oil companies but hasn't had serious talks with anyone, according to people close to the process.
Anadarko declined to comment on the speculation but said its business is improving and that its high costs, a
major source of criticism, are coming down.
Analysts and investors aren't buying the talk of a turnaround at Anadarko, which burned up a lot of credibility
when it shocked investors by lowering its growth target earlier this year. Instead, they are hoping for a quick
hit from a deal.
The so-far fruitless search for a partner is emblematic of a quick, painful fall from grace for the company,
which made its first major discoveries a half-century ago in the Anadarko Basin of Texas, Oklahoma and
Kansas. Anadarko has grown to become the seventh-largest U.S. energy company by market value, and
expects to produce 192 million barrels of oil equivalent this year.
As recently as this spring, the company, based in the Woodlands, Texas, was considered a growth star,
promising it could increase production 10% a year. But that plan blew apart in June when the company
announced a production shortfall. In July, Anadarko threw out its 2004 growth forecast and laid off 400
employees in an effort to cut $100 million in annual costs. Five top executives left the company soon after.
(The company's chief executive was bounced in March and replaced by former CEO Robert J. Allison Jr.)
"While many exploration and production companies have been guilty of over-promising and under-delivering,
Anadarko had done so to a greater extent than most in the last couple of years," says Shannon Nome, an oil
and gas analyst at J.P. Morgan in Houston. Despite its recent rebound, Anadarko is off nearly 11% from its
June peak and is the worst performer of any major energy company this year.
Even if Anadarko did a deal with a smaller company, a bonanza for shareholders is unlikely. Industry
executives point to the largest energy deal so far this year, when Devon Energy Corp. bought Ocean Energy
Inc. in February, for a paltry 3.6% premium over its share price before the deal.
Another knock on the company: Anadarko's assets, largely North American oil and gas wells, are expensive
to run and are depleted quickly, which makes them unattractive to most buyers. "The types of assets that
Anadarko would overwhelmingly have are assets that are typical of those that the major oil companies have

--- Page 224 ---
Page 224 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
been selling," says Tyler Dann, an analyst who covers major oil companies for Banc of America Securities in
Houston.
Some analysts, though, point out that Anadarko has made some progress in recent months. It has sold
several high-cost, low-margin properties, which will help it lower its finding and developing costs, which
soared 23% last year to $10.52 a barrel. The company expects those costs to fall to between $7.50 and
$8.50 a barrel this year, probably closer to the lower end of the range, which would put it roughly in line with
the industry norm.
The company expects production growth to rebound from two flat years. "We think we have one of the best
portfolios in the business," company spokeswoman Teresa Wong says. If these trends continue, the company
may well be better off on its own.
But even if everything goes right for Anadarko, its stock price could drop or remain stalled, largely because
investors are betting on a takeover. In addition, at its current size, Anadarko would have to find a lot of oil and
gas to boost production enough to make a difference to the stock. "The problem is when you become too big
there's not as many of these good opportunities," says Goldman Sachs analyst Arjun N. Murti in New York.
Ultimately, the market will give up on a takeover and value the company based on its business prospects.
That, analysts say, is why the company's shares could fall. In a report last week, Mr. Murti said Anadarko
runs "the risk of a permanent re-rating down into the domestic oil laggard group."
In analyst-speak, that is a long way of saying "Watch out below."

China's Wealthiest Citizens Seek To Be Seen in Moe Positive Light
China's Wealthiest Citizens Seek To Be Seen in Moe Positive Light
By James T. Areddy Dow Jones Newswires
631 words
24 September 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
B13C
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SHANGHAI -- Being identified as affluent in China has appeared increasingly risky this year as several of the
country's wealthiest people have been arrested, fled the country, disappeared and even died in strange ways.
"Anyone who accumulates wealth becomes a target," said Chauncey Shey, president and chief executive of
Softbank Corp.'s Softbank China Venture Capital.
Zhou Zhengyi, a property tycoon estimated to be worth $320 million was allegedly the perpetrator of a
Shanghai land scandal this year said to have reached into high levels of China's government, while Yang Bin,
a multimillionaire flower merchant and one-time darling of North Korea now finds himself serving 18 years in a
northeastern Chinese jail for fraud and bribery. This month, mysterious circumstances surrounded the death
of an industrial diamond producer Qiao Jinling, said to be worth $120 million.
The stories of each have been splashed across front pages of newspapers in China and fed "a feeling that if
you are a successful businessperson in China, you must be a crook," says Marjorie Yang, chairman and chief
executive of Hong Kong textile maker Esquel Group.
But whatever the circumstances befalling these particular rich individuals, some of the country's other leading
entrepreneurs recently said they refuse to be painted with the same brush. Gathered in Shanghai by Forbes
magazine, which has calculated their riches as it does those of billionaires world-wide, the Chinese
entrepreneurs demanded recognition for their contribution to China's economic development in recent years.
"Wealth is not an evil thing," said Guo Guangchang, president of high-technology operation Fosun Group and
ranked No. 9 in China by Forbes with a wealth of $360 million.
"We should maintain a positive image despite the criticism, and win the trust of the entire society," Mr. Guo
said.
Four of the Forbes 100 richest -- a fifth was delayed by problems with his private plane -- gathered at the
recent conference. They said that they have beaten the odds while providing jobs to tens of thousands of
people, paying their taxes and improving the social fabric of the country.
Describing how "the first bucket of gold was accumulated," Liu Yongxing, chairman of poultry operation New
Hope Group and said to be worth $480 million, said "nothing but courage" allowed him to succeed.
Chinese Internet revolutionary Charles Zhang, chief executive of Sohu.com Inc., one of the largest
Chinese-lanuage Web sites, called for better understanding in China of what entrepreneurs contribute, "or the
rich will feel threatened" and leave the country.
Applauding the entrepreneurial spirit of China at every turn was Steve Forbes, chief of the New York-based
magazine. Forbes had his guests driven around Shanghai in brand-new, cappuccino-colored Series 7 sedans
provided by Bayerische Motoren Werke AG and put some of them up at the swanky Grand Hyatt Shanghai.
The magazine also defended the integrity of the rich list which is becoming known in China as a tip sheet
useful to tax authorities as well as would-be kidnappers. "People like Zhou Zhengyi are not representative in
any way of what private businesspeople are doing for China now or in the future," said Russell Flannery, who
compiles the Forbes China list.
The millionaire panelists said businesspeople create jobs in China and are improving its social fabric, even
though they themselves face hurdles in securing bank financing and doing other things that their counterparts
in other countries take for granted. But as a wealth gap emerges in a country which has more than 650 million
people that live on under $2 a day, according to the World Bank, the businesspeople said the government
has a duty to defend -- rather than target -- entrepreneurs.

U.S. Push for Weaker Dollar Rattles Markets Around Globe
A Global Journal Report
U.S. Push for Weaker Dollar Rattles Markets Around Globe
By Aaron Lucchetti in New York, Greg Ip in Washington and Phillip Day in Singapore
1,624 words
23 September 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Bush administration's push for a weaker dollar against the Japanese and Chinese currencies rattled
global stock and bond markets, threatening collateral damage to the delicate economic expansions under
way in both Japan and the U.S.
The U.S.-orchestrated call by the seven major industrial countries over the weekend for more-flexible
exchange rates sent the dollar to a 33-month low against the Japanese yen and an eight-week low against
the euro yesterday. Implicit in the weekend call was the goal that major currencies would be allowed to rise in
value against the dollar.
Fears that a stronger euro and yen would hamper both Europe's and Japan's export-led expansions sent
stock-market gauges down sharply in both places. While a lower dollar should enable U.S. manufacturers to
compete better both at home and abroad -- a key goal of the Bush administration -- U.S. stocks also fell as
investors worried the lower dollar's benefits would be more than offset by rising interest rates as foreigners
buy fewer Treasury bonds.
Despite the market turmoil, policy makers and economists generally agree that a lower U.S. dollar is essential
to a better-balanced global economy. World growth in the last year has depended disproportionately on U.S.
consumers' willingness to buy imported goods, especially from China and Japan, with money, in effect,
borrowed heavily from foreigners. As a result, the U.S. deficit in trade and investment income is expected to
hit a record 5.1% of economic output this year, according to the International Monetary Fund. A weaker dollar
would make imports more expensive in the U.S. market and U.S. exports cheaper overseas, narrowing that
gap.
"An unbalanced global economy has finally come to its senses," Stephen Roach, Morgan Stanley's
long-pessimistic chief economist, crowed yesterday. "I am now encouraged for the first time in four years."
Goldman Sachs's chief U.S. economist Bill Dudley also welcomed the dollar's decline, provided it happens
gradually, ideally over several years. "A precipitous decline could harm the economy by leading to a sharp
rise in bond yields," he warned.
Although it may yet prove to be a temporary bout of volatility, yesterday's market action suggests investors
are for now worried about a disruptive and precipitous decline.
In their statement released in Dubai Saturday, finance ministers and central bank governors from the
so-called Group of Seven richest countries said: "We emphasize that more flexibility in exchange rates is
desirable." U.S. Treasury Secretary John Snow said the statement wasn't aimed at any country in particular.
But his aides made it clear that it was drafted with Japan and China in mind, both of which regularly sell their
own currencies in exchange for dollars in order to keep their value artificially depressed. Still, the White
House's effort is aimed as much at Midwest voters, alarmed at the loss of millions of manufacturing jobs, as
at it is China and Japan, which have steadfastly ignored such advice for years now.
Indeed, Mr. Snow said yesterday, "There's no change in the strong-dollar policy." Japanese policy makers
also insisted the statement changed nothing. Sadakazu Tanigaki, Japan's new finance minister appointed
Monday, said Japan would stick to its policy of fighting sharp exchange-rate moves. "There will be no change
in our fundamental policy," he said, adding that stable exchange rates are desirable and that Tokyo will act in
the currency markets as the situation demands.
But many investors were betting that even if Japan continued to intervene, it would do so a little less
strenuously, letting the yen continue to creep higher against the dollar. Japanese authorities held off through
much of Monday's trading session before stepping in to buy dollars. "The Bank of Japan will continue to
intervene, but the line in the sand will keep shifting to a stronger yen," said Ian Morris, U.S. economist for

--- Page 228 ---
Page 228 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
HSBC Securities. Some analysts now see 105 yen to the dollar as a level Japanese authorities may choose
as their new target, after abandoning the 115 level on Friday.
There appears less prospect of change from China. Its State Administration of Foreign Exchange said in a
statement on its Web site yesterday that China will "continue to keep the renminbi [or yuan] exchange rate
basically stable." Even Mr. Snow concedes it would be unwise for China to let the yuan float before fixing its
banking system, saddled with bad loans. Still, by accepting a stronger yen Japan has bolstered its own
criticism of China's pegged exchange rate. Japan's previous criticism was undermined by its own efforts to
hold down the yen. China may also find other Asian countries more willing to weigh in on its currency policy,
since they also compete with China for exports.
The dollar, already enduring a lackluster year, suffered one of its worst trading sessions in months. In late
trading yesterday, the dollar was fetching 112.13 yen, down from late Friday's level of 114.30 and 118 earlier
this month. J.P. Morgan said the dollar's drop from noon Friday to noon Monday was its biggest since June of
2002. The dollar also weakened against sterling, the Swiss franc and the euro, which rose to $1.1474 from
$1.1361 Friday.
The dollar's decline led world stocks down: The Nikkei average of leading Japanese stocks sank 463.32, or
4.2%, to 10475.10, its steepest one-day drop in both point and percentage terms since Sept. 17, 2001. Japan
has recently shown signs of emerging from a decadelong economic slump, but should the yen continue
appreciating, it could undermine the export boom that has laid the foundation for that expansion. Meanwhile,
the Dow Jones Industrial Average sank 109.41, or 1.13%.
Ordinarily, U.S. stock investors welcome a lower dollar because it boosts multinationals' foreign currency
profits when translated back into dollars, while enabling U.S.-based producers to gain market share. Most
economists still think that ultimately will be the result of its current drop. But the complexity and scale of
cross-border financial flows today mean any government-induced attempt to steer exchange rates risks
unintended consequences. For example, if the U.S. Administration succeeds in its call on Asian governments
to let the dollar fall, it could lose a major source of buying of U.S. bonds, sending yields higher.
"If China were to protest U.S. foreign policy by selling the dollar for euros or gold, it could set the stage for a
large correction which would drive up U.S. bond yields, weaken the housing market, depress American
domestic consumption and jeopardize the president's re-election," independent economist David Hale says in
remarks he plans to deliver later this week to the congressionally appointed Commission on U.S.-China
Economic Security Review, which studies economic relations between the U.S. and China and their impact
on national security. If Asian central banks abandoned the dollar, bond yields could rise 0.5 to one
percentage point, he earlier predicted.
Yesterday, those worries caused the 10-year Treasury note to fall $5.63 for every $1,000 face value, pushing
the yield up to 4.24% from 4.17% Friday, and the price of gold to jump $5.40 to a seven-month high of
$388.30 per ounce. "It was a radical and immediate shift against U.S. assets," said Joseph Balestrino, a
bond-fund manager with Federated Investors, Pittsburgh. Mr. Balestrino says he may buy more European,
Canadian and Australian bonds because he feels the U.S. current-account deficit is too large relative to the
economy's health and because of the costs of securing Iraq.
The U.S. must sell hundreds of billions of dollars of assets every year to finance its gaping current-account
deficit. Through most of the 1990s and early this decade, that was relatively easy because the U.S. appeared
to offer more-attractive returns than other economies. But in the last year, with foreigners' appetite for U.S.
stocks and other direct investments diminishing, the U.S. has relied to a striking degree on Asian central
banks to finance that deficit. When those banks buy dollars to keep their own currencies down, they usually
invest the proceeds in U.S. Treasury bonds and bills.
In the 12 months through July, foreigners purchased a net $233 billion of U.S. Treasury notes and bonds, and
Japanese and Chinese investors -- primarily their central banks -- accounted for half of that, according to
Treasury Department data. (Data on Treasury bills are unavailable.) That means foreigners' net purchases
accounted for almost the entire $257 billion in notes and bonds the Treasury issued in that period to finance
budget deficits.
Some are skeptical that a reduction in buying by Asian central banks would have much impact, noting they
primarily hold bonds and bills with a maturity of a few years or less, and those yields are being restrained by
the Federal Reserve's easy monetary policy.
But Gerald Lucas, Treasury strategist at Bank of America, said over time 10- and 30-year yields, which
heavily influence mortgage rates, do seem correlated with foreign central bank holdings of U.S. government
paper. The Japanese and Chinese aren't likely to sell, but if they merely buy less, it would tend to raise
long-term rates, he said, adding: "Be careful of what you ask for, because you may just get it."

Snow's Currency Job
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Snow's Currency Job
869 words
23 September 2003
The Wall Street Journal
J
A24
English
Copyright 2003 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
As the U.S. economy accelerates, the main risk to a sustained recovery is clearly international. Growth
remains sluggish in Europe and Japan, free trade took a hit at Cancun, and this weekend U.S. Treasury
Secretary John Snow decided that the time was somehow right to lead the world back to the future of
currency manipulation.
Global financial markets immediately responded with catcalls and hisses, with stocks falling just about
everywhere (especially hard in Japan and Korea), the dollar diving, and bond yields and gold rising.
Yesterday Mr. Snow scrambled to explain that "There has been no change in the strong dollar policy." But
who are investors supposed to believe -- Mr. Snow, or their own eyes?
What the world read over the weekend, coming out of the meeting of G-7 finance ministers in Dubai, was a
communique that included a notable change in exchange-rate policy. The ministers declared that "more
flexibility in exchange rates is desirable for major countries or economic areas to promote smooth and
widespread adjustments in the international financial system, based on market mechanisms." That new
language supplants the usual and useful G-7 boilerplate about the virtues of "exchange-rate stability." What
investors fear is that this means we are headed back to a world of beggar-thy-neighbor competitive
devaluations.
Everybody understands that Mr. Snow's political targets here are Japan and especially China, which is
becoming a U.S. Presidential campaign issue. His successful browbeating of the G-7 came after his failed
attempt earlier this month to prod China to revalue the yuan. The Bush Administration seems to believe that if
it shows it can flog China just as hard as the Democrats do, then somehow the issue will go away. But the
Administration is playing with protectionist matches.
It's always possible that things aren't yet this bad, and that we are merely witnessing the hard currency
education of one more rookie Treasury secretary. Mr. Snow ran a railroad in private life and perhaps he
wasn't paying attention to the global currency crises of the past 70 or so years.
Since arriving at Treasury he has been repeating that currency prices ought to be "flexible" and "set by the
market," as if the dollar was like corn or tin. But currencies aren't just another commodity; they are a medium
of exchange. Exchange rates aren't set on some trading floor but are mainly a function of monetary policy
set by central banks.
If Mr. Snow really wants a cheaper dollar relative to (say) the Japanese yen, the Federal Reserve is going to
have to increase the supply of dollars relative to yen. (Or the Bank of Japan has to do the reverse.) This
implies changes in domestic interest rates and future inflation rates in both countries. Yesterday's dollar
plunge and gold leap suggest that markets foresee a higher U.S. inflation rate if the Snow policy succeeds.
In Japan, meanwhile, the risk is that a rising yen will send that country back into the deflation it only recently
seemed to be escaping.
Regarding China, the argument is that Beijing is manipulating its currency by keeping the yuan fixed at 8.28
to the dollar. But that rate has been fixed since 1994, a period of phenomenal Chinese growth that has
benefited U.S. exporters, among many others. While China has a huge trade surplus with the U.S., it has a
trade deficit with much of the rest of the world. This hardly suggests a country running a competitive
devaluation strategy. In any event, we learned from Japan's revaluation of the yen in the 1980s that currency
rates affect trade flows very little once companies and economies adjust.
Currency manipulation can, however, create the kind of instability that produces genuine financial crises. We
saw this in the late 1990s, with the Asian crisis triggered by IMF advice to Thailand and Indonesia to devalue.
The same trend was also fashionable in the 1970s, with Britain nearly bankrupting itself as it pursued
prosperity through devaluation. And let's not forget the glories of the 1930s.

--- Page 231 ---
Page 231 of 258 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Things got enough out of hand in the mid-1970s that the industrial powers sat down to rewrite the currency
rules of IMF members to discourage the practice. The insert above gives a flavor of that advice, as does
language elsewhere urging members "to assure orderly exchange arrangements and to promote a stable
system of exchange rates." That language, by the way, was drafted by then-Secretary Bill Simon's U.S.
Treasury.
It is especially distressing now, on the cusp of a global recovery, to see Treasury repudiating that historic
good counsel. The U.S. is the conductor of the world monetary system; if it starts to manipulate currencies for
narrow political purposes, all bets are off for the rest of the world. Yesterday's financial Bronx cheer was a
warning that Mr. Snow and the White House would do well to heed.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Return to Bretton Woods" -- WSJ Sept. 24, 2003)
(See related letters: "Letters to the Editor: Anchor Exchange Rates To Something Real" -- WSJ Sept. 29,
2003)
